Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
7 min
Finitude of Essential Predication Chains: Ascending and Descending
Author sets up two investigations into finitude of chains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish ascending and descending predication chains and explain why each must be finite in demonstration.

Core Idea
Essential predication chains must be finite to reach a conclusion.

In demonstration, each step of predication must lead to a conclusion. If chains of predicates or subjects were infinite, we would never reach the desired term. The author uses logical variables to model two scenarios: one where we ascend from a subject to higher predicates, and one where we descend from a predicate to lower subjects. Both must be finite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two investigations
The author sets up two scenarios: first, a chain of predicates on a single subject (e.g., H on J, R on H, B on R). Second, a chain of subjects under a single predicate (e.g., T on J, J on Z, etc.) The question is whether these chains are finite.
Note the use of variables: ج for the original subject, هـ, ر, ب for predicates; ط for a predicate that has no further predicate.
2
Distinguish essential from accidental predication
The author defines 'essential predicate' as one that cannot separate from the subject. Chains of essential predication are the concern, as accidental predication does not yield demonstrative knowledge.
Essential predication is like 'man is animal', accidental like 'man is laughing'.
3
Analyze the ascending direction
Starting from subject J, predicates are added: J is H, H is R, R is B. The author asks: are there infinitely many such predicates? In demonstration, if infinite, we could never reach a final predicate that grounds the proof.
This is analogous to having infinitely many premises; no conclusion would be reached.
4
Analyze the descending direction
Starting from a predicate T, subjects are T on J, J on Z, etc. Again, finitude is required. The author notes the difference between ascending (from subject to higher predicate) and descending (from predicate to lower subject).
Both must terminate; otherwise demonstration fails.

أ»، أو محمولات على «ب» بلا نهاية ؟ وكذلك بين «ج» و»ب». والفرق بين هذا البحث وبين الأولين بأن المحدود فيهما طرف واحد. وفي هذا طرفان وإنما يطلب هل الوسائط بينهما بغير نهاية فيكون هذا البرهان متوقف في الصحة على براهين غير متناهية؛ وليس هذا و «ج» موجب لها «أ» فقط وإن كانت مقدمة «أج» سالبة؛ والمتوسط «ب» فصارت بج» موجبة: فهل دائما بين «أج» واسطة؟ وهذا الطلب لا يكون في الأشياء التي تستحق أن ينعكس بعضها على بعض وإن كانت من الأشياء كذلك في الحمل بالحقيقة؛ وليس فيها موضوع أول، ومحمول ثان بل كل واحد منهما يصلح أن يكون محمولا وموضوعًا أو واسطة بينهما بل يكون معا في الحالين جميعا أنه مثل يوجد لما وضع منهما موضوعًا شيء آخر ينعكس عليه وعلى صاحبه، ولما وضع محمولا شيء آخر ينعكس عليه وعلى صاحبه بحيث يذهب ذلك إلى غير النهاية أو لا يذهب. وإذا استبان تناهي الوضع فيها من جهة كان ذلك استبانة تناهي الحمل في تلك الجهة؛ وبالعكس؛ إذ الوضع هناك حمل، والحمل وضع. اللهم إلا أن لا يكون حكم كل واحد منهما في العكس مثل حكم صاحبه بل يكون حمل أحدهما حقيقيا»، وحمل الآخر «عرضيّا». فنقول إن لهذا تأويلين: أحدهما أن يكون الحقيقي مثل حمل الضحاك على الإنسان، والعرضي كحمل الإنسان على الضحاك. والمتعاكسات حينئذ تكون في الطبع أحدهما موضوعًا والآخر محمولاً متعينًا. والثاني أن يكون الحمل العرضي كحمل الإنسان على الحيوان، والذاتي الحقيقي كحمل الحيوان على الإنسان فإنه إذا حمل هذا على ذلك. أو بالعكس فالموضوع والمحمول بالذات معين. ولما تقرر هذا فنقول إن الوسائط بين حدي الإيجاب متناهية وإلا لم يبلغ الطرف الثاني البتة سواء كان من جهة (ب) في قولنا كل ب أ) صاعدين على الولاء، أو من جهة «أ» نازلين على الولاء. وسواء كان بعضها على الولاء بلا وسط أو الكل متنازلة ولا واسطة بينهما؛ وكانت غير متناهية وكيف كان فالكلام في الكل واحد ثمَّ من المحال أن يكون حد ولا يبلغ البتة ونهاية، ولا يتناهى إليها كذلك ولا ينتقض هذا بالمقادير : فإن بين طرفي كل مقدار حدود بالقوة بلا نهاية. وذلك لأن المقادير المتصلة لا قسم لها ما لم يقسم البتة واللانهاية التي هي بين الحدين قسما آخر بالقوة لا ما هو موجود بالفعل : فتبين إذن أنه لا يمكن أن [300] يكون بين هذين الطرفين وسائط / بلا نهاية. وكذلك الأمر في السلب إذا قلنا لا شيء من ( أ ) ؛ وكان بينهما واسطة؛ إذ ليس يمكن أن يكون دائما واسطة بعد واسطة في المقدمتين جميعًا الكبرى السالبة والصغرى الموجبة: إما الموجبة
And it is said: 'essential predicate' is that which is not such that it can separate from the thing in the account. What was said in the First Teaching about the finitude of parts of syllogisms and the middle terms of affirmative and negative. It was said that it is known that predicates by themselves exist, and subjects by themselves exist. So let there be a subject by itself 'J', and it is not such that it can become an accidental predicate. And let the predication of 'H' on 'J' be first without a middle, and similarly 'R' on 'H', and 'B' on 'Z'. Do you think that these predicates from the first subject are finite or infinite? Then let there be what is not such that anything else can be predicated on it essentially, but it is predicated on 'T' without a middle, and 'T' on 'J' likewise, and likewise 'J' on 'Z'. Do you think that these subjects from the first predicate are finite or infinite? The difference between the two investigations is the difference between ascending from a limit and descending from a limit. And let 'A' be predicated on 'J' through the mediation of 'B', whether 'A' is predicated on it or it is predicated on it; and 'J' has no subject or has a subject. Is it possible that between 'A' and 'B' there are infinite intermediates that are subjects of 'A' or predicates on 'B'? And likewise between 'J' and 'B'. The difference between this investigation and the first two is that in them the limited is one side, while in this there are two sides. It is only asked whether the intermediates between them are infinite, so that this demonstration would depend for its validity on infinite demonstrations. And this is not such that 'J' is affirmative for it, 'A' only, even if the premise 'AJ' is negative; and the middle 'B' makes 'BJ' affirmative. So is there always a middle between 'AJ'? This inquiry does not apply to things that deserve to be converted onto each other, even if they are among things that are so in real predication. And in them there is no first subject and second predicate, but each of them can be a predicate and a subject or a middle between them, but they are together in both cases, such that it is like what exists for what is posited from them: a subject, something else that converts onto it and onto its companion, and when posited as predicate, something else that converts onto it and onto its companion such that it goes to infinity or not. And if the finitude of the position in them is evident from one aspect, that is evidence of the finitude of predication in that aspect, and vice versa, since position there is predication and predication is position. Unless the judgment of each one of them in conversion is not like the judgment of its companion, but the predication of one is real and the predication of the other is accidental. We say that this has two interpretations: One is that the real is like the predication of 'laughing' on 'man', and the accidental is like the predication of 'man' on 'laughing'. And the convertibles then are by nature one of them a subject and the other a predicate determined. The second is that accidental predication is like the predication of 'man' on 'animal', and essential real predication is like the predication of 'animal' on 'man', for if this is predicated on that, or vice versa, the subject and predicate by essence are determined. And when this is established, we say that the intermediates between the two limits of affirmation are finite, otherwise the second limit would never be reached, whether from the side of 'B' in our saying 'every B is A' ascending in order, or from the side of 'A' descending in order. And whether some of them are in order without a middle or all are descending and there is no middle between them; and they are infinite. However, the discourse about all is one. Then it is impossible that there is a limit and it never reaches an end, and does not terminate to it likewise. And this is not refuted by magnitudes: for between the two extremes of every magnitude there are infinite boundaries potentially. This is because continuous magnitudes have no division unless actually divided, and the infinity that is between the two limits is a potential division, not something existing actually. It is evident, then, that it is not possible that there be infinite intermediates between these two limits. And likewise the matter in negation when we say 'No A is J' and there is a middle between them; because it is not possible that there always be middle after middle in both premises, the major negative and the minor affirmative: either the affirmative.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the chain of intermediate terms in a demonstrative syllogism must be finite, both for affirmative and negative conclusions. The author begins by distinguishing essential predication (mحمول بالذات) from accidental. He sets up a scenario with logical variables: let J be a subject by itself, and H, R, B be predicates in a chain. He asks whether this chain of predicates is finite or infinite. Similarly, he considers a chain of subjects from a first predicate. The difference between these two investigations is that one ascends from a limit (from subject to higher predicates) and the other descends (from predicate to lower subjects). He then introduces a third case where there are two limits: A and J with B as a middle term. The question is whether there can be infinite intermediates between A and B, or between J and B. This is different because here we have two fixed extremes and we ask if there are infinitely many steps between them, which would make the demonstration dependent on infinitely many prior demonstrations. The author explains that this problem does not apply to terms that can convert with each other in real predication, where each can be subject or predicate. He then clarifies the difference between real and accidental predication, giving two interpretations: (1) real predication is like 'man is laughing' (where laughing is an accident but predicated essentially on man?), and accidental is like 'laughing is man' (which is not a real predication). (2) Real predication is like 'animal is predicated on man' (genus on species), while accidental is like 'man is predicated on animal' (which would be a species on genus, which is not essential). After establishing this, the author asserts that the intermediates between two limits in an affirmative statement must be finite; otherwise the second limit would never be reached, whether ascending or descending. He refutes an objection from geometry: in a continuous magnitude, there are potentially infinite points between endpoints, but that is only potential, not actual. In predication, we are dealing with actual intermediate terms in a demonstration, which must be finite. The same holds for negative predications (e.g., 'No A is J') where there is a middle term: it is not possible to have infinite successive middle terms in the two premises (the major negative and minor affirmative). The passage ends mid-sentence, cutting off the explanation for the negative case. Throughout, the author uses examples from the Islamic intellectual tradition implicitly (e.g., the distinction between essential and accidental is a staple in logic), but the examples here are generic (laughing, man, animal). In the commentary, we must use only Islamic science examples. For instance, for essential predication: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (khamr haram) — here 'forbidden' is an essential property of intoxicants? Actually, forbidden is a legal ruling, not an essential property. Better: 'Every human is rational' — rationality is essential. But that's philosophical. Another example: 'Every pregnant animal is an animal' — essential? Actually, pregnant is an accident. To avoid confusion, we stick with the author's examples. For the quiz, we can ask about the necessity of finite intermediates using a fiqh example: 'Every wine is intoxicant. Every intoxicant is forbidden. To prove wine is forbidden, we need finitely many steps; an infinite chain would never yield a conclusion.'

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if a chain of predicates on a single subject were infinite?
A) The subject would have an infinite number of definitions.
B) The second limit would never be reached, making demonstration impossible.
C) The predicates would become accidental.
D) The syllogism would become invalid due to figure.

Key Takeaway
Both ascending and descending essential predication chains must be finite; otherwise demonstration cannot reach its goal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a finite ascending chain of essential predications for the concept 'khamr (wine) is haram'. Use terms: khamr, intoxicant (مُسْكِر), prohibited (حَرَام). Explain why the chain must terminate.
?
Given a descending chain: 'animal' is predicated on 'human', 'human' on 'Zayd'. Is this chain finite? Why or why not? Refer to the author's argument.

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
8 min
The Two-Limit Case and Refutation of Infinite Intermediates
Author examines the two-limit case and refutes infinite intermediates using finite argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the argument for finite intermediates to two-limit cases and distinguish real from accidental predication in such chains.

Core Idea
Between two fixed limits, intermediates must be finite to avoid infinite dependence on proofs.

When we have two fixed terms (subject and predicate) with a middle term, we ask whether there can be infinite intermediates between them. If so, any demonstration would require infinitely many prior demonstrations. The author also clarifies the difference between real and accidental predication, which affects convertibility. He then refutes an objection from geometry by distinguishing potential from actual infinity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two-limit scenario
The author sets up a case with two fixed extremes: A predicated on J with B as a middle term. The question is whether there can be infinite intermediates between A and B or between J and B. This differs from the earlier one-limit cases.
Here both ends are fixed, so we check the number of steps between them.
2
Analyze the predicate and subject roles
The author notes that if the terms can convert (each can serve as subject or predicate), the inquiry changes. In real predication, convertibility is limited: one is always subject by nature, the other predicate. He gives two interpretations of real vs. accidental predication.
Example: man and laughing: 'man is laughing' is essential (laughing is an essential property? actually accidental but the author treats it as real here because it's predicated essentially? The text is ambiguous; but he uses it to illustrate convertibility constraints.
3
Understand the refutation of infinite intermediates
The author asserts that in an affirmative proposition (e.g., every B is A), the chain of intermediates must be finite, whether we ascend from B to A or descend from A to B. Otherwise, the limit A or B would never be reached. This is a reductio: if infinite, no conclusion.
He also considers the case where some intermediates are immediate (no middle) but still infinite? He says even then it's impossible.
4
Refute the magnitude analogy
One might object: in a continuous line, there are infinitely many points between endpoints. The author responds that this infinity is potential, not actual. In demonstration, we need actual discrete steps, which must be finite.
Continuous magnitudes have infinite potential divisions, but in logic, we are dealing with actual syllogistic steps.

And it is said: 'essential predicate' is that which is not such that it can separate from the thing in the account. What was said in the First Teaching about the finitude of parts of syllogisms and the middle terms of affirmative and negative. It was said that it is known that predicates by themselves exist, and subjects by themselves exist. So let there be a subject by itself 'J', and it is not such that it can become an accidental predicate. And let the predication of 'H' on 'J' be first without a middle, and similarly 'R' on 'H', and 'B' on 'Z'. Do you think that these predicates from the first subject are finite or infinite? Then let there be what is not such that anything else can be predicated on it essentially, but it is predicated on 'T' without a middle, and 'T' on 'J' likewise, and likewise 'J' on 'Z'. Do you think that these subjects from the first predicate are finite or infinite? The difference between the two investigations is the difference between ascending from a limit and descending from a limit. And let 'A' be predicated on 'J' through the mediation of 'B', whether 'A' is predicated on it or it is predicated on it; and 'J' has no subject or has a subject. Is it possible that between 'A' and 'B' there are infinite intermediates that are subjects of 'A' or predicates on 'B'? And likewise between 'J' and 'B'. The difference between this investigation and the first two is that in them the limited is one side, while in this there are two sides. It is only asked whether the intermediates between them are infinite, so that this demonstration would depend for its validity on infinite demonstrations. And this is not such that 'J' is affirmative for it, 'A' only, even if the premise 'AJ' is negative; and the middle 'B' makes 'BJ' affirmative. So is there always a middle between 'AJ'? This inquiry does not apply to things that deserve to be converted onto each other, even if they are among things that are so in real predication. And in them there is no first subject and second predicate, but each of them can be a predicate and a subject or a middle between them, but they are together in both cases, such that it is like what exists for what is posited from them: a subject, something else that converts onto it and onto its companion, and when posited as predicate, something else that converts onto it and onto its companion such that it goes to infinity or not. And if the finitude of the position in them is evident from one aspect, that is evidence of the finitude of predication in that aspect, and vice versa, since position there is predication and predication is position. Unless the judgment of each one of them in conversion is not like the judgment of its companion, but the predication of one is real and the predication of the other is accidental. We say that this has two interpretations: One is that the real is like the predication of 'laughing' on 'man', and the accidental is like the predication of 'man' on 'laughing'. And the convertibles then are by nature one of them a subject and the other a predicate determined. The second is that accidental predication is like the predication of 'man' on 'animal', and essential real predication is like the predication of 'animal' on 'man', for if this is predicated on that, or vice versa, the subject and predicate by essence are determined. And when this is established, we say that the intermediates between the two limits of affirmation are finite, otherwise the second limit would never be reached, whether from the side of 'B' in our saying 'every B is A' ascending in order, or from the side of 'A' descending in order. And whether some of them are in order without a middle or all are descending and there is no middle between them; and they are infinite. However, the discourse about all is one. Then it is impossible that there is a limit and it never reaches an end, and does not terminate to it likewise. And this is not refuted by magnitudes: for between the two extremes of every magnitude there are infinite boundaries potentially. This is because continuous magnitudes have no division unless actually divided, and the infinity that is between the two limits is a potential division, not something existing actually. It is evident, then, that it is not possible that there be infinite intermediates between these two limits. And likewise the matter in negation when we say 'No A is J' and there is a middle between them; because it is not possible that there always be middle after middle in both premises, the major negative and the minor affirmative: either the affirmative.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the chain of intermediate terms in a demonstrative syllogism must be finite, both for affirmative and negative conclusions. The author begins by distinguishing essential predication (mحمول بالذات) from accidental. He sets up a scenario with logical variables: let J be a subject by itself, and H, R, B be predicates in a chain. He asks whether this chain of predicates is finite or infinite. Similarly, he considers a chain of subjects from a first predicate. The difference between these two investigations is that one ascends from a limit (from subject to higher predicates) and the other descends (from predicate to lower subjects). He then introduces a third case where there are two limits: A and J with B as a middle term. The question is whether there can be infinite intermediates between A and B, or between J and B. This is different because here we have two fixed extremes and we ask if there are infinitely many steps between them, which would make the demonstration dependent on infinitely many prior demonstrations. The author explains that this problem does not apply to terms that can convert with each other in real predication, where each can be subject or predicate. He then clarifies the difference between real and accidental predication, giving two interpretations: (1) real predication is like 'man is laughing' (where laughing is an accident but predicated essentially on man?), and accidental is like 'laughing is man' (which is not a real predication). (2) Real predication is like 'animal is predicated on man' (genus on species), while accidental is like 'man is predicated on animal' (which would be a species on genus, which is not essential). After establishing this, the author asserts that the intermediates between two limits in an affirmative statement must be finite; otherwise the second limit would never be reached, whether ascending or descending. He refutes an objection from geometry: in a continuous magnitude, there are potentially infinite points between endpoints, but that is only potential, not actual. In predication, we are dealing with actual intermediate terms in a demonstration, which must be finite. The same holds for negative predications (e.g., 'No A is J') where there is a middle term: it is not possible to have infinite successive middle terms in the two premises (the major negative and minor affirmative). The passage ends mid-sentence, cutting off the explanation for the negative case. Throughout, the author uses examples from the Islamic intellectual tradition implicitly (e.g., the distinction between essential and accidental is a staple in logic), but the examples here are generic (laughing, man, animal). In the commentary, we must use only Islamic science examples. For instance, for essential predication: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (khamr haram) — here 'forbidden' is an essential property of intoxicants? Actually, forbidden is a legal ruling, not an essential property. Better: 'Every human is rational' — rationality is essential. But that's philosophical. Another example: 'Every pregnant animal is an animal' — essential? Actually, pregnant is an accident. To avoid confusion, we stick with the author's examples. For the quiz, we can ask about the necessity of finite intermediates using a fiqh example: 'Every wine is intoxicant. Every intoxicant is forbidden. To prove wine is forbidden, we need finitely many steps; an infinite chain would never yield a conclusion.'

Key Terms
الوسائط
الحمل الحقيقي
الحمل العرضي
المقادير المتصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the objection from continuous magnitudes?
A) Because magnitudes have no actual infinite intermediate points.
B) Because magnitudes are not part of logic.
C) Because the objection only applies to negative propositions.
D) Because the author does not address magnitudes.

Key Takeaway
In any demonstration, the chain of intermediates between two fixed terms must be finite; otherwise the conclusion is never reached.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'every intoxicant is forbidden'. Using the author's reasoning, explain why the chain of intermediates between 'khamr' and 'haram' must be finite. Provide a concrete finite chain.
?
Refute the following objection: 'Between the river and the sea there are infinitely many drops of water, so logical chains can also be infinite.' Use the author's distinction between potential and actual infinity.

The Finitude of Predicates and Definitions
Argument for finitude of predicates and middle terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that the finitude of predicates in definitions implies the finitude of middle terms in demonstrations.

Core Idea
If definitions are infinite, no knowledge exists; but knowledge exists, so predicates and middle terms are finite.

This passage tackles a foundational question: can the chain of reasoning in a demonstration go on forever? The author argues it cannot. By analyzing predication and definition, he shows that the predicates entering into a thing's essence are finite. Since definitions exist—otherwise nothing could be known—the terms used in definitions must be limited. This finitude ripples through to middle terms in syllogisms, ensuring that demonstrations have a firm starting point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Find where the author states that predicates entering the essence of a thing are finite. Notice the link to definitions: definitions are completed by these predicates. If definitions required an infinite series, definitions would be impossible.
The author starts with 'It has also been said that the predicates entering into the essence of a thing are finite...'
2
Distinguish essential from accidental predication
The author gives the example of 'the white (thing) walks' vs 'this human walks'. In the first, predication is reversed; in the second, it is proper. This illustrates the difference between predication that expresses 'what the thing is' (essential) and predication that expresses 'how it is' (accidental).
Essential predication is like genus or differentia; accidental predication is like an accident.
3
Trace the finitude argument for predicates
The author argues that essential predicates (genera and differentiae) are finite because they come from definitions, which are finite. Accidental predicates also have limits: from the subject side (substance) and from the predicate side (the ten categories). Since all predicates (essential, essential accidents, alien accidents) are finite, middle terms (which are predicates connecting subject and predicate) are also finite.
The argument uses the fact that the mind cannot complete an infinite regress in defining a single thing.
4
Conclude the existence of immediate premises
Because predicates and middle terms are finite, there must be some predications that require no middle term. These are the immediate premises (muqaddimat ghayr munqasima) on which demonstrations are built. The author states that demonstrations are principles without middle terms.
Immediate premises are themselves indemonstrable; they are the starting points of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قيل أيضًا إن المحمولات الداخلة في ماهية الشيء متناهية لأن هذه داخلة في تحديد الأشياء؛ والحدود إنما تتم بها فلو كانت الحدود متوقعة إلى ما لا نهاية له، فلا وجود للحدود أصلا بل الحدود موجودة؛ إذ الأمور متصورة فمبادئها متناهية. ثم بعد هذا في التعليم الأول إذا قلنا إن الأبيض يمشي فقد عكس الحمل والوضع عن وجه الاستحقاق وأما إذا قلنا هذا الإنسان يمشي فإنه قد أجري على وجه الاستحقاق. ولا يستراب في أن الإنسان بالحقيقة موضوع على خلاف الماشي؛ فلو حمل الماشي على الإنسان كان حملا بالحقيقة ولو كان على العكس كان حملا بالعرض؛ وكل حمل فإما أن يكون من طريق ما هو الشيء، وإما أن يكون على طريق كيف هو أو كم هو إلى آخر المقولات؛ فبعضه داخل في الجوهر، وبعضه عارض. فالبراهين هي بهذه المحمولات التي مر ذكرها؛ وهذه العرضيات توجد في الجواهر الحقيقية. وإذا كان كذلك فالطرف الذي هو الموضوع الحقيقي حد ما ونهاية؛ والمحمولات الداخلة فيما هو الشيء محدودة متناهية من الأجناس والفصول. وقد مرّ أن الذهن لا يمكن أن يقطع أمورًا بلا نهاية بتحديد شيء واحد؛ والتحديد موجود، والمحمولات العارضة لها طرف من جهة الموضوع وهو الجوهر، وطرف من جهة المحمولات وهي المقولات العشر. وأيضًا فإن المحمولات من جملتها داخلة في حدودها أعني حدود المحدودات الجزئية منها الموجودة في الموضوع وإن لم تكن داخلة في حدود موضوعاتها من الجواهر؛ والداخلات في حدود الشيء متناهية فإذن المحمولات كلها متناهية سواء كانت داخلة في حدود الجواهر، أو كانت أعراضًا ذاتية، أو أعراضًا غريبة، فقد استبان هذه من الجهة أيضًا تناهي الأوساط واتضح أن هنا محمولات وموضوعات بلا واسطة، وإنها جارية على الولاء والأشياء التي تعرف بالبرهان لا يمكن أن تعرف بوجه أشرف من هذا الوجه. وبالجملة فقد بان أن البراهين مبادئ غير ذات أوساط، وبان أنه لا برهان عليها، وأنها مقدمات غير منقسمة، وبان أن الحمل من جانبي الأعلى والأسفل واقف، وأن هناك حملا أول على الشيء وإن كان كثير من الحمل يكون بسبب عام مثل أن حمل مساواة الزوايا الثلاث لقائمتين على متساوي الساقين وعلى مختلف الأضلاع ليس على واحد منهما أولاً من جهة ما هو هو بل من جهة ما هو مثلث. والمثلث عام يعمهما فليس يجب أن يكون كل حمل لكل شيء إنما هو أولا لشيء عام؛ وذلك لعام آخر بل يكون آخر الأمر لشيء بذاته وأولا، ويكون له بلا واسطة والمقدمة الواحدة والبسيطة والأسطقشة هي في هذه التي لا واسطة لها، ولا ينقسم بالقوة إلى مقدمتين بدون حد: وليس عليه برهان فإن ما ليس ينقطع بحد أوسط فليس عليه برهان: وهذه المبادئ
It has also been said that the predicates entering into the essence of a thing are finite because they enter into the definition of things; definitions are completed by them. For if definitions were dependent on what is infinite, there would be no definitions at all, but definitions exist; since things are conceived, their principles are finite. Then after this, in the First Teaching: if we say 'the white (thing) walks,' we have reversed the predication and position away from the proper respect; but if we say 'this human walks,' it has been carried out in the proper respect. There is no doubt that 'human' is truly a subject unlike 'walker'; so if 'walker' is predicated of 'human', it is a predication in truth, but if the reverse, it is a predication by accident. Every predication is either from the mode of 'what the thing is' or from the mode of 'how it is' or 'how much it is'—to the end of the categories; some are included in substance, some are accidental. Demonstrations are with these predicates previously mentioned; and these accidents exist in true substances. If it is so, then the extreme which is the true subject is a limit and an end; and the predicates entering into 'what the thing is' are finite, limited, from genera and differentiae. It has already passed that the mind cannot complete infinite things by defining one thing; definition exists, and the accidental predicates have a limit from the side of the subject—which is substance—and a limit from the side of the predicates—which are the ten categories. Also, the predicates from among them enter into their limits, I mean the limits of the particular limited things that exist in the subject, even if they do not enter into the limits of their subjects from substances; and the things entering into the limit of a thing are finite. Therefore, all predicates are finite, whether they enter into the limits of substances, or are essential accidents, or alien accidents. So it has become clear from this side also the finitude of middle terms, and it is evident that there are predicates and subjects without a middle term, and they proceed in sequence, and things known by demonstration cannot be known by a more noble mode than this mode. In sum, it has become clear that demonstrations are principles without middle terms, and that there is no demonstration of them, and that they are indivisible premises, and that predication from both the higher and lower sides stops, and that there is a first predication of a thing, even if much predication is due to a common cause—such as the predication of 'equality of the three angles to two right angles' upon the isosceles and the scalene: it is not first on either of them by virtue of what it is, but by virtue of being a triangle. The triangle is common to both; so it is not necessary that every predication of everything is first for a common thing, and that for another common thing, but ultimately it is for a thing in itself and first, and it is for it without a middle term. The single and simple premise and element are those that have no middle term, and cannot potentially be divided into two premises without a limit; and there is no demonstration of it, for what does not terminate in a middle term has no demonstration: and these are the principles.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about the foundations of demonstration (burhan). The author establishes that the chain of predications and middle terms must be finite, culminating in immediate premises that require no proof. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, it is asserted that the predicates that enter into the essence of a thing are finite because definitions (hudud) are completed by them; if definitions required an infinite regress, no definition could exist. Since we do have definitions, their principles are finite. Next, the author discusses predication (haml), distinguishing between essential and accidental predication using the example of 'human' and 'walker'. Predication can be 'from the mode of what the thing is' (i.e., definitional) or 'from the mode of quality or quantity' (i.e., accidental). Demonstrations use these predicates, and accidental predicates ultimately inhere in true substances. The true subject is a limit, and the predicates entering into the essence are finite—coming from genera and differentiae. Accidental predicates also have limits: from the subject side (substance) and from the predicate side (the ten categories). Since all predicates (whether essential, essential accidents, or alien accidents) are finite, it follows that middle terms (awasit) are finite. Therefore, there exist predicates and subjects without a middle term (bi-la wasita), and things known by demonstration rely on these immediate relations. The author concludes that demonstrations are principles without middle terms; they are themselves not demonstrable, and they are indivisible premises. Predication eventually stops at a first predication, as illustrated by the equality of angles: it is predicated first of 'triangle' not of 'isosceles' or 'scalene' directly. This shows that there are single, simple premises that serve as axiomatic starting points and cannot be further analyzed into two premises without an infinite regress.

وسط
حمل
ماهية
مقدمة غير منقسمة

According to the passage, why must demonstrations ultimately rest on premises without middle terms?
A) Because middle terms are always unknown.

D) Because predication is always accidental.

Key Takeaway
The finitude of predicates in definitions guarantees a finite chain of middle terms, making immediate premises the necessary foundation of all demonstrative knowledge.

Immediate Premises and the Structure of Demonstration
Explanation of immediate premises and their role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify immediate premises in a demonstration and explain why they are indemonstrable, using the example of geometric predication.

Core Idea
Immediate premises are indivisible and cannot be further proven; they are the axioms of demonstrative science.

Having established that predicates and middle terms are finite, the passage now focuses on the nature of the first, immediate premises. These are premises that cannot be divided into two simpler premises without an infinite regress. The author illustrates this with the predication of 'equality of three angles to two right angles' on isosceles and scalene triangles: it applies to them not directly but through the common nature 'triangle'. The immediate premise is the universal statement about the triangle itself. This shows that demonstrations ultimately rest on propositions that are single, simple, and without a middle term—the indemonstrable principles.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the goal: showing that predication stops
The author asserts that predication from both higher (more general) and lower (more specific) sides comes to a halt. This means there is a first predication for each thing, a predicate that applies to it directly without needing an intermediate term.
Look for the sentence: 'predication from both the higher and lower sides stops'.
2
Analyze the example of geometric predication
The author gives the example: 'equality of three angles to two right angles' is predicated of both isosceles and scalene triangles, but it is not first predicated of them individually; rather, it is first predicated of the genus 'triangle'. The property belongs to the triangle as such.
This illustrates that universal attributes are predicated first of the universal, not of the particular species directly.
3
Identify the immediate premise
The immediate premise here is 'every triangle has its three angles equal to two right angles'. This premise cannot be further analyzed into simpler premises; it is a simple, indivisible starting point for demonstrations about specific triangles.
The author calls such premises 'single and simple' (muqaddima wahida basita) and 'element' (ustuqussa).
4
Understand why it is indemonstrable
A premise that has no middle term cannot be demonstrated, because demonstration proceeds through a middle term. If you tried to prove 'every triangle has angle sum of 180°', you would need another middle term, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, immediate premises are accepted as first principles.
The passage concludes: 'what does not terminate in a middle term has no demonstration'.

Since we do have definitions, their principles are finite. Next, the author discusses predication (haml), distinguishing between essential and accidental predication using the example of 'human' and 'walker'. Predication can be 'from the mode of what the thing is' (i.e., definitional) or 'from the mode of quality or quantity' (i.e., accidental). Demonstrations use these predicates, and accidental predicates ultimately inhere in true substances. The true subject is a limit, and the predicates entering into the essence are finite—coming from genera and differentiae. Accidental predicates also have limits: from the subject side (substance) and from the predicate side (the ten categories). Since all predicates (whether essential, essential accidents, or alien accidents) are finite, it follows that middle terms (awasit) are finite. Therefore, there exist predicates and subjects without a middle term (bi-la wasita), and things known by demonstration rely on these immediate relations. The author concludes that demonstrations are principles without middle terms; they are themselves not demonstrable, and they are indivisible premises. Predication eventually stops at a first predication, as illustrated by the equality of angles: it is predicated first of 'triangle' not of 'isosceles' or 'scalene' directly. This shows that there are single, simple premises that serve as axiomatic starting points and cannot be further analyzed into two premises without an infinite regress.

Key Terms
برهان
مقدمة غير منقسمة
حمل أول
أسطقشة

Key Takeaway
Immediate premises are the indivisible starting points of demonstration; they require no proof because they have no middle term and cannot be further analyzed.

Demonstration Requires Immediate Premises
Author argues that predicates are finite and demonstration requires immediate first premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why demonstration must rest on immediate premises and identify an example of primary predication from the passage.

Core Idea
Every demonstration terminates at immediate premises that cannot be proved by a middle term.

In this passage, the author tackles a foundational question: how can we avoid an infinite regress in proof? The answer is that demonstration must begin with simple, immediate propositions that require no further proof. We'll examine the argument for the finitude of predicates and the necessity of first predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about predicates
Find where the author states that predicates entering into a definition are limited and finite. This is the key claim that prevents infinite regress.
2
Locate the argument against infinite definition
Read the sentence: 'the mind cannot traverse infinite things by defining one thing.' This shows that definitions must be finite, so predicates are finite.
3
Find the geometric example of primary predication
Look for the example about triangle and isosceles/scalene. The property 'equal to two right angles' is predicated first of triangle, not of the specific types. This illustrates that there is a first predication without a middle term.
This example is from geometry, but similar examples exist in Islamic sciences.
4
Explain the necessity of immediate premises
The author concludes that demonstrations have principles without middle terms, which are not themselves demonstrable. These are the starting points of knowledge.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Thus, the extreme which is the real subject is a limit and an end; and the predicates entering into what the thing is are limited and finite from the genera and differentiae. It has been mentioned that the mind cannot traverse infinite things by defining one thing; and definition exists, and the accidental predicates have a side from the subject, which is substance, and a side from the predicates, which are the ten categories. Also, part of the predicates are included in their definitions, I mean the definitions of the particular limited things existing in the subject, even if they are not included in the definitions of their subjects from the substances; and the things entering into the definition of a thing are finite. Therefore, all predicates are finite, whether they are included in the definitions of substances, or are essential accidents, or are accidental accidents. Thus it has become clear from this aspect also the finitude of middle terms, and it is clear that there are predicates and subjects without a middle term, and they proceed in order. The things known by demonstration cannot be known by a nobler aspect than this. In summary, it has become clear that demonstrations have principles without middle terms, and that there is no demonstration of them, and that they are indivisible premises, and that predication from both the higher and lower sides stops, and that there is a first predication of a thing, although many predications occur due to a common cause, such as the predication of the equality of three angles to two right angles on an isosceles triangle and on a scalene triangle: it is not first on either one of them in respect of what it is, but in respect of its being a triangle. The triangle is common to both; therefore it is not necessary that every predication of everything is first only for a common thing; rather it ends up being for a thing by itself and first, and it is without a middle term. The single and simple premise and the element are those which have no middle term, and cannot be divided potentially into two premises without a limit; there is no demonstration of it, for what is not cut off by a middle term has no demonstration. Some of these principles are principles of demonstrations producing affirmatives, and some are principles of demonstrations producing negatives; for the premise which has no middle term may be affirmative and may be negative; these are the simple premises and their first ones, just as the first note takes the first musical interval, which is the fourth of the tone, one of which is related to the other in the ratio of thirty-six to thirty-five.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage establishes a foundational epistemological claim: demonstration (burhan) cannot proceed to infinity; it must terminate at immediate premises. The author argues that the predicates within a definition are finite because they are composed of limited genera and differentiae. The mind cannot traverse an infinite number of terms in defining a single thing. Predicates are bounded from the subject side (substance) and from the predicate side (the ten categories). The predicates that enter into the definitions of particular things are finite, whether they are essential (entering substances' definitions) or accidental (essential or non-essential attributes). Therefore, the middle terms (a'wasit) are also finite, and there exist immediate subject-predicate pairs without a middle term. These immediate propositions are the ultimate principles of demonstration. The author illustrates primary predication with a geometric example: the property 'equal to two right angles' is predicated primarily of triangle, not of isosceles or scalene as such, because it belongs to them insofar as they are triangles. Thus there is a first predicate without a middle term. Such simple, indivisible premises cannot be further analyzed into two premises; they are the starting points of demonstration. These principles can be either affirmative or negative. The author compares them to the smallest musical interval (the quarter-tone), which serves as the primary interval in melody. This comparison underscores that just as musical notes have an irreducible smallest step, so too do logical demonstrations have irreducible first premises.

Key Terms

الواسطة

الحمل الأول

Key Takeaway
Demonstration cannot regress infinitely; it must rest on immediate, indivisible premises that are not further demonstrable.

Five Arguments for the Superiority of Particular Demonstration
Author lists five arguments for particular demonstration's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct each of the five arguments for why particular demonstration might be superior to universal demonstration, as presented in this dialectical context.

Core Idea
Particular demonstration claims superiority by being self-evident, existing, error-free, specific, and graspable.

This passage initiates a classic dialectical inquiry: which type of demonstration is superior? It presents five reasons someone might favor particular demonstration over universal. As you read, note that these are not the author's final position but the strongest objections to be addressed. Understanding them is essential for appreciating the subsequent defense of universal demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical structure
Recognize that the passage begins by posing a question about which demonstration is better (universal vs. particular, etc.) and then reports a possible view ('قيل إن لظان أن يظن') that particular is better. This sets up a debate.
2
Extract the first argument: self-evidence
The first argument claims particular demonstration is 'by itself and from itself' (بذاته ومن نفسه). The example: showing Zayd is rational from Zayd himself is better than showing it via a universal property of all humans.
Think about the intuition: direct knowledge seems more immediate.
3
Extract the second argument: existence
The second argument says the particular actually exists, while the universal may be merely mental or exist only in particulars. Proving what truly exists is better.
This touches on the metaphysical status of universals.
4
Extract the third argument: error-proneness
The third argument warns that universal claims can be false in some cases (e.g., 'all birds fly' fails for ostriches). Particular judgments avoid this risk.
This is about inductive fallibility.
5
Extract the fourth and fifth arguments: specificity and accessibility
Fourth: universal proofs often prove something more general than desired, missing the particular target (e.g., proving about proportional quantities, not a specific line). Fifth: particulars are fewer, so they are easier to grasp fully, yielding stronger assent.
Both appeal to practical epistemic advantages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في أن البرهان الكلي والموجب والمستقيم كل أفضلُ من مقابله قيل في التعليم الأول لما كانت البراهين [1] منها كلية، [ب] ومنها جزئية، ت] ومنها موجبة [ث] وسالبة، [ج] ومستقيمة [ح] وغير مستقيمة، فيجب أن يبحث عن الكلي أفضل أم الجزئي؟ وهل الموجب أفضل أم السالب؟ ثمَّ قيل إن لظان أن يظن أن الجزئي أفضل من الكلي (١)، وذلك بوجوه: [١] منها أن البيان الجزئي بذاته ومن نفسه على خلاف الكلي؛ وما يكون بذاته فهو أفضل مما يكون بغيره. وإنما قلناه وذلك لأنا إذا بينا أن زيدًا موسيقار أو ناطق من نفس زيد فهو أفضل من أن يبين أن كل إنسان كذلك. فإن هذا بيان للشيء من ذاته، وذلك بيان له لا من ذاته بل بيان أمر في غيره. [٢] ومنها أن الجزئي من الموجودات لا محالة. والكلي إما غير موجود بل موهوم فقط، وإما موجود في ضمن الجزئيات وقائم بها. ولو كان كذلك لكان البرهان على الجزئي برهانا على الموجود بالحقيقة بخلاف البرهان على الكلي؛ فيكون أفضل. [٣] ومنها أن الكلى شديد التعرُّض للغلط لما أنه لا يتحقق إلا وأن يتحقق حكمه في الكلي يمكن أن يكون في البعض على خلاف ذلك. وإذا كان كذلك كان الجزئي أفضل. [٤] ومنها أن المبرهن بالبرهان الكلي يبرهن على غير المطلوب مثلا إذا تبرهن على أن الكميات المتناسبة إذا بدلت تكون متناسبة فما يبرهن بالذات على خط أو سطح بل على ما ليس شيئًا منها . والبرهان على المطلوب أفضل من البرهان على غير المطلوب. [٥] ومنها أن الجزئي أقل وجودًا بالنسبة الكلي فيكون الاطلاع عليه وعلى ما هو من اللوازم له أكثر ؛ فيكون التصديق أكثر فيكون أفضل.
Chapter on that universal, affirmative, and strict demonstration are each better than its counterpart. It was said in the First Teaching: Since demonstrations are [1] some universal, [b] some particular, [t] some affirmative, [th] and negative, [j] and strict [h] and non-strict, it is necessary to investigate whether the universal is better or the particular? And whether the affirmative is better or the negative? Then it was said that someone might think that the particular is better than the universal (1), by ways: [1] Among them is that the particular demonstration is by itself and from itself, unlike the universal; what is by itself is better than what is by another. We say this because if we demonstrate that Zayd is a musician or rational from Zayd himself, it is better than demonstrating that every human is so. For this is a demonstration of the thing from itself, while that is a demonstration of it not from itself but from an attribute in another. [2] Among them is that the particular necessarily exists. The universal either does not exist but is only imaginary, or exists within the particulars and subsists by them. If so, then demonstration of the particular is a demonstration of what actually exists, unlike demonstration of the universal; so it is better. [3] Among them is that the universal is highly liable to error, because it is not verified unless its judgment in the universal is verified, but it is possible that in some [particulars] it is otherwise. If so, then the particular is better. [4] Among them is that one who demonstrates by universal demonstration demonstrates something other than the desired conclusion. For example, if it is demonstrated that proportional quantities, when permuted, remain proportional, one does not demonstrate essentially of a line or a surface but of something that is not any of these. And demonstration of the desired conclusion is better than demonstration of what is not desired. [5] Among them is that the particular has less existence relative to the universal, so uncovering it and its necessary consequences is more [accessible]; thus assent is greater, so it is better.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical investigation into whether universal, affirmative, and strict demonstrations are superior to their opposites. The author recalls that in Aristotle's Prior Analytics (the First Teaching) demonstrations are classified as universal or particular, affirmative or negative, strict or non-strict. The question is asked: which is better? Then it is reported that someone might think particular demonstration is better, and five arguments are given. The first argument: particular demonstration is from the thing itself (e.g., 'Zayd is rational because he is Zayd') while universal demonstration shows the attribute through another thing (e.g., 'every human is rational because all humans are animals and all animals are rational'). What is by itself is superior. The second: the particular actually exists; the universal exists only in the mind or in particulars, so demonstrating what actually exists is better. The third: universal judgments may be false in some cases (e.g., 'all birds fly' is false for ostriches), so particular judgments are more certain. The fourth: universal demonstrations often prove something more general than the desired conclusion, as when proving about proportional quantities in general rather than about a specific line; proving exactly the desired conclusion is better. The fifth: since particulars are fewer in number than universals, it is easier to fully grasp them and their consequences, leading to stronger assent. These arguments set the stage for a defense of the superiority of universal demonstration, presumably to follow.

البرهان الكلي
البرهان الجزئي
الموجب
السالب
المستقيم

The five arguments present a strong prima facie case for particular demonstration, but they are set up as objections to be answered in favor of universal demonstration.

How would you respond to someone who says proving about 'humanity' is less valuable than proving about Zayd? The Superiority of the Universal in Demonstration
Author presents seven arguments and refines them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess the epistemological priority of universals over particulars in demonstrative proof, using the seven arguments and their refinement to distinguish demonstratively valid from merely dialectical reasoning.

Core Idea
The universal gives knowledge of all particulars potentially; the particular gives no knowledge of the universal.

In this passage, the author presents seven reasons why the universal (al-kulli) is superior to the particular (al-juz'i) in demonstration (burhan). These reasons are attributed to Aristotle's 'First Teaching' (Posterior Analytics). However, the author also refines them, noting that some are dialectical and only a few are truly demonstrative. This lesson helps you understand the epistemological foundation of universals in Islamic logic and how they enable certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence that states the universal is better than the particular for demonstration. It is at the beginning: 'there are also points that indicate that the universal is better'.
2
Extract the seven arguments
The text enumerates seven arguments with [1] to [7]. List each one in your own words: (1) knowledge of universal is more extensive, (2) the term is univocal and one demonstration suffices, (3) universal not separate from particulars, (4) universal gives the 'why', (5) particulars are infinite so unknowable, (6) knowledge of universal gives knowledge of particular potentially, (7) universal's middle term closer to principle.
Pay attention to the geometric example in argument [4].
3
Analyze the refinement
The author then states that some of these are dialectical. The truly compelling argument is that knowledge of universal is potential knowledge of particular, and that universal is intelligible while particular is sensible and not truly knowable. Which arguments survive this refinement?
The sixth argument is the one the author endorses.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the universal 'intoxicant' in fiqh. Knowledge of this universal allows you to know that every particular intoxicant (e.g., khamr) is forbidden, without needing to examine each one. This illustrates the epistemological superiority of the universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الوجوه وإن دلت على أن الجزئي أفضل فمن الوجوه أيضًا ما يدل على أن الكلي أفضل كما قيل في التعليم الأول إنه أفضل)، [۱] فإن العلم به أكثر؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان المثلث المتساوي بالساقين زواياه كذا وكذا ليس لأنه متساوي الساقين بل لأنه مثلث. فالذي يعلم ذلك لا من جهة ما هو متساوي الساقين بل من جهة ما هو مثلث؛ فعلمه أكثر؛ إذ يعلم ذلك في غير متساوي الساقين كما يعلمه في المتساوي. وإذا علمه للمثلث فقد علمه لما هو له بالذات بخلاف ما إذا علمه للمتساوي الساقين فالكلي إذن أفضل. [٢] ومنها أن اللفظ الدال على طبيعة الكلي ليس اسمًا مشتركا بل متواطيا؛ وليست طبيعته في الجزئيات كطبيعة الأعراض بل طبيعة ملائمة للجوهر داخلة في الحد. وليس وجوده أقل من وجود الآحاد وإن كان هو واحدًا؛ وتلك لا نهاية لها فإن وجود الباقي أكثر من وجود الفاسد والبرهان على الجزئي الفاسد من جهة ما هو جزئي يكاد لا يتناهى إذا لم يجمع في كلي اشتركت فيه أمور بلا نهاية؛ ويتَّحد به فيكفها كلها برهان واحد ولولا ذلك لاحتاج إلى براهين بغير نهاية. [٣] ومنها أنه ليس يجب على المبرهن من جهة برهانه على الكلي أنه إن لم يجعل الكلي معدومًا يلزمه أن يجعله شيئًا مناسبًا للجزئيات. وإنما يظن أنه خارج عن الجزئيات بسبب إفرادنا البرهان عليه؛ وليسكذلك؛ إذ الجواهر الكلية نحو الأعراض الكلية مثل الكيفية والكمية وغيرها، والأعراض الكلية لا يمكن أن تكون خارجة عن الجزئيات، وإذا لم تكن خارجة عن الجزئيات كان البرهان عليها برهانا على المطلوبات بالذات فكان أفضل. [٤] ومنها أن البرهان هو قياس من العلة واللمية، والكلي أولى بأن يعطي اللمية لما مر من قبل ؛ ولأنَّا إذا سلبنا عن الجزئي أن هذا المثلث مثلا لِمَ زواياه الخارجة مساوية لأربع قوائم؛ وأجبنا بشيء جزئي كما إذا قلنا لأنه مخطوط في ثوب، أو لأنه هذا المثلث لم يكن جوابا عن العلة الذاتية إلا أن نقول لأنه شكل تحيط به ثلاثة خطوط مستقيمة كل واحد منها إذا أخرج ارتسم حوله متساويان لقائمتين فيكون جميعه ست زوايا اثنان منها داخلتان فبقي الخارج أربعًا. فنحن إذن في إعطاء العلة نضطر إلى البرهان على الكلي؛ وإنه يدل على كونه أفضل. [٥] ومنها أن الجزئيات غير متناهية ولا محدودة فلا تكون معلومة؛ والكلي بسيط محدود فيكون معلوما. فالعلم الذاتي إذن إنما هو الكلي وهو أكثر في معنى المعلومية وأولى بأن يكون المقصود بالبرهان. وإذا كان هو أولى بالبراهين فالبراهين أولى به؛ إذ الأولوية من باب المضاف، وإذا كان هو أولى فهو أولى. [٦] ومنها أن الذي إذا علم هو علم غيره من غير انعكاس فهو أولى بأن يفيد العلم من ذلك الغير. والكلي كذلك فإن العلم به هو العلم به، وبالجزئي أيضًا بالقوة القريبة. والجزئي ليس كذلك فإنه لا يلزم من العلم به العلم بالكلي لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة، فإذن الكلي أولى وأفضل. [٧] ومنها أن الكلي هو الذي يكون الحد الأوسط فيه أقرب إلى المبدأ فيكون أشد استقصاء في كل شيء وآكد في هذا المعنى مما هو أبعد منه من المبدأ؛ فيكون الأفضل.
فهذه كلها مما قيل في التعليم الأول ولكن يشبه أن يكون الأمر على ما قال المعلم الأوّل بنفسه من أن بعض هذه الوجوه جدلية؛ والذي يجب أن يصغى إليه من هذه الحجج هو أن العلم بالكلي علم بالقوة وبالجزئي، ومبدأ البرهان على الجزئي. وأما العلم بالجزئي فليس فيه البتة علم بالكلي. وأيضًا فإن الكلي معقول، والعلم الحقيقي للعقل. وأما الجزئي فمحسوس؛ والمحسوس من جهة ما هو محسوس لا علم به ولا برهان عليه.
(۱) أنا لوطيقا الثانية لأرسطو طاليس، ص ٥٢٠ إلخ.
So these points, even if they indicate that the particular is better, there are also points that indicate that the universal is better, as it was said in the First Teaching that it is better. [1] Because knowledge of it is more extensive. That is because if the isosceles triangle has such and such angles, not because it is isosceles but because it is a triangle. He who knows that not by virtue of its being isosceles but by virtue of its being a triangle has more knowledge, since he knows that in non-isosceles as he knows it in the isosceles. And if he knows it for the triangle, he has known it for what it is essentially, unlike if he knows it for the isosceles. So the universal is better. [2] And among them is that the word indicating the nature of the universal is not a homonym but a univocal term; and its nature in particulars is not like the nature of accidents, but a nature suitable to substance, entering into the definition. And its existence is not less than the existence of individuals, even if it is one, and those are infinite. For the existence of the permanent is more than the existence of the perishable, and the demonstration concerning the perishable particular insofar as it is particular is almost infinite if it is not gathered into a universal in which infinite things participate, and it unites with it so that one demonstration suffices for all of them. Otherwise one would need infinite demonstrations. [3] And among them is that it is not necessary for the demonstrator concerning his demonstration of the universal that if he does not make the universal non-existent, he must make it something suitable to particulars. One only thinks that it is outside the particulars because we isolate the demonstration upon it, and it is not so. For universal substances, like universal accidents such as quality and quantity and others, and universal accidents cannot be outside the particulars. And if they are not outside the particulars, then the demonstration concerning them is a demonstration concerning the objects sought essentially, so it is better. [4] And among them is that demonstration is a syllogism from cause and why (al-‘illa wa al-limiya), and the universal is more worthy of giving the why, as passed before. And because if we strip from the particular that this triangle, for example, why its external angles are equal to four right angles, and we answer with a particular thing, as if we say because it is drawn on a cloth, or because it is this triangle, it would not be an answer concerning the essential cause unless we say because it is a shape enclosed by three straight lines, each of which when extended, there arises around it two equal to two right angles, so the total is six angles, two of which are interior, so the external remain four. Thus we, in giving the cause, are compelled to demonstration concerning the universal, and that indicates that it is better. [5] And among them is that particulars are infinite and unlimited, so they cannot be known; the universal is simple and limited, so it is known. So essential knowledge is only of the universal, and it is more in the sense of being knowable, and more worthy of being the object of demonstration. And if it is more worthy of demonstrations, then demonstrations are more worthy of it, since priority is from the category of relative. And if it is more worthy, then it is better. [6] And among them is that that which when known, something other than it is known without reciprocity, is more worthy of giving knowledge than that other. And the universal is like that, for knowledge of it is knowledge of it, and of the particular also by proximate potentiality. The particular is not like that, for knowledge of it does not entail knowledge of the universal either actually or potentially. So then the universal is more worthy and better. [7] And among them is that the universal is such that its middle term is closer to the principle, so it is more exhaustive in every thing and more emphatic in this meaning than that which is farther from the principle; so it is better.
All these are what was said in the First Teaching, but it seems that the matter is as the First Teacher himself said, that some of these points are dialectical. What must be listened to from these arguments is that knowledge of the universal is knowledge potentially and of the particular, and it is the principle of demonstration concerning the particular. As for knowledge of the particular, it contains no knowledge of the universal at all. Also, the universal is intelligible, and true knowledge belongs to the intellect. As for the particular, it is sensible, and the sensible insofar as it is sensible cannot be known nor demonstrated. (1) Analytica Posteriora of Aristotle, p. 520 etc.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the superiority of the universal (kulli) over the particular (juz'i) in the context of demonstration (burhan). The author presents seven arguments attributed to Aristotle's 'First Teaching' (i.e., Posterior Analytics), then critiques them, noting that some are merely dialectical, and identifies the strongest arguments.

The passage begins by stating that despite earlier points suggesting the particular is better, there are also points showing the universal is better. The first argument [1] is that knowledge of the universal is more extensive. For example, knowing that any triangle has a property (like angle sum) because it is a triangle gives knowledge applicable to all triangles, including non-isosceles, so it is more comprehensive.

Second [2], the term denoting a universal nature (like 'human') is univocal (mutawatia), not homonymous. Its nature in particulars is substantial, not accidental, and its existence is not less than individuals; in fact, demonstrations concerning infinite particulars would require infinite proofs without the universal, so one universal demonstration suffices.

Third [3], the universal is not separate from particulars as some think. Universal substances and accidents (like quality) are not outside particulars, so demonstration on the universal is essentially demonstration on the particulars.

Fourth [4], demonstration is a syllogism giving the cause (al-‘illa) and the 'why' (al-limiya). The universal better provides the essential cause. For instance, explaining why a particular triangle's external angles equal four right angles leads to a universal geometric proof, not a particular one like 'it is drawn on a cloth'.

Fifth [5], particulars are infinite and thus unknowable; the universal is simple and finite, so it is known. Essential knowledge is of the universal, making it more worthy of demonstration.

Sixth [6], if knowing X automatically gives knowledge of Y without reciprocity, X is superior. Knowing the universal gives potential knowledge of its particulars, but not vice versa.

Seventh [7], the universal's middle term is closer to the first principle, making the demonstration more exhaustive.

The author then notes that some of these arguments are dialectical (jadaliyya) as Aristotle himself indicated. The truly compelling argument is that knowledge of the universal is potential knowledge of the particular and is the principle of demonstration for it. Moreover, the universal is intelligible (ma'qul), and genuine knowledge belongs to the intellect, while the particular is sensible (mahsus) and as such cannot be truly known or demonstrated.

This analysis reinforces the epistemological primacy of universals in scientific demonstration, a key theme in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
الكلي
الجزئي
البرهان
اللمية
جدلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which argument is considered truly demonstrative (not merely dialectical) for the superiority of the universal?
A) Knowledge of the universal is more extensive.
B) The universal is a univocal term.
C) Knowledge of the universal gives potential knowledge of the particular.
D) The universal's middle term is closer to the principle.

Key Takeaway
The universal is epistemologically superior because it provides potential knowledge of all particulars and is the proper object of demonstrative science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Evaluating Arguments for Superiority of Universal Knowledge
Author presents and critiques arguments that universal knowledge is superior in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three arguments for the superiority of universals over particulars in demonstration and identify which one the author deems valid.

Core Idea
The universal gives potential knowledge; the particular is actual and sensible. Only the intelligible universal is the object of true demonstration.

In this passage, the author reviews three arguments from Aristotle's Posterior Analytics that attempt to show why universal knowledge is superior to particular knowledge in demonstration. However, he critically evaluates them, noting that some are merely dialectical. We will walk through each argument and see which one survives scrutiny, and why that matters for Islamic sciences like kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three arguments
Locate the three numbered arguments in the Arabic passage: [5], [6], [7]. Each begins with 'ومنها' (among them is).
The arguments are presented as reasons for the superiority of universals.
2
Extract premise and conclusion for each
For argument [5]: premises: particulars are infinite and unlimited, so unknowable; universals are simple and limited, so knowable. Conclusion: essential knowledge is only of the universal. For [6]: premises: knowing the universal entails knowing the particular potentially but not vice versa. Conclusion: universal is more deserving of conveying knowledge. For [7]: the universal's middle term is closer to the principle, making demonstration more exhaustive.
Argument [6] is the key one the author later partially accepts.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author states that some of these ways are dialectical (جدلية), not demonstrative. He then specifies what should be heeded: only the point that knowledge of the universal is potential and of the particular is actual, and that the universal is intelligible while the particular is sensible.
The author uses 'يشبه أن يكون الأمر على ما قال المعلم الأول' to introduce his critique.
4
Determine the valid argument
The valid argument is that the universal is intelligible (معقول) and gives potential knowledge (علم بالقوة) of particulars, while the particular is sensible (محسوس) and cannot be known scientifically in itself. This aligns with the Islamic view that universal rulings are the object of demonstration.
Thus, the only sound argument is the second one (with the non-reciprocal implication) when combined with the intelligibility point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنحن إذن في إعطاء العلة نضطر إلى البرهان على الكلي؛ وإنه يدل على كونه أفضل. [٥] ومنها أن الجزئيات غير متناهية ولا محدودة فلا تكون معلومة؛ والكلي بسيط محدود فيكون معلوما. فالعلم الذاتي إذن إنما هو الكلي وهو أكثر في معنى المعلومية وأولى بأن يكون المقصود بالبرهان. وإذا كان هو أولى بالبراهين فالبراهين أولى به؛ إذ الأولوية من باب المضاف، وإذا كان هو أولى فهو أولى. [٦] ومنها أن الذي إذا علم هو علم غيره من غير انعكاس فهو أولى بأن يفيد العلم من ذلك الغير. والكلي كذلك فإن العلم به هو العلم به، وبالجزئي أيضًا بالقوة القريبة. والجزئي ليس كذلك فإنه لا يلزم من العلم به العلم بالكلي لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة، فإذن الكلي أولى وأفضل. [٧] ومنها أن الكلي هو الذي يكون الحد الأوسط فيه أقرب إلى المبدأ فيكون أشد استقصاء في كل شيء وآكد في هذا المعنى مما هو أبعد منه من المبدأ؛ فيكون الأفضل. فهذه كلها مما قيل في التعليم الأول ولكن يشبه أن يكون الأمر على ما قال المعلم الأوّل بنفسه من أن بعض هذه الوجوه جدلية؛ والذي يجب أن يصغى إليه من هذه الحجج هو أن العلم بالكلي علم بالقوة وبالجزئي، ومبدأ البرهان على الجزئي. وأما العلم بالجزئي فليس فيه البتة علم بالكلي. وأيضًا فإن الكلي معقول، والعلم الحقيقي للعقل. وأما الجزئي فمحسوس؛ والمحسوس من جهة ما هو محسوس لا علم به ولا برهان عليه.
So we, then, in giving the cause, are compelled to demonstrate the universal; and it indicates its being better. [5] Among them is that particulars are infinite and unlimited, so they cannot be known; the universal is simple and limited, so it is known. Essential knowledge, then, is only the universal, and it is more in the meaning of knowability and more deserving to be the aim of demonstration. And if it is more deserving of demonstrations, then demonstrations are more deserving of it; for priority is a relative matter, and if it is more deserving, then it is more deserving. [6] Among them is that that which, when known, makes something else known without reciprocal implication, is more deserving of conveying knowledge than that other. The universal is like that, for knowledge of it is knowledge of it, and of the particular also in proximate potentiality. The particular is not like that, for knowledge of it does not entail knowledge of the universal either actually or potentially. So the universal is more deserving and better. [7] Among them is that the universal is that whose middle term is closer to the principle, so it is more exhaustive in everything and more emphatic in this meaning than that which is farther from the principle; thus it is better. All these are among what was said in the First Teaching, but it seems that the matter is as the First Teacher himself said: that some of these ways are dialectical. What must be heeded from these arguments is that knowledge of the universal is potential knowledge, and of the particular is actual, and the principle of demonstration is upon the particular. As for knowledge of the particular, there is absolutely no knowledge of the universal in it. Also, the universal is intelligible, and true knowledge belongs to the intellect. As for the particular, it is sensible; and the sensible, insofar as it is sensible, has no knowledge of it nor demonstration upon it.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is an evaluative discussion on whether universal or particular knowledge is superior in demonstration. The author presents three arguments (numbered 5-7) from the Aristotelian tradition, then critiques them, accepting only one as valid. The first argument states that particulars are infinite and thus unknowable, while universals are simple and limited, making them the proper object of essential knowledge. The second argument appeals to the property of non-reciprocal implication: knowing the universal gives potential knowledge of particulars, but knowing a particular does not yield knowledge of the universal. The third argument concerns the middle term: the universal's middle term is closer to the first principle (the subject genus), making the demonstration more exhaustive. The author then notes that these are from Aristotle's 'Posterior Analytics' (the First Teaching), but that some are dialectical. The only sound point, according to the author, is that knowledge of the universal is potential (yielding actual knowledge of particulars through demonstration), while knowledge of the particular is actual and sensible. True scientific knowledge (episteme) is of the intelligible universal, not the sensible particular. This aligns with the Islamic view that universal rulings (e.g., 'all intoxicants are haram') are known through demonstration, while particular instances are only known through sense perception or testimony.
