Five Arguments for Affirmative Demonstrations
Author presents five arguments for superiority of affirmative over negative demonstrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze and evaluate the five arguments given for the superiority of affirmative demonstrations over negative ones in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Affirmative demonstrations are more fundamental because they are simpler, prior, and directly known.

The author presents five distinct reasons why affirmative demonstrations (al-barāhīn al-mūjiba) are superior to negative ones. These arguments appeal to parsimony, epistemic priority, composition, and teleology. In this lesson, we will unpack each argument, assess its logical merits, and see how this debate connects to broader concerns in Islamic philosophy and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis and the five arguments
Skim the Arabic text and find the claim that affirmative demonstrations are better. Note the five numbered arguments (بوجوه). List them briefly in your own words.
Each argument begins with ومنها that.
2
Analyze the logical structure of arguments 1-3
For each of the first three arguments, identify the premises and the conclusion. Check if the reasoning is deductive or inductive. Argument 3 claims that negative requires affirmative premise—is that always true?
Argument 3 is about epistemic primacy: negative is only known through affirmative.
3
Analyze arguments 4 and 5
Argument 4 uses composition and the nature of existence (wujūd) to argue for priority. Argument 5 uses teleology. Determine whether these are sound logical arguments or metaphysical assertions.
Argument 4 connects to the broader Islamic philosophical principle that existence is self-evident.
4
Evaluate the overall strength of the case
Consider the author's case as a whole. Are there any counterarguments? How might a defender of negative demonstrations respond?
This step requires critical thinking beyond the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل إن البراهين المأخوذة من المبادئ الموجبة فقط فهو أولى وأفضل؛ وذلك بوجوه: [١] منها أنه لا يفتقر إلى استعمال الأشياء المختلفة الكثيرة الأصناف؛ والبرهان على السلب يفتقر إلى ذلك إذا لم تكن السوالب منتجة. [٢] ومنها أن مبادئ الموجب أقل نوعًا؛ والكائن من الأقل أفضل لما أن الغلط في القليل أقل. [٣] ومنها أن البرهان الموجب إلى مقدمة سالبة أصلا؛ والسالب يفتقر إلى مقدمة موجبة فلا يعرف إلا بالموجب، ولو كان كذلك لكان الموجب أعرف فكان أفضل. [٤] ومنها أن الموجبة أقدم وأعرف أما أنها أقدم فلأنها تتم بحدين ورابطة والسالبة تفتقر إليهما وإلى حرف السلب أيضًا. وأما أنها أعرف فلأن الإيجاب معنى وجودي؛ والمعنى الوجودي يعرف بذاته ويتصور بنفسه. ولا يفتقر في التوهم إلى القياس إلى السلب بخلاف العدمي فإنه إنما يعرف بالوجودي؛ فما لم يعرف الوجود لم يعرف اللا وجود. [٥] ومنها أن الموجب أفضل لما به يفضي إلى الأفضل وهو وجود الشيء للشيء؛ إذ هو الأفضل في الجملة وإلا لا يمكن اتصافه بكونه أفضل أصلا؛ وليس كذلك.
Then it was said that demonstrations taken only from affirmative premises are more worthy and better; and that by aspects: [1] Among them is that it does not require the use of many different kinds of things; the demonstration of a negative does require that if the negatives are not productive. [2] Among them is that the principles of the affirmative are fewer in type; what comes from the lesser is better because error in the few is less. [3] Among them is that the affirmative demonstration proceeds to a negative premise in no way; the negative requires an affirmative premise, so it is not known except by the affirmative; if that is the case, then the affirmative is more known and thus better. [4] Among them is that the affirmative is prior and more known: prior because it is completed by two terms and a copula, while the negative needs them and also a negation particle. More known because affirmation is an existential meaning; the existential meaning is known by itself and conceived by itself. It does not require, in supposition, to be measured against negation, unlike the privative, which is only known by the existential; for unless existence is known, non-existence is not known. [5] Among them is that the affirmative is better because it leads to what is better, namely the existence of a thing for a thing; for that is better in general; otherwise it could not be described as being better at all; and it is not like that.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is a set of five arguments asserting the superiority of affirmative demonstrations over negative ones in demonstration. The author lists them as part of a broader discussion on the best type of demonstration. The first argument notes that affirmative demonstrations avoid the need to handle diverse negative cases, which are required when negatives are not productive in a syllogism. The second appeals to parsimony: affirmative principles are fewer in kind, reducing chances of error. The third argument is epistemic: an affirmative demonstration never uses a negative premise, whereas a negative demonstration requires an affirmative premise to be known; thus the affirmative is more known and better. The fourth argument points to priority in composition (affirmative has two terms and a copula, negative adds a negation particle) and in epistemological primacy: existence (wujūd) is known directly, while non-existence requires prior knowledge of existence. This is crucial: in Islamic theology, existence is a primary concept. The fifth argument is teleological: the affirmative leads to a better thing, i.e., the existence of something for something (e.g., 'man is rational'), whereas the negative only denies. The conclusion is that affirmative demonstrations are inherently more fundamental and better.

Key Terms
الموجب
السالب
المعنى الوجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It uses fewer terms.
B) It is composed of two terms and a copula only.
C) Affirmation is an existential meaning known by itself, while negation is privative and depends on existence.
D) Affirmation is easier to memorize.

Key Takeaway
Affirmative demonstrations are superior because they are simpler, epistemically prior, and lead to a better conclusion — the existence of an attribute for a subject.

Three Reasons Direct Demonstration is Superior to Reductio
Author presents three reasons for superiority of direct over reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare direct and reductio demonstrations and explain why direct is considered better based on three specific criteria.

Core Idea
Direct demonstrations are epistemically self-sufficient; reductio requires extra steps and less certain premises.

In this passage, the author argues that direct demonstrations (mustaqīm) are superior to reductio ad absurdum (khalaf). He gives three reasons: logical self-sufficiency, epistemic priority of premises, and economy of premises. Understanding these criteria helps you evaluate the strength of different types of proofs in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of demonstration
Find the Arabic terms for 'direct' (المستقيم) and 'reductio' (الخلف). Note that the author has already defined them earlier; here he compares them.
2
Extract the first reason: logical self-sufficiency
The direct demonstration alone necessitates the conclusion by its own truth, while reductio requires another syllogism (it proves by falsehood). Understand why this makes direct more 'worthy'.
Direct: 'يجيب لصدقه وحده وبذاته صدق النتيجة'; Reductio: 'يوجب بكذبه صدق النتيجة لا بذاته ولا وحده بل بقياس آخر'.
3
Extract the second reason: epistemic status of premises
Direct premises are known by themselves and accepted; reductio premises are assumed for the sake of argument. The former is more certain, so the proof is nobler.
مقدمات المستقيم أعرف لما أنها معروفة بذواتها ومسلمة.
4
Extract the third reason: economy of premises
Direct demonstrations use fewer premises than reductio, which is better because 'what comes from the lesser is better.'
مقدماتها أقل بالنسبة إلى مقدمات الخلف.
5
Apply the criteria to an Islamic proof
Consider a proof from kalam: 'The world is temporally originated; everything originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' This is direct. Contrast with a reductio: 'Assume the world has no cause, then... contradiction, so it must have a cause.' Which criteria make the direct version preferable?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل إن البراهين المستقيمة أفضل من الغير وهو الخلف؛ وقد مرَّ ذكرهما من قبل فلا حاجة إلى الإعادة، وذلك بوجوه أيضًا : [١] منها أن المستقيم كما مر هو الذي يوجب لصدقه وحده وبذاته صدق النتيجة؛ فلا يفتقر إلى قياس آخر بخلاف الخلف فإنه يوجب بكذبه صدق النتيجة لا بذاته ولا وحده بل بقياس آخر فيكون المستقيم أولى وأفضل . [۲] ومنها أن المستقيم مقدماته أعرف لما أنها معروفة بذواتها ومسلمة على خلاف مقدمات الخلف؛ والكائن من الأعرف أعرف وأشرف. [٣] ومنها أن مقدماته أقل بالنسبة إلى مقدمات الخلف؛ وقد مرَّ من قبل أن الكائن من الأقل أفضل.
Then it was said that direct demonstrations are better than the other, namely reductio; and their mention has passed before, so there is no need to repeat. And that by aspects also: [1] Among them is that the direct, as mentioned, is that which by its truth alone and by itself necessitates the truth of the conclusion; so it does not require another syllogism, unlike reductio, which necessitates the truth of the conclusion by its falsehood, not by itself nor alone but by another syllogism; so the direct is more worthy and better. [2] Among them is that the premises of the direct are more known because they are known by themselves and accepted, unlike the premises of reductio; and what comes from the more known is more known and nobler. [3] Among them is that its premises are fewer relative to the premises of reductio; and it has passed before that what comes from the lesser is better.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage gives three arguments for why direct demonstrations (mustaqīm) are superior to reductio ad absurdum (khalaf). The first argument is logical self-sufficiency: a direct proof yields the conclusion directly from true premises, while reductio relies on assuming the opposite, deriving a contradiction, and then inferring the original conclusion via an extra syllogism. The second argument concerns the epistemic status of premises: direct demonstrations start from premises that are known and accepted per se, whereas reductio often uses premises that are only assumed for the sake of argument. The third argument is about parsimony: direct proofs use fewer premises. These arguments echo Aristotle's preference for direct demonstration as more 'scientific' (apodeictic). In the Islamic tradition, this relates to proofs in kalam and fiqh: a direct proof (e.g., 'the world is originated, therefore it has a creator') is preferable to a reductio that tries to show the impossibility of no creator.

Key Terms
المستقيم
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given for the superiority of direct demonstration over reductio?
A) Direct demonstration uses fewer terms.
B) Direct demonstration yields the conclusion by its own truth without needing another syllogism.
C) Direct demonstration is easier to construct.
D) Direct demonstration is always affirmative.

Key Takeaway
Direct demonstrations are superior because they are self-sufficient, have more certain premises, and use fewer premises than reductio proofs.

Two Definitions of Demonstration: Necessary and Probable Matters
Author discusses demonstration for necessary vs. probable matters and the possibility of demonstration for the latter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two definitions of demonstration (burhan) given in the passage and determine whether probable matters can be demonstrated under each definition.

Core Idea
Demonstration is not limited to necessary truths; it can apply to probables if defined broadly.

This passage expands on the scope of demonstration, clarifying that while necessary truths are uncontroversially demonstrable, the status of probable truths is debated. The author presents two definitions and shows how each affects the possibility of demonstration for probables. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying demonstrative reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of burhan
Locate the two definitions in the Arabic: (1) any syllogism that gives the cause in the manner of the thing's existence; (2) a syllogism that gives the cause and guarantees absolutely unchanging existence.
The second definition is stricter and excludes probables.
2
Apply each definition to probable matters
Under the first definition, probable matters can be demonstrated using probable premises. Under the second, they cannot; instead, other syllogistic types (dialectical, rhetorical, etc.) apply.
Notice the author uses 'if by demonstration is meant...' to contrast the definitions.
3
Understand the concept of bayan
Bayan is proof that distinguishes existence from non-existence. It is divided into necessary (perpetual merit) and probable (predominant merit). This shows that proof is not confined to necessity.
Bayan is broader than burhan in some senses.
4
Consider possible things in existence and possibility states
In the state of existence, only probables are inquired (rare and equal are not regular). In the state of possibility, a demonstration can show something is possible (not necessary) for probable, equal, and rare cases.
This clarifies that demonstration is possible even for non-necessary existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البرهان على الضروري فقد مرَّ ؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا كلام فيه على خلاف ما يكون على الأكثري فإن فيه من الكلام منهم من إلى ذلك ضرورة انتفاء اليقين فيما يتبعه. وعند المعلم الأول يمكن أن يكون على الأكثري برهان مؤلَّف من مقدمات أكثرية يعطي شيئًا من أسباب الأكثرية. ويكون به يقين غير زائل من جهة ما هو أكثري وإن كان ظناً من جهة ما هو موجود (۱). وبالجملة إن كان المراد بالبرهان كل قياس يكون على الشيء من جهة العلة؛ وعلى نحو وجوده فيكون على الأكثري برهان. فأما إذا كان المراد به ما يعطي العلة على شرط أن يعطي وجودًا غير متغير البتة فلا برهان عليه بل عليه قياس من البرهاني والجدلي والخطابي والمغالطي والشعري. ثم البيان إنما يكون لوجود متميز عن لا وجود وإنه إما أن يكون للاستحقاق على الدوام فيكون ضروريًا، أو لاستحقاق يكون غالبا وهو الأكثري. فالأمور الممكنة تعتبر حال وجودها؛ وحال إمكانها أيضًا : أما حال وجودها فذلك على سبيل التوقع فلا طلب فيه إلا عن الأكثريات ولا قياس عليها فإن لوجودها فضيلة على اللا وجود في الطبع والإرادة كما مرَّ من قبل. وأما المتكافي في الوجود واللا وجود فلا برهان عليه بل عليها يخرجه عن كونه متكافيًا. وأما حال إمكانها فعلى جميع إضافة برهان على الممكن الأكثري وعلى المساوي وعلى (٢) الأقلي أعني البرهان الذي يبين أنه ممكن لا ضروري.
As for the demonstration of the necessary, it has passed; and it is among that about which there is no dispute. Unlike what is the case with the probable, for there is discussion about it, some [holding] that [it is impossible] due to the necessary absence of certainty in what follows it. According to the First Teacher, it is possible that there be a demonstration on the probable composed of probable premises that gives something of the causes of probability. And thereby there is certainty that does not disappear insofar as it is probable, even if it is opinion insofar as it exists. In sum, if by demonstration is meant every syllogism that is about a thing from the perspective of the cause and in the manner of its existence, then there is demonstration on the probable. But if by demonstration is meant what gives the cause on the condition that it gives an existence that is absolutely unchanging, then there is no demonstration on it; rather there is a syllogism of the demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, sophistical, and poetical types. Then the proof (bayān) is only for an existence distinguished from non-existence. And it is either for a perpetual merit, which is necessary, or for a predominant merit, which is the probable. So possible things are considered in their state of existence and also in their state of possibility. As for their state of existence, that is by way of expectation; there is no inquiry except about probables, and no syllogism upon them, for their existence has a merit over non-existence in nature and will, as has passed. As for the equal in existence and non-existence, there is no demonstration on it; rather [something] removes it from being equal. As for their state of possibility, there is a demonstration added to the probable possible, the equal, and the rare, I mean the demonstration that shows that it is possible not necessary.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage addresses the scope of demonstration (burhān). First, the author states that demonstration of necessary truths is uncontroversial. The difficulty lies with probable (aktharī) matters. Some argue that because probability lacks certainty, no demonstration is possible. However, the First Teacher (Aristotle) holds that a demonstration can be composed of probable premises and still yield certainty regarding the probabilistic nature, even if it is opinion regarding particular instances. The author then distinguishes two definitions of demonstration: (1) any syllogism that gives the cause as the thing exists — this allows demonstration of probables; (2) a syllogism that gives the cause and guarantees unchanging existence — this excludes probables, leaving them to other types (dialectical, rhetorical, etc.). Next, he discusses proof (bayān) as pertaining to existence versus non-existence, and divides it into necessary (perpetual) and probable (predominant). He then considers possible things: in their state of existence, we only inquire about probables (since rare and equal are not regular); in their state of possibility, we can have a demonstration that simply shows the thing is possible (not necessary), covering probable, equal, and rare possibilities. The key point is that demonstration is not limited to necessary truths; it can also address probabilistic or possible matters in a qualified sense.

Key Terms
ضروري
أكثري
برهان
بيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When demonstration means giving the cause with unchanging existence.
B) When demonstration means any syllogism that gives the cause in the manner of existence.
C) When demonstration uses dialectical premises.
D) When demonstration is only for necessary matters.

Key Takeaway
The scope of demonstration depends on its definition; under a broad definition, probable matters can be demonstrated, while under a strict definition, they require other syllogistic forms.

Sense Perception Is Not Demonstration
Author argues that sense perception is not demonstration or its principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why sense perception cannot serve as a demonstration or its principle in Islamic logic, and distinguish between universal and particular knowledge.

Core Idea
Sense perception yields particulars; demonstration requires universals.

This passage from a classical logic textbook makes a critical epistemological distinction: sense perception (ḥiss) is neither a demonstration (burhān) nor a principle of demonstration. The reason is that demonstrations and their principles are universals (kulliyyāt) that are not restricted to time, place, or individual instances, whereas sense perception always captures particular, contingent facts. Understanding this distinction is essential for building valid proofs in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author asserts that sense perception is not a demonstration nor a principle of demonstration. Pinpoint this claim in the Arabic text.
2
Understand the reason
The reason given is that demonstrations are universals—they apply to all cases, not just specific times, places, or individuals. Sense perception, by contrast, is always of particular instances.
The key contrast is universal vs. particular.
3
Consider the example
The author gives an example: sensing that the angles of a particular triangle equal two right angles does not establish that it holds for all triangles. A demonstration requires a universal proof, not a single observation.
This example shows why sense data alone cannot yield certainty.
4
Draw the implication
Sense perception can only provide raw material for induction (istiqra'), but induction does not produce the certainty required for demonstration. Demonstrations must rest on universal, necessary premises.
Induction may be probabilistic, but demonstration is certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل في التعليم الأول إن الحس» ليس برهانا ولا مبدءًا مبدأ البرهان بما هو حس؛ إذ البراهين ومبادئها كليات لا تختص بوقت وشخص وأين، ولو كنا نحس / أن زوايا المثلث المحسوس مساوية لقائمتين لما كان ينعقد لنا [٣٠٨]
Then it was said in the First Teaching that sense perception is neither a demonstration nor a principle of a demonstration insofar as it is sense; for demonstrations and their principles are universals that are not specific to a time, person, or place. And if we sensed that the angles of a sensed triangle are equal to two right angles, it would not be established for us [that it is always true].
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage critiques the role of sense perception in demonstration. The author references the 'First Teaching' (likely Aristotle's Posterior Analytics), stating that sense perception (al-ḥiss) is neither a demonstration (burhān) nor a principle (mabda') of demonstration, because demonstrations and their principles are universal (kulliyyāt) — they do not depend on a specific time, person, or place. In contrast, sense perception is always of particular, contingent instances. The author gives an example: if we merely sensed that the angles of a particular measured triangle are equal to two right angles, we could not conclude that it holds universally for all triangles. To have a demonstration, we need a universal proof. The passage implies that sense data can only serve as a starting point for induction, not as the foundation of certain knowledge required for demonstration.

Key Terms
حس (ḥiss): Sense perception; perception of particulars via the senses, as opposed to universal intellection. [Note: Stable term; same meaning in falsafah and logic.]
كلي (kullī): Universal; that which applies to all individuals of a kind, not restricted to time, place, or person. [Note: Standard term in logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is sense perception not a principle of demonstration according to the passage?
A) Because sense perception is subject to error
B) Because demonstrations are universal, but sense perception is particular
C) Because sense perception only deals with material objects
D) Because demonstrations require necessary premises

Key Takeaway
Demonstrations are universal; sense perception is particular, so it cannot by itself serve as demonstration or its principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Scope of Demonstration: Necessary vs. Majority Matters
Author discusses demonstration on necessary vs. majority matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the broad and strict definitions of burhan and apply them to determine whether a demonstration is possible for majority matters.

Core Idea
Burhan's scope depends on definition: broad gives demonstration for majority; strict only for necessary.

The author has already discussed demonstration on necessary matters without controversy. Now he turns to whether demonstration can also apply to matters that hold for the most part (al-akthari). This hinges on how we define 'demonstration'—a key distinction that affects the certainty of our knowledge in various Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of burhan
Find where the author states: if burhan means any syllogism giving the cause in the manner of existence, then majority matters can have burhan; if it means giving the cause with unchangeable existence, then they cannot.
Look for the phrase 'إن كان المراد بالبرهان كل قياس...' and 'فأما إذا كان المراد به...'.
2
Extract the conditions for each definition
Broad: 'every syllogism that is about a thing from the aspect of its cause, and in the manner of its existence.' Strict: 'that which gives the cause on the condition that it gives existence that is unchangeable at all.'
3
Apply the definitions to majority matters
Under the broad definition, a burhan can be composed of majority premises and yields certainty regarding its general truth, though opinion regarding specific instances. Under the strict, no burhan, only syllogisms of other types (dialectical, rhetorical, etc.).
The author says: 'يكون به يقين غير زائل من جهة ما هو أكثري وإن كان ظناً من جهة ما هو موجود.'
4
Connect to the states of possible things
The author distinguishes existence state (expectation, only majority matters sought, no syllogism) from possibility state (demonstration possible for all types—majority, equal, minority).
The equal (mutakafi) has no demonstration in existence, but in possibility a demonstration can show it is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البرهان على الضروري فقد مرَّ؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا كلام فيه على خلاف ما يكون على الأكثري فإن فيه من الكلام منهم من إلى ذلك ضرورة انتفاء اليقين فيما يتبعه. وعند المعلم الأول يمكن أن يكون على الأكثري برهان مؤلَّف من مقدمات أكثرية يعطي شيئًا من أسباب الأكثرية. ويكون به يقين غير زائل من جهة ما هو أكثري وإن كان ظناً من جهة ما هو موجود. وبالجملة إن كان المراد بالبرهان كل قياس يكون على الشيء من جهة العلة؛ وعلى نحو وجوده فيكون على الأكثري برهان. فأما إذا كان المراد به ما يعطي العلة على شرط أن يعطي وجودًا غير متغير البتة فلا برهان عليه بل عليه قياس من البرهاني والجدلي والخطابي والمغالطي والشعري. ثم البيان إنما يكون لوجود متميز عن لا وجود وإنه إما أن يكون للاستحقاق على الدوام فيكون ضروريًا، أو لاستحقاق يكون غالبا وهو الأكثري. فالأمور الممكنة تعتبر حال وجودها؛ وحال إمكانها أيضًا : أما حال وجودها فذلك على سبيل التوقع فلا طلب فيه إلا عن الأكثريات ولا قياس عليها فإن لوجودها فضيلة على اللا وجود في الطبع والإرادة كما مرَّ من قبل. وأما المتكافي في الوجود واللا وجود فلا برهان عليه بل عليها يخرجه عن كونه متكافيًا. وأما حال إمكانها فعلى جميع إضافة برهان على الممكن الأكثري وعلى المساوي وعلى الأقلي أعني البرهان الذي يبين أنه ممكن لا ضروري.
As for the demonstration of the necessary, it has already passed; and it is among that which there is no discussion about, unlike what is on the majority (akthari), for there is discussion about it, from them who [say] that due to the necessity of the absence of certainty in what follows it. And according to the First Teacher (Aristotle), a demonstration can be on the majority, composed of majority premises, giving something of the causes of majority. And by it there is certainty that does not cease, with respect to its being majority, even if it is opinion with respect to its being existent. In summary, if by demonstration is meant every syllogism that is about a thing from the aspect of its cause, and in the manner of its existence, then there is a demonstration on the majority. But if what is meant is that which gives the cause on the condition that it gives existence that is unchangeable at all, then there is no demonstration on it, but rather there is a syllogism falling under demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, sophistical, and poetical. Then the explanation (bayan) is only for existence distinguished from non-existence, and it is either for deserving perpetually, so it is necessary, or for deserving that is frequent, and it is the majority. The possible things are considered with respect to their state of existence, and also their state of possibility. As for their state of existence, that is in the way of expectation, so there is no request except about the majority matters, and no syllogism about them, for their existence has superiority over non-existence in nature and will, as has passed. As for the equal (mutakafi) in existence and non-existence, there is no demonstration on it, but rather [something] takes it out of being equal. As for their state of possibility, then in all respects there is a demonstration on the possible majority, on the equal, and on the minority, meaning the demonstration that shows that it is possible, not necessary.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is an exploration of the scope of demonstration (burhan). The author begins by contrasting demonstration of necessary truths, which is uncontroversial, with demonstration of matters that hold for the most part (akthari). According to the First Teacher (Aristotle), it is possible to have a demonstration composed of premises that are themselves majority-accepted, yielding a certainty that is stable with respect to its generality, even if it remains opinion regarding specific instances. The key definitional distinction: if burhan is taken broadly as any syllogism that gives the cause of a thing in the manner of its existence, then majority matters can have a burhan. But if burhan is defined more strictly as giving the cause on condition that the existence is absolutely unchangeable, then majority matters cannot have such a burhan; they can only be argued through other types of reasoning (dialectical, rhetorical, etc.). The author then explains that explanation (bayan) concerns existence distinguished from non-existence, either perpetual (necessary) or frequent (majority). Possible things are considered in two states: in the state of existence, they are matters of expectation, and only majority matters are sought, but there is no syllogism about them because existence has natural superiority. For things equal (mutakafi) in existence and non-existence, there is no demonstration. In the state of possibility, demonstration applies to the possible that is majority, equal, or minority—the demonstration that shows something is possible, not necessary.

Key Terms
برهان
الأكثري
المعلم الأول
ضروري
ممكن
متكافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of demonstration can be constructed for matters of the majority (akthari)?
A) A demonstration with necessary premises yielding absolute certainty.
B) A demonstration composed of majority premises giving some causes of majority, yielding certainty from that aspect.
C) No demonstration is possible at all for majority matters.
D) Only dialectical and rhetorical arguments can be used.

Key Takeaway
Whether demonstration can cover majority matters depends on whether burhan is defined broadly or strictly—a distinction that impacts the certainty of knowledge in Islamic sciences.

Why Sense Perception Cannot Be a Principle of Demonstration
Author argues that sense perception cannot be a principle of demonstration because principles are universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument against sense perception as a principle of demonstration and identify the epistemological role of intellect and divine emanation.

Core Idea
Demonstrations require universals; sense only gives particulars, so it cannot be a principle.

This passage tackles a fundamental epistemological question: can sense perception provide the principles for certain knowledge? The author, drawing on the Aristotelian tradition, argues that it cannot. Principles of demonstration must be universal, necessary, and independent of time, place, and observer. Sense perception, by its nature, only grasps particulars and cannot yield such universals. Even induction, which moves from particulars to a general claim, falls short because it cannot generate necessity. The true source of universal concepts is the intellect, illuminated by divine emanation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rejected claim
Find the position the author is arguing against: that sense perception can serve as a demonstration or as a principle of demonstration. The author says this was already stated in the 'First Teaching' (Aristotle's Posterior Analytics).
2
Extract the main reason for rejection
Look for the statement that demonstrations and their principles are 'kulliyat' (universals) not restricted to time, person, or place. This is the core logical reason: sense perception is tied to particulars, but demonstration requires universals.
3
Analyze the triangle example
The author gives a concrete example: even if we sense that a particular triangle has angles equal to two right angles, that sensation alone cannot produce the universal judgment that 'every triangle' has that property. Sensation gives only the particular case, not the universal rule.
The example is taken from geometry, a demonstrative science.
4
Understand why induction fails
The author anticipates a possible objection: could induction (istiqra') from many sensed instances yield a universal? He says no, because induction only 'restricts a universal matter in some of the forms towards demonstration' — that is, it gives a limited generalization, not a necessary, universal truth.
Induction is seen as insufficient for establishing certainty.
5
Identify the alternative source of universals
The author asserts that universal, intelligible concepts come not from sense but from the intellect, which abstracts from many particulars a universal form. This ability is attributed to 'divine emanation' (fayd ilahi), indicating a metaphysical source.
This aligns with Avicennan epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل في التعليم الأول إن الحس ليس برهانا ولا مبدءًا مبدأ البرهان بما هو حس؛ إذ البراهين ومبادئها كليات لا تختص بوقت وشخص وأين، ولو كنا نحس أن زوايا المثلث المحسوس مساوية لقائمتين لما كان ينعقد لنا من إحساس ذلك رأي كلي أن كل مثلث كذلك؛ ولا علم بالعلة. ولا يقال شكل بالاستقراء فإنه يقيد أمرًا كليا في البعض من الصور نحو البرهان. فإن ذلك لا لأن الحس أدركها ونالها ولكن لأن العقل من شأنه أن يقتضي من الجزئيات المتكثرة كليًّا مجرَّدًا معقولا لا سبيل إليه للحس بل لإشراق فيض إلهي عليه. وأما الكلام في كيفية الإحساس واختلاف الآراء فيها فذلك بمعزل عما نحن فيه.
Then it was said in the First Teaching that sense perception is not a demonstration nor a principle of demonstration insofar as it is sense; for demonstrations and their principles are universals that are not restricted to a time, a person, or a place. Even if we were to sense that the angles of a sensible triangle are equal to two right angles, there would not be constituted for us from that sensation a universal opinion that every triangle is such; nor [would we have] knowledge of the cause. And one should not say that it is a syllogism by induction, for it [induction] restricts a universal matter in some of the forms towards demonstration. For that is not because sense perceived and attained them, but because the intellect, by its nature, infers from multiple particulars a universal, abstract, intelligible [concept] to which sense has no path, but rather through an effusion of divine emanation upon it. As for the discussion about the manner of sensation and the differences of opinions about it, that is far from what we are engaged in.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the idea that sense perception can serve as a principle or even a component of demonstration. The author refers to the First Teaching (likely Aristotle's Posterior Analytics) and makes several points: demonstrations and their principles are universal in nature—they do not depend on particular times, persons, or places. Even if we were to sense that a particular triangle has angles equal to two right angles, that sensation alone would not produce the universal judgment that every triangle has that property, nor would it give knowledge of the cause. Induction (istiqra') is not a solution either, because induction can only restrict a universal to some cases, not establish it demonstratively. The real source of universal concepts and principles is the intellect, which abstracts from multiple particulars a universal, intelligible form—something the senses cannot achieve. This is attributed to divine emanation. The author then explicitly sets aside further debate about the nature of sensation, as it is not relevant to the present discussion.

Conditions for Shared Principles in Subordinate Sciences
Author explains when sciences can share principles, using subordinate sciences as examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions for sharing principles between sciences to determine whether two Islamic sciences can share a particular principle.

Core Idea
Only subordinate sciences share particular principles; universal principles are common to all sciences.

This passage from the logical text addresses a key epistemological question: when can different sciences legitimately share principles? The author distinguishes between particular principles, which are specific to a science's subject matter, and universal principles, which apply across all sciences. Understanding this distinction is crucial for recognizing the structure and boundaries of Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish actual vs. potential sharing
The author states that sciences which share a subject 'actually' have one under the other. Identify what 'actual sharing' means: it occurs when one science is subordinate to another, allowing the sharing of particular principles. For example, physics is under metaphysics in the Aristotelian scheme.
In Islamic sciences, Usul al-Fiqh is subordinate to Fiqh because Fiqh derives its principles from Usul.
2
Identify the condition for particular principles
The author says that for two sciences to share a particular principle, they must be related by subordination in their subject genera. If they are not subordinate, they cannot share a particular principle. Find the phrase in the Arabic that expresses this condition.
The Arabic says: 'وأما الأجناس التي ليس بعضها تحت بعض فلا يمكن ذلك فيها' — 'As for genera that are not under each other, that is not possible.'
3
Recognize universal principles as an exception
The author gives the law of non-contradiction ('everything either is or is not') as an example of a universal principle that all sciences can share. Understand that such principles are not tied to any specific subject matter.
This is because universal principles apply to all existents, regardless of the science's subject.
4
Apply the criterion to an Islamic science pair
Take a concrete pair of Islamic sciences, such as Kalam (speculative theology) and Fiqh (jurisprudence). Determine if they are subordinate or not. Since they study different subjects (Kalam studies Allah's attributes, Fiqh studies human actions), they are not subordinate and thus cannot share particular principles.
However, they may both use universal principles like the law of non-contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتشاركة واقعا بعضها تحت بعض. وأما الأجناس التي ليس بعضها تحت بعض فلا يمكن ذلك فيها؛ وأعني بالأجناس الموضوعات. وأما أن يكون مبدءًا داخلا في الوسط للآخر مثل الخطوط المتوارية التي بين المتواريين فيكون حينئذ إما متشاركين في الجنس؛ فيكون أحدهما مبدءًا والآخر نتيجة، أو لا متشاركين في الجنس أعني الموضوع بل في جنسه؛ فيكون أيضًا أحد العلمين تحت الآخر، فيكون الشركة في المبدأ على نحو ما قلناه من قبل. وأما العلوم المختلفة التي ليس بعضها تحت بعض فلا يمكن اشتراكها في المبدأ الخاص البتة. وأما المبادئ العامة مثل قولنا كل شيء إما أن يكون وإما أن لا يكون فقد يشترك فيها؛ فإن هذه المبادئ صالحة في بيان أحوال جميع الموجودات المختلفة التي بعضها كم وبعضها كيف وغير ذلك. وبالجملة لا يكون في العلوم المختلفة اشتراك بالفعل بل بالقوة، والنتائج المطلوبة في العلوم وإن كانت تزيد / على المقدمات على النحو المعلوم في [٣٠٩,] تركيب القياس فليست زيادة مفرطة خارجة عن نسبة محفوظة؛ ولا حاجة إلى الإطناب في هذا الباب.
Those that share [in subject] actually have one under another. As for genera that are not under each other, that is not possible; and by genera I mean subjects. And as for when a principle is internal to the middle of another, like parallel lines between two parallels, then either they share in genus, so one is principle and the other is result, or they do not share in genus (I mean the subject) but in its genus, so also one of the two sciences is under the other, and the sharing in the principle is as we said before. As for different sciences that are not under each other, they cannot share in the particular principle at all. As for universal principles like our saying 'everything either is or is not', they may share in them; for these principles are valid in explaining the states of all diverse existents, some of which are quantity, some quality, and so on. In summary, there is no actual sharing in different sciences, but potential, and the results sought in the sciences, although they exceed the premises in the manner known in the composition of the syllogism, are not an excessive increase outside a preserved proportion; and there is no need for prolixity in this topic.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions under which different sciences can share principles. The author begins by stating that sciences that actually share a subject matter have one under the other. For example, physics is under metaphysics. For genera that are not subordinate, such sharing is impossible. The author clarifies that by 'genera' he means the subjects of sciences. Then he considers a scenario where a principle is internal to the middle term of another science, like parallel lines (a geometric example). In such a case, if the two sciences share in genus, one can serve as principle and the other as result. If they do not share in the subject genus but in a higher genus, still one science falls under the other, allowing sharing. Different sciences that are not subordinate cannot share a particular principle at all. However, universal principles like the law of non-contradiction ('everything either is or is not') can be shared across all sciences because they apply to all existents. Ultimately, sharing in different sciences is not actual but potential. The author concludes by noting that the results in sciences, though they exceed the premises in syllogistic composition, do not exceed in an unbalanced way, so there is no need to elaborate further.

الأجناس (ajnas)
الموضوعات (mawdu'at)

Two Types of Principles: Premises vs Subject Genera
Author distinguishes between principles as premises and principles as subject genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between principles as first premises and principles as subject genera, and explain why minor premises cannot be shared across distinct sciences.

Core Idea
Principles are of two kinds: the first premises and the subject matter; only the first can be general.

In this passage, the author builds on earlier discussions about shared principles among sciences. He clarifies that 'principles' is not a single category but covers two distinct meanings: the first premises from which demonstration proceeds, and the subject genera that define the domain of each science. Understanding this distinction is crucial for recognizing what can and cannot be transferred between disciplines, especially in the Islamic sciences where principles often overlap (e.g., metaphysics and kalam).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of principles
The author explicitly states that principles are said of two types: (1) principles in which demonstration occurs, i.e., the first premises; (2) principles that are the genera of sciences, i.e., their subject matters and what pertains to them.
Look for the Arabic terms: 'مبادئ فيها البرهان' and 'مبادئ هي أجناس العلوم'.
2
Determine which type can be general
The first type (premises) may include general principles, such as the axiom 'things equal to one thing are equal'. The second type (subject genera) must be specific to each science unless two sciences are proportionate in genus.
The author gives the example of 'الواحد بوجه ما للموجود' (the one in a certain respect to the existent) to illustrate a principle that pertains to the subject.
3
Contrast sharing possibilities
For premises that are specific (as mentioned), two sciences do not share them; one affirms the other, and they belong primarily to one science and secondarily to another (e.g., physics and metaphysics).
This shows that even when two sciences share a subject genus, the specific premises may still be exclusive.
4
Analyze the role of minor premises
Since the subjects of scientific problems are either the genus of the art, its species, or an accident, minor premises (الصغريات) cannot be among shared principles. If sharing is necessary, it must be in major premises only.
The author emphasizes that minor premises are too tied to the specific subject to be transferred.
5
Apply the distinction to an Islamic science
Consider kalam and falsafa: they share the subject of 'existent' (الموجود) but have different specific premises. The principle 'everything temporally originated has a cause' is a major premise in kalam but may be a minor premise in physics.
This illustrates how the same principle can be primary in one science and secondary in another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بان فيما سلف أن العلوم تشترك في المبادئ، والمبادئ منها خاصة ومنها عامة فعسى الحق هو أن المبادئ مناسبة في الجنس أي في الموضوع ولكن هذا لا يمكن، وأن العلوم التي لا يناسب في الموضوع فإن مبادئها الخاصة بأجناسها لا تناسب في الموضوع. والذي يجب أن يُعتقد فيه أنه الحق هو أن المبادئ تقال على النوعين: إما مبادئ فيها البرهان أي المقدمات الأولى في العلوم ، وإما مبادئ ففيها البرهان وهي أجناس العلوم أي موضوعاتها، وما يتعلق بها مما يوضع معها، أو يساويها كالواحد بوجه ما للموجود. [1] فالقسم الأول يجوز أن تكون فيها مبادئ عامة كقولنا الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية. [ب] وأما القسم الثاني فلا يجوز إلا أن تكون خاصة أو يتناسب علمان في الجنس. وما كان من المبادئ التي بمعنى المقدمات بما هو خاص كما مرَّ فلا يشترك فيها العلمان: أحدهما صدق الآخر؛ ويكون لأحدهما أولا، وللثاني ثانيًا. ولما كانت الموضوعات في المسائل العلمية إما جنس الموضوع للصناعة أو نوع منه أو عرض منه. فلا يجوز أن تكون الصغريات من المبادئ المشتركة بوجه من الوجوه بل إن كان ولا بد؛ فالكبريات على النحو الذي يجوز به الشركة.
It has become clear from what preceded that sciences share in principles, and among principles are specific and general. Perhaps the truth is that principles are appropriate in genus, i.e., in subject, but this is not possible. And sciences that are not appropriate in subject have their specific principles by their genera not appropriate in subject. What must be believed as true is that principles are said of two types: either principles in which demonstration occurs, i.e., the first premises in the sciences, or principles in which demonstration occurs and they are the genera of the sciences, i.e., their subjects, and what pertains to them that is posited with them, or equals them, like the one in a certain respect to the existent. So the first type may include general principles, like our saying 'things equal to one thing are equal'. The second type is not permissible unless they are specific or two sciences are proportionate in genus. And as for principles that are in the sense of premises, being specific as mentioned, the two sciences do not share in them: one affirms the other; and they belong to one primarily and to the second secondarily. And since the subjects in scientific issues are either the genus of the subject of the art, or a species of it, or an accident of it, it is not permissible for the minor premises to be among shared principles in any way; rather, if necessary, the major premises are in the manner that permits sharing.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous discussion by explicitly distinguishing two types of principles. The author first notes that it was previously established that sciences share principles, and among principles are specific and general. He then considers the possibility that principles are appropriate in genus (i.e., in subject) but dismisses it as impossible. Instead, the correct view is that 'principles' are said of two senses: first, principles in which demonstration occurs, meaning the first premises of the science; second, principles in which demonstration occurs and which are the genera of the sciences, i.e., their subjects and what is associated with them. For example, the concept of 'one' is related to the existent. The first type can include general principles like the axiom 'things equal to one thing are equal to each other'. The second type cannot be general; it must be specific unless two sciences are proportional in genus. Specific premises are not shared between two sciences; they belong to one primarily and to the other secondarily (e.g., physics and metaphysics). Finally, since the subjects of scientific problems are either the genus of the art (e.g., body for physics), a species (e.g., human for medicine), or an accident (e.g., health), minor premises cannot be shared. If sharing must occur, it is only in major premises.

Key Terms
المبادئ فيها البرهان
مبادئ هي أجناس العلوم
الصغريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They share them equally.

C) They cannot share them under any condition.
D) They must be identical for both sciences.

Key Takeaway
Principles in sciences are either first premises (which can be general) or subject genera (which must be specific); minor premises are never shared across sciences.

How Demonstration Can Lead to Definition: The Causal Middle Term
Author explains how demonstration can sometimes lead to definition via causal middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a middle term in a demonstration is a cause of existence or a cause of necessity, and determine if it can help in forming a definition.

Core Idea
A demonstration reveals a definition only when its middle term is the cause of the thing's existence.

We often think of definition and demonstration as separate tasks. Yet, as the author shows, demonstration can sometimes lead directly to definition. This happens when the middle term of the syllogism is the essential cause of the thing being demonstrated. In this lesson, we walk through the criteria for such a demonstration and see how the lunar eclipse example illustrates the process.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the 'that' from the 'what'
Understand that before we can ask 'what is it' (حد), we must first establish 'that it is' (هلية). Both induction and syllogism only prove existence, not essence.
This is the epistemological priority: existence precedes essence.
2
Analyze the middle term's causality
In a demonstration, the middle term can either be the cause of the conclusion's existence (علة وجود) or merely the cause of its necessity (علة لزوم). Only the first type can aid in definition.
If the middle term is not a cause of existence, the demonstration cannot lead to a definition.
3
Connect the middle term to the definition
If the middle term is an essential cause, it will either be the complete definition or a part of it. The demonstration then reveals that definition.
The example in the passage: the lunar eclipse demonstration's middle term (earth screening sunlight) is part of the eclipse's definition.
4
Verify with the lunar eclipse example
Examine the syllogism: The moon eclipses when opposite the sun behind the earth. The middle term 'opposite the sun behind the earth' is the cause of the eclipse. From this, the definition of lunar eclipse is derived.
The definition: the moon ceases to shine due to the earth screening the sunlight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاستقراء أيضًا إنما هو لإثبات هَلِيّة بسيطة كانت أو مركبة، وحكمه حكم القياس. وقد يتفق لبعض البراهين منفعة في جنس بعض الحدود، وبالعكس كما أنا لا نطلب لم الشيء إلا بعد أن يضع هل الشيء كذلك لا يعرف ما الشيء إلا بعد أن يعرف هل الشيء؛ قد يحصل لنا على سبيل الفرض بأن لا يكون الحد الأوسط علة لوجود النتيجة بل علة للزوم، أو يكون عارضًا غريبا لازما؛ وقد يحصل بالذات. وذلك إنا إذا عرفنا الشيء من قياس بحد أوسط هو سبب وجوده فهذا الطريق هو الطريق الذي يؤدي بالهل حقيقته؛ والطريق الأول لا ينفعنا البتة في اكتساب ما هو، وفي اقتناء الحدود. وهذا الطريق لما كان يدل فيه على علة وجود الشيء علة ذاتية فلا يبعد أن يكون مما يفهمها من وجوده شيئًا زائدا على وجوده المطلق وهو وجود العلة الذاتية. وذلك إما حده وإما جزء من حده؛ فحينئذ لا يبعد أن يبينه مع مراعاة الشرائط المذكورة على حده. ومثال هذا أن من قاس على أن القمر ينكسف فيقال إن القمر قد يقع قبالة الشمس وراء ستر الأرض؛ وإذا وقع كذلك ينكسف؛ فإن كسوف القمر يثبت به. والحد التام لكسوف القمر عندهم هو أن لا يضيء القمر بعد ما كان يضيء بستر الأرض عنه ضوء الشمس لوقوعه من الشمس على القطر، وإنه هو المكتسب من البرهان على الكسوف. ثم الشيء قد يعرف بالفعل، وقد يعرف بقوة قريبة من الفعل؛ وحينئذ يحتاج إلى التنبيه فالبرهان يدل على الحد على سبيل التنبيه. وأما الحد فلا يبرهن عليه البتة. وبالجملة يمكن أن يُصار من البرهان إلى الحد، ويمكن أن يُصار من الحد إلى البرهان كما يُحد الشيء أولا فيقتنص من حده البرهان على عوارضه وخصوصا من الحدود التي فيها شيء علة، وشيء آخر معلول مثل قولنا إن الرعد صوت يحدث في الغمام لطفء النار فيه؛ وطفء النار علة، والصوت معلول ومجموعهما هو الحد التام. والحد بجملته علة صورية للمحدود، وكل جزء منه علة لا محالة؛ وإنما يكون البرهان مفيدًا للحد إذا كان فيه جزء هو علة، وجزء هو معلول على نحو ما قلناه.
And induction (istiqra') is also only for establishing existence, whether simple or composite, and its ruling is the ruling of syllogism. And it may happen that some demonstrations are useful in the genus of some definitions, and vice versa, just as we do not ask the 'why' of a thing except after positing the 'whether' of the thing; similarly, we do not know the 'what' of a thing except after knowing the 'whether' of the thing. It may occur to us by way of hypothesis that the middle term is not the cause of the existence of the conclusion but rather the cause of the necessity (luzum), or a strange accident that is necessary; and it may occur essentially. That is, if we know a thing from a syllogism by a middle term that is the cause of its existence, then this path is the path that leads to its true 'whether'. And the first path is of no use to us at all in acquiring the 'what', and in obtaining definitions. And this path, since it indicates the cause of existence of the thing—an essential cause—it is not far-fetched that it makes us understand from its existence something additional to its absolute existence, namely the existence of the essential cause. And that is either its definition or a part of its definition. Then it is not far-fetched that it be explained, with observance of the conditions mentioned, upon its definition. An example of this is one who argues that the moon eclipses: it is said that the moon may come opposite the sun behind the veil of the earth; and if it occurs like that, it eclipses. Then the lunar eclipse is established by this. And the complete definition of the lunar eclipse according to them is that the moon does not shine after it had been shining because the earth screens the sunlight from it, due to its occurrence between the sun and the diameter; and this is what is acquired from the demonstration of the eclipse. Then a thing may be known actually, and it may be known by a potentiality close to actuality; then it needs indication (tanbih). So demonstration indicates the definition by way of indication. As for definition, it is never demonstrated. In sum, it is possible to go from demonstration to definition, and it is possible to go from definition to demonstration, just as one first defines a thing and then from its definition one captures the demonstration of its accidents—especially from definitions in which there is something that is a cause and something else that is an effect, like our saying: 'Thunder is a sound occurring in the cloud due to the extinguishing of fire in it.' The extinguishing of fire is the cause, and the sound is the effect, and their combination is the complete definition. The definition in its entirety is a formal cause of the defined, and every part of it is a cause as such. Demonstration can only be useful for definition when it contains a part that is a cause and a part that is an effect in the manner we have stated.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous lesson by exploring how demonstration can still be helpful in obtaining definitions, albeit indirectly. The author starts by noting that induction (istiqra') also only establishes existence (هلية), like syllogism. Then he explains that some demonstrations may be useful for definitions, and vice versa. The key idea is that we need to know that something exists (هل الشيء) before asking why it is (لم الشيء), and likewise we need to know existence before knowing essence. The author describes two ways the middle term in a demonstration can relate to the conclusion: either it is not the cause of existence but of necessity (luzum) or a necessary accident, or it can be an essential cause (cause of existence). When the middle term is an essential cause, the demonstration can lead to the definition, because the cause's existence is something beyond the mere existence of the thing, and that cause is either the definition itself or part of it. The example given is the lunar eclipse: one demonstrates that the moon eclipses when it is opposite the sun behind the earth's shadow. From this demonstration, the complete definition of lunar eclipse is derived: the moon ceases to shine due to the earth screening the sunlight, occurring when the moon is on the diameter opposite the sun. Thus, the demonstration points to the definition. The author then distinguishes between actual and potential knowledge: if something is known only potentially, demonstration can 'indicate' (tanbih) the definition. However, definition itself is never demonstrated directly. Finally, he summarizes that one can go from definition to demonstration (by deducing accidents) and from demonstration to definition, especially when the definition contains a causal relation, as in the example of thunder: thunder is sound in clouds due to the extinguishing of fire. The quenching is the cause, the sound is the effect, and the whole is the complete definition. In such cases, demonstration can be useful because the middle term (cause) appears in the definition. This passage shows the nuanced relationship between the two, correcting the oversimplified view that they are completely separate.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
الهلية
الحد الأوسط
علة للزوم
التنبيه
علة صورية

Key Takeaway
A demonstration can lead to a definition only when its middle term is an essential cause of the thing's existence, providing either the complete definition or a part of it.

Co-extensive vs Specific Causes in Demonstration
Author distinguishes co-extensive from specific causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a co-extensive cause (musāwiya) and a more specific cause (akhaṣṣ), and explain why only co-extensive causes can serve as middle terms in demonstrations and definitions.

Core Idea
A co-extensive cause is convertible with its effect; a specific cause is not, and this determines its role in proof.

In this passage, the author builds on earlier causal theory by introducing a critical distinction: causes may be co-extensive with their effects (each implies the other) or more specific (they entail the effect but not vice versa). This distinction is vital for constructing valid demonstrations, because only a co-extensive cause can function as a middle term in a syllogism that proves the existence of the effect. The author illustrates with examples from natural phenomena: clouds have multiple causes, and so does fever. Understanding this distinction helps you evaluate whether a proposed cause can be used in a rigorous proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause and effect pair
Locate the cause (e.g., putrefaction) and the effect (e.g., septic fever). Note whether the cause is being considered for a general or specific effect (e.g., absolute fever vs septic fever).
The effect must be clearly specified.
2
Check uniformity (muṭṭarida)
Determine if the cause always produces the effect. In the text, each specific cause (e.g., putrefaction for septic fever) is uniform: whenever putrefaction occurs, septic fever follows.
Uniformity is necessary but not sufficient for co-extensiveness.
3
Check convertibility (in‘ikās)
Determine if the effect always implies this specific cause. For septic fever, it does not always imply putrefaction—it could also arise from spiritual heat. Thus, the cause is not convertible.
If the effect can occur without the cause, the cause is not co-extensive.
4
Classify the cause
If both uniformity and convertibility hold, the cause is co-extensive (musāwiya). If only uniformity holds, it is specific (akhaṣṣ). Example: putrefaction for septic fever is specific; a common factor shared by all specific causes (if any) could be co-extensive.
The author notes that specific causes may share a general meaning that is co-extensive.
5
Assess suitability for demonstration
Only a co-extensive cause can serve as a middle term in a syllogism proving the existence of the effect and be included in its definition. A specific cause cannot be used for the general effect, only for its subtype.
The text explicitly states: putrefaction is not a cause of absolute fever, nor taken in its explanatory statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العلة الفاعلية فلا يجب من وجودها وجود المعلول وإن وجد معها العلة القابلية أي المادية ما لم تتحقق الشرائط بتمامها؛ فإن العلة لا تكون علة تامة إلا عند تحقق جميع الشرائط. ألا ترى أن القوة المبردة التي في الأفيون إذا وصلت إلى البدن فيما لم ينفعل الأفيون أولاً عن الحرارة الغريزية لم يؤثر فيه بالتبريد، والمعلول إذا وجد بالفعل لا بد من وجود العلل الأربع. وقد قيل في الغائية إنها لا تكون لازمة ربَّما لم يوجد بعد كالاضطجاع مع وجود الفراش لأن العلة الغائية ليست علة من حيث هي موجودة في الأعيان بل من حيث متصورة في الأذهان؛ فإن الفاعل إذا تصورها كان ذلك التصور يحمله على أن ينفعل، فالعلة الغائية من حيث الماهية متقدمة على المعلول، ومن حيث الوجود متقدمة عنه. واعلم أن العلة قد تكون مساوية للمعلول منعكسة إليه؛ وحينئذ يمكن أن تجعل حدا أوسط في القياس المنتج لوجود المعلول، وتؤخذ أيضًا في القول الشارح للمعلول. وقد تكون أخص منه ؛ وذلك إنما يكون إذا كانت علة الشيء فوق واحدة نحو السحاب فإنه يحصل تارة عن تكاثف الهواء بالبرد، وأخرى عن تراكم الأبخرة. وكذلك الحُمى فإنها تحصل تارة عن عفونة الخلط، وأخرى عن حرارة الروح بلا عفونة. وكل واحدة من أمثال هذه العلل تكون مطردة غير منعكسة فإنه يلزم من وجودها وجود المعلول؛ ولا ينعكس. ثمَّ العلل الخاصة المشتركة في المعلول قد تشترك في معنى عام هو علة مساوية للمعلول، وقد لا تشترك في ذلك. والتي لا تشترك في ذلك فإنها لا تصلح لأن تجعل حدا أوسط في القياس المنتج لوجود المعلول، ولا أن تؤخذ أيضًا في القول الشارح له؛ فإن العفونة ليست علة الحمى المطلقة، ولا مأخوذة في القول الشارح للحمى المطلقة بل هي علة لحمى الغُت التي هي نوع من الحُمى يُؤخذ في القول الشارح لهذا النوع. فصل: في تفصيل دخول أصناف العلل في الحدود والبراهين ليتم الوقوف به على مشاركة ما بين البرهان والحد
As for the efficient cause, its existence does not necessitate the existence of the effect, even if the receptive cause, i.e., material, exists with it, unless the conditions are fully realized. For the cause is not a complete cause except when all conditions are realized. Do you not see that the cooling power in opium, when it reaches the body while the opium has not first been acted upon by the innate heat, does not cool it? And when the effect exists actually, the four causes must necessarily exist. And it has been said regarding the final cause that it is not necessary; perhaps it may not exist afterwards, like lying down despite the presence of the bed, because the final cause is not a cause inasmuch as it exists externally, but inasmuch as it is conceived in minds. For when the agent conceives of it, that conception impels him to act. So the final cause in terms of quiddity is prior to the effect, and in terms of existence is prior to it. And know that the cause may be co-extensive with the effect, convertible to it; then it can be made a middle term in the syllogism producing the effect's existence, and it is also taken in the explanatory statement of the effect. And it may be more specific than it; that occurs when the cause of a thing is more than one, like clouds, which occur sometimes due to condensation of air by cold, and other times due to accumulation of vapors. Similarly, fever occurs sometimes due to putrefaction of the humors, and other times due to heat of the spirit without putrefaction. Each one of such causes is uniform (mutṭarida) but not convertible (ghayr mun‘akisa): its existence entails the existence of the effect, but not vice versa. Then the specific causes that share the effect may share in a general meaning that is a co-extensive cause of the effect, or they may not share in that. Those that do not share in that are not suitable to be made a middle term in the syllogism producing the effect's existence, nor to be taken in the explanatory statement of it. For putrefaction is not a cause of absolute fever, nor is it taken in the explanatory statement of absolute fever; rather it is a cause for septic fever, which is a type of fever taken in the explanatory statement of that type. Chapter: On detailing the entry of the types of causes into definitions and proofs to enable one to grasp the commonality between demonstration and definition.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage expands on the conditions and classifications of causes introduced earlier. The author first explains that the efficient cause does not guarantee the effect unless all conditions are met, giving the example of opium's cooling power requiring that the opium not be overwhelmed by innate heat. He then addresses the final cause, noting that it is not always actualized externally (like lying down on a bed but not lying down); it exists as a mental concept that drives the agent, so it is prior in quiddity and existence. Next, the author introduces a crucial distinction: a cause may be co-extensive (musāwi) with its effect—meaning the cause and effect imply each other—such a cause can serve as a middle term in a syllogism proving the effect's existence and in the explanatory statement (definition). Alternatively, a cause may be more specific (akhass) than the effect, as when a single effect has multiple causes, like clouds from condensation or vapor accumulation, or fever from putrefaction or spiritual heat. Each such cause is 'uniform' (muṭṭarida) but not 'convertible' (ghayr mun‘akisa): if the cause exists, the effect exists, but the converse does not hold. The author then notes that if multiple specific causes share a common factor that is co-extensive with the effect, that common factor can serve as a proper middle term; otherwise, like putrefaction for absolute fever, it cannot. The passage concludes with a section heading that introduces the next topic: how types of causes enter into definitions and proofs to clarify the relationship between demonstration and definition.

Key Terms

مساوية
أخص
مطردة غير منعكسة
