Using Dissection of Wholes to Derive Causal Explanations
Author explains how dissection of wholes into parts yields necessary attributes and causal reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use dissection (tashrīḥ) of a whole into its parts to derive necessary attributes and provide causal (limmī) explanations in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Dissecting wholes into parts reveals necessary attributes that provide the 'why' behind phenomena.

Beyond dividing universals into particulars, the logical method of tashrīḥ—dissecting a whole into its constituent parts—unlocks causal knowledge. By analyzing composite entities like animals or plants, we discover necessary properties that answer 'why' questions. This tool is invaluable in Islamic medicine, kalam, and usul al-fiqh for understanding the rational structure of creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the whole and its parts
Take a composite entity mentioned in the passage, e.g., an animal. Dissect it into its first-level parts (organs), second-level (simple organs like bones), third-level (humors), down to the smallest constituents.
This mirrors how anatomists or jurists analyze a compound object.
2
Examine necessary concomitants (lawāzim)
For each part or combination, ask what predicates or accidents necessarily follow. For example, from the part 'horn', we derive that the animal lacks upper front teeth because the matter goes to the horn.
These are not accidental but logically necessary given the composition.
3
Use concomitants to answer 'why' questions
When asked 'Why does this animal have a rumen?', respond with the discovered concomitant: 'because it is horned'. This gives a causal explanation (limmī), not just a fact (annī).
The cause is the presence of the horn, which necessitates the rumen for digestion.
4
Chain multiple causal steps
Some questions form a chain: e.g., flood intensity at new moon → month like winter → diminished moonlight → sun's position. Each step dissects the cause further, leading to deeper understanding.
This hierarchical causation is typical in natural philosophy and kalam's study of created order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في الانتفاع بقسمة الكل إلى الأجزاء وتمام الكلام في توسط العلل المنعكسة وغير المنعكسة وتحقيق الحال فيه ليس يجب أن نقتصر على استنباط الأمور التي توجد في الحدود والمقاييس من القسمة التي للكلي إلى الجزئي بل ومن التشريح الذي للكل إلى الأجزاء مثل تشريح الحيوان والنبات إلى أجزائه الأولى نحو الأعضاء الآلية ثم الثانية كالأعضاء البسيطة ثم الثالثة كالأخلاط وكذلك إلى آخر الأجزاء. وليس ينبغي أن نقتصر على ذلك فقط بل أن نتأمل إذا كثرت الأجزاء والجزئيات أنه ما الذي يلزم كل واحد أو كل عدة من المحمولات والعوارض وأيضًا أي الأجزاء تلزم أي الجزئيات. واعلم أنا لما استنبطنا من القسمة أن الحيوان تحت الحساس وتحت ذي النفس فقد استنبطنا من التشريح أن الحيوان مركب من جوهر متمسك ومن جوهر سيال. وكذلك استنبطنا لوازم الجزئيات من الأجزاء مثل أن كل حيوان ذي قرن فلا أسنان على فكه الأعلى؛ وذلك لأن المادة تذهب في قرنه. وكل حيوان ذي قرن فله كرش لأنه لا تخبد المغض. فيجب أن يكون لغذائه قبل الوصول إلى الجوف الباطن هضم ما. وكل سمكة فلا رئة لها، وأمثال هذه المستنبطات وعللها نافعة في إعطاء اللم وإن لم يكن كل ما ذكرناه وإعطاء علة مثلا إذا قيل لنا لِمَ لهذا الحيوان كرش فنقول لأنه ذو قرن. وإن قيل لم ليس له كرش فنقول لأنه ليس له قرن. ثمَّ من المسائل ما يكون تحت سبب واحد إلا أنها مختلفة في القرب والبعد فليست مسألة واحدة وإن كانت مشتركة في ذلك السبب مثلا إذا قيل لم صار السيل عند المحاق أشد فيقال لأن الشهر عند المحاق أشبه بالشتاء. ثمَّ إذا قيل ولم صار الشهر عند المحاق أشبه بحال الشتاء فيقال لأن القمر ينقص ضوؤه الذي يلينا فيعدم التسخّن الكائن منه. ثمَّ إذا قيل لم يكون هذا أيضًا فيقال لأن الشمس صارت
Chapter: On Benefiting from Division of the Whole into Parts, and Completing the Discussion on the Intermediation of Reciprocal and Non-Reciprocal Causes, and Investigating the Matter Therein. It is not necessary that we confine ourselves to deriving the matters that exist in definitions and syllogisms from the division of the universal into the particular, but also from the dissection of the whole into parts, such as the dissection of animals and plants into their first parts like the instrumental organs, then the second like the simple organs, then the third like the humors, and so on to the last parts. And we should not confine ourselves only to that, but also consider, when parts and particulars are many, what predicates and accidents are necessary for each one or for each set, and also which parts are necessary for which particulars. And know that when we derived from division that the animal is under the sentient and under the animate, we derived from dissection that the animal is composed of a cohesive substance and a fluid substance. Similarly, we derived the necessary concomitants of the particulars from the parts: for example, every horned animal has no teeth on its upper jaw, because the matter goes into its horn. And every horned animal has a rumen, because it does not chew the cud properly; so it is necessary that its food have some digestion before reaching the internal cavity. And every fish has no lung, and similar derivations and their causes are useful in giving the 'why' (limmī), even if not everything we mentioned; and giving a cause: for example, if we are asked: Why does this animal have a rumen? We say: because it is horned. And if it is asked: Why does it not have a rumen? We say: because it is not horned. Then, among the questions are those that fall under a single cause, but they differ in proximity and distance, so they are not a single question even if they share that cause. For example, if it is asked: Why does the flood become stronger at the new moon? It is said: because the month at the new moon is more like winter. Then if it is asked: And why is the month at the new moon more like the winter state? It is said: because the moon's light that faces us decreases, so the heating resulting from it is absent. Then if it is asked: And why is this also? It is said: because the sun becomes...
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion on the benefits of dividing a whole into its parts (tashrīḥ) as a complement to the usual division of the universal into particulars. The author argues that we should not limit ourselves to division into species and genera; we should also dissect wholes into their constituent parts—like animals into organs, simple tissues, and humors—and then examine what predicates and accidents are necessary for each part or each group of parts. This analysis yields necessary concomitants (lawāzim) that are useful for causal explanation ('ilm al-lim). For example, from the dissection of animals we know that every horned animal lacks upper front teeth because the matter goes to the horn, and has a rumen because it cannot properly chew the cud, necessitating pre-digestion. Such derivations allow us to answer 'why' questions: when asked why an animal has a rumen, we respond 'because it is horned'; if asked why it lacks a rumen, we respond 'because it is not horned'. The passage also illustrates a chain of causal explanations: the flood is stronger at the new moon because the month is like winter; the month is like winter because the moon's light diminishes, reducing heating; this in turn is due to the sun's position. Thus, understanding the parts and their necessary attributes leads to deeper causal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

For each part, derive a necessary concomitant: e.g., takbirat al-ihram necessitates standing (qiyam) in the obligatory prayer. Use this to answer a 'why' question: 'Why does a praying person stand?' 'Because takbirat al-ihram is an integral part that requires standing.'

Then answer: 'Why is the world temporally originated?' 'Because it is composed of substances and accidents, both of which are temporally originated.'

Multiple Causes Under One General Cause
Author illustrates causal chains in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace a chain of proximate causes back to a single remote cause in a demonstrative explanation.

Core Idea
Different questions often share one ultimate cause.

In demonstration, we often ask 'why' repeatedly until we reach a final cause. This passage shows how multiple specific questions can all be answered by tracing back to one general cause, using the tide at new moon as an example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The author states that some problems fall under one cause but differ in proximity and distance. This means they are not entirely separate questions at the deepest level.
2
Examine the tide example
Three questions are asked: (1) Why is the tide stronger at new moon? (2) Why does the month resemble winter? (3) Why does the moon's heating decrease? Each answer is a proximate cause.
The chain shows how one question leads to the next.
3
Observe the chain of causation
The proximate causes (month resembling winter, decreased moonlight) are intermediate. The ultimate cause is the conjunction (alignment) of sun and moon, which unifies all specific causes.
This illustrates the hierarchical nature of causes in demonstration.
4
Apply the concept
In any field of Islamic sciences, look for a chain of 'why' questions until you reach a final, general cause. Recognise that multiple questions may have a single answer.
For instance, in kalam, the temporality of the universe and its order both trace back to the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ من المسائل ما يكون تحت سبب واحد إلا أنها مختلفة في القرب والبعد فليست مسألة واحدة وإن كانت مشتركة في ذلك السبب مثلا إذا قيل لم صار السيل عند المحاق أشد فيقال لأن الشهر عند المحاق أشبه بالشتاء. ثمَّ إذا قيل ولم صار الشهر عند المحاق أشبه بحال الشتاء فيقال لأن القمر ينقص ضوؤه الذي يلينا فيعدم التسخّن الكائن منه. ثمَّ إذا قيل لم يكون هذا أيضًا فيقال لأن الشمس صارت محاذية لجانبه الأعلى، فهذه المسائل كلها تحت سبب واحد وهو الاجتماع.
Then among the problems, there are some that fall under one cause, except that they differ in proximity and distance, so they are not a single problem even though they share that cause. For example, if it is said: 'Why does the tide become stronger at the new moon?' it is said: 'Because the month at the new moon resembles winter.' Then if it is said: 'And why does the month at the new moon resemble winter?' it is said: 'Because the moon's light facing us decreases, so the heating from it is absent.' Then if it is said: 'And why does this happen?' it is said: 'Because the sun is aligned with its upper side.' So all these problems are under one cause, which is conjunction (or alignment).
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage discusses a subtle point about causation in demonstration: multiple specific questions (masā'il), each with its own proximate cause, can all ultimately be traced back to a single remote cause. The author uses the example of tidal strength at the new moon. The first question asks why the tide is stronger at the new moon, and the answer refers to the month resembling winter (proximate cause). A second question asks why the month resembles winter, answered by the reduction of moonlight and consequent lack of heating. A third question asks why this occurs, answered by the sun's alignment with the moon's upper side. All these causes are different in 'proximity and distance' from the final effect, yet they all fall under the general cause of 'social' (conjunction or alignment of sun and moon). The point is that a single ultimate cause can explain a chain of intermediate causes, and one should not mistake these as entirely separate problems.

Key Terms
سبب
قرب وبعد
اجتماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The month resembling winter
B) The decrease of moonlight
C) The sun's alignment with the moon's upper side
D) The conjunction (alignment) of sun and moon

Key Takeaway
In demonstration, multiple specific causes can all be reduced to a single general cause.

Cause Equivalent to Effect: The Eclipse Example
Author explains when a cause is co-extensive with its effect using the lunar eclipse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a cause and effect are co-extensive and explain why proving one from the other does not constitute circular reasoning.

Core Idea
When cause and effect are equal, each can prove the other — but only from known to unknown.

This passage introduces the concept of a cause that is equivalent to its effect, using the lunar eclipse as an example. The author demonstrates that from the cause (interposition of the earth) we can prove the effect (eclipse) via a limmi demonstration, and from the effect we can prove the cause via an inni demonstration. He then addresses the objection that this is circular, clarifying that circularity only arises if the same unknown is used as a premise in both directions, which is not the case here.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause and effect
In the eclipse example, the cause is the interposition of the earth between the sun and the moon; the effect is the lunar eclipse. They are co-extensive: each implies the other.
Co-extensive means sabab musāwin lil-musabbab.
2
Understand the two demonstration types
A limmi demonstration goes from cause to effect (why). An inni demonstration goes from effect to cause (that). The author states both are valid here.
These are standard terms in burhān theory.
3
Analyze the objection of circularity
One might argue that proving A from B and B from A is circular (dawr). The author refutes this by noting that in each case, the premise is known and the conclusion was unknown before the demonstration.
Circularity would require that both conclusions be known from the start.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the relationship between the occurrence of a solar eclipse and the need for prayer (salāt al-kusūf). If they are co-extensive, one can prove the need for prayer from the eclipse (inni) and the occurrence of the eclipse from the prescribed prayer (limmi). Check if this is valid.
This example is from fiqh and hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم السبب قد يكون مساويًا للمسبب مثل توسط الأرض بين الشمس والقمر للخسوف فيتبين هذا بذلك وبالعكس؛ فيقال هذا دور لكنه بالحقيقة لا يكون دورًا لأن الخسوف الذي يتبين من التوسط كان مجهولا بخلاف ما يتبين التوسط منه . فالتوسط يعطي البرهان اللم للخسوف، والخسوف يعطي برهان الإنّ للتوسط.
Then the cause may be equal to the effect, like the interposition of the earth between the sun and the moon for the eclipse; so this is made known by that and vice versa. It is said this is a circle, but in reality it is not a circle because the eclipse which is made known from the interposition was unknown, unlike what the interposition is made known from it. So the interposition gives the 'why' demonstration of the eclipse, and the eclipse gives the 'that' demonstration of the interposition.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific case where a cause and its effect are co-extensive (mutually entailing). The example is the lunar eclipse: the cause is the interposition of the earth between the sun and the moon, and the effect is the eclipse. Since the interposition is the cause, it can be used in a demonstration 'limmi' (from cause to effect) to prove the eclipse. Conversely, if we observe the eclipse, we can infer the interposition as a demonstration 'inni' (from effect to cause). The author addresses a potential objection: this looks like circular reasoning (dawr). However, he clarifies it is not circular because in the first demonstration the eclipse is unknown and becomes known from the cause, while in the second the interposition is unknown and becomes known from the eclipse. The two demonstrations are separate; they are not a single circular argument. This illustrates that when cause and effect are equivalent (each implies the other), one can prove either from the other, provided we start from the known to the unknown in each case.

Key Terms
سبب مساوي للمسبب
البرهان اللم
برهان الإنّ
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the eclipse example, why is it not a circular argument to prove the interposition from the eclipse and the eclipse from the interposition?
A) Because the cause and effect are the same thing.
B) Because in each demonstration, the known premise is different and the conclusion was unknown.
C) Because both demonstrations use the same middle term.
D) Because it is a valid logical circle.

Key Takeaway
When cause and effect are co-extensive, demonstrations in both directions are possible and non-circular if each starts from a known premise and proceeds to an unknown conclusion.

?

Proving One Conclusion via Different Causes
Author shows how a single conclusion can be demonstrated using different causal types (formal, final, material, efficient).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct two distinct causal demonstrations (efficient and material) for the same claim using the appropriate middle terms.

Core Idea
One truth, many causal paths.

This passage expands on the eclipse example to show a broader principle: a single fact can be demonstrated using different types of causes as middle terms. The author lists the four Aristotelian causes—efficient, formal, final, material—and illustrates with human mortality: the efficient cause (heat drying moisture) and the material cause (everything generated is corruptible). He then explains how these causes relate and that a perfect demonstration combines them. This lesson will help you see how flexibility in choosing a middle term does not weaken but enriches demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common conclusion
Find the single claim that is proven by multiple causes. Here it is 'man must die' (الإنسان يجب أن يموت).
The conclusion is the same; only the middle term changes.
2
Extract the efficient cause demonstration
Locate the efficient cause: internal heat dries the moisture needed for life. This is a causal chain: heat (efficient cause) → death. The middle term is 'heat that dries moisture' or simply 'efficient cause'.
The efficient cause is the agent of change.
3
Extract the material cause demonstration
Find the material cause: 'everything subject to generation is subject to corruption' (كل موضوعة للكون فهي موضوعة للفساد). Man is generated, so man is corruptible. The middle term is 'subject to generation' (material).
The material cause provides the substrate.
4
Analyze the perfect mediation
Understand the author's claim: when both material and efficient causes are present (potentially or actually), the demonstration is more complete. Matter needs an agent; agent needs matter. Perfect middle term combines both.
The 'perfect mediation' (توسط تام) is the sum of both causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألا ترى؟ أن التوسط علة للخسوف فيؤخذ الخسوف في حده؛ وليس الخسوف علة للتوسط فليس يؤخذ في حده فنقول إنا قد نبرهن على نتيجة واحدة بوسائط من أسباب مختلفة؛ فتارة من الفاعل، وتارة من الصورة، وتارة من الغاية، وتارة من العنصر؛ مثاله أنا نبرهن على أن الإنسان يجب أن يموت ببيان العلة الفاعلة للموت وهي الحرارة المفضية للرطوبة التي تتعلق بها الحياة، وتارة من جهة العلة المادية / فإن كل موضوعة للكون فهي موضوعة للفساد. وكذلك سائر العلل؛ وذلك لأن الشيء إذا كان مادة يلزمها هيئة ما بالضرورة. وكان أيضًا هناك علة فاعلة تلزم عنها تلك الهيئة بالضرورة فيتبين أن يكون توسط المادة صالحًا لإنتاج وجود الهيئة. وكذلك توسط الفاعل. وكذلك توسطهما معًا لكن توسط أيهما كان وحده يتضمن في القوة توسط الآخر لأن المادة لا تخرج إلى الفعل إلا بفاعل؛ والفاعل في ذوات المادة لا يفعل إلا في مادة فيكون التوسط التام مجموعها جميعًا إما بالقوة وإما بالفعل وهذا من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه من قبل؛
Don't you see? That the interposition is a cause of the eclipse, so the eclipse is taken in its definition; but the eclipse is not a cause of the interposition, so it is not taken in its definition. So we say: We can prove one conclusion by means of different causes: sometimes from the efficient, sometimes from the form, sometimes from the end, sometimes from the material. Its example: we prove that man must die by showing the efficient cause of death, which is the heat that dries up the moisture upon which life depends; and sometimes from the material cause: for everything subject to generation is subject to corruption. And likewise the other causes. That is because when a thing is matter, a certain form necessarily follows it; and there is also an efficient cause from which that form necessarily follows. So it becomes clear that the mediation of the matter is suitable for producing the existence of the form, and likewise the mediation of the efficient. And likewise the mediation of both together. But the mediation of either alone potentially includes the mediation of the other, because matter does not emerge into actuality except by an agent, and the agent in material things only acts upon matter. So the perfect mediation is the sum of both, either potentially or actually. And this is among what has been discussed before.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous discussion of the eclipse to introduce a broader point: a single conclusion can be demonstrated using different types of causes. The author lists four Aristotelian causes: efficient (fāʿil), formal (ṣūra), final (ghāya), and material (ʿunṣur). The example is the necessity of death for humans. Efficient cause: internal heat drying up the essential moisture. Material cause: everything generated is subject to decay. The author then explains why multiple causal demonstrations are possible: if a thing is matter, it has a necessary form; and if there is an efficient cause, the form necessarily results. Thus each type of cause can serve as a middle term to prove the same conclusion. Furthermore, mediation by matter alone implicitly includes the efficient cause (since matter needs an agent to become actual), and vice versa. Therefore, a perfect demonstration would involve both, either potentially or actually. This discussion underscores the flexibility of scientific demonstration: the same fact can be known through different causal pathways.

Key Terms
علة فاعلة (ʿilla fāʿila): Efficient cause; that which initiates a change or brings about an effect. [Note: Standard Aristotelian cause; stable in Islamic philosophy.]
علة مادية (ʿilla mādiyya): Material cause; the subject matter or stuff out of which something is made. [Note: Same.]
علة صورية (ʿilla ṣūriyya): Formal cause; the essence or structure that makes a thing what it is. [Note: Same.]
علة غائية (ʿilla ghāʾiyya): Final cause; the purpose or end for which something exists. [Note: Same.]
توسط تام (tawassuṭ tāmm): Perfect mediation (middle term); when both material and efficient causes are combined actually or potentially. [Note: Specific to this author's discussion of causal demonstration.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because each cause is a separate middle term that entails the conclusion.
B) Because all causes are essentially identical.
C) Because the material cause alone is sufficient without the efficient.
D) Because the conclusion is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
A single conclusion can be demonstrated using different types of causes as middle terms, and the perfect demonstration combines them actually or potentially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Use the efficient cause: intoxication leads to harm, and the material cause: it is made from grapes which are permissible but become forbidden due to the process. Ensure each demonstration has a clear middle term.

Rejecting the Mandate for Single Cause and Causality Equivalence
Author critiques two mistaken beliefs: only one cause can be used, and cause must be equivalent to effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the author's critique of two common misconceptions about causal demonstration in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Not all demonstrations require a single cause, nor is convertibility between cause and effect always necessary.

In this passage, the author refutes two widespread assumptions about causal logic: that each conclusion must be proven by a single cause, and that every cause must be equivalent (convertible) with its effect. He shows that these are not absolute requirements, except in a specific scenario. Understanding these limitations prevents over-restrictive use of demonstration in Islamic sciences such as kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first misconception
The text says “it may be thought that one should not use more than one cause for a single desired conclusion” – i.e., a single conclusion can only have one cause. This is a common error in causal reasoning.
2
Analyze the author's rejection of the first misconception
He says it is not absolutely so, but rather in the manner explained previously (referring to earlier discussion). Multiple causes (efficient, formal, material, etc.) can be used for the same conclusion.
This shows that causal pluralism is acceptable in demonstration.
3
Identify the second misconception
The text says “it may also be thought that the cause must be equal to the effect, convertible with it” – meaning cause and effect must be co-extensive and imply each other.
This is the idea that a valid causal argument requires a two-way implication.
4
Analyze the author's restriction on equivalence
He says it is not necessary except in one respect: when the middle term is a cause of the major term. In other demonstrations, equivalence is not obligatory.
This refines the concept of tawassut (mediation) in demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا قد يظن أنه لا يجوز أن توسط في مطلوب واحد إلا سبب واحد؛ وليس كذلك على الإطلاق بل على النحو الذي بينا. وقد يظن أيضًا أن العلة يجب أن تكون مساوية للمعلول منعكسة عليه، وهذا أيضًا غير واجب إلا في وجه واحد؛ وذلك أن يكون الأوسط علة للأكبر
And therefore it may be thought that one should not use more than one cause for a single desired conclusion; but it is not so absolutely, but rather in the manner we have explained. And it may also be thought that the cause must be equal to the effect, convertible with it; this also is not necessary except in one respect, namely that the middle term be a cause of the major term.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This concluding passage addresses two common misconceptions about causal demonstration. The first: that only one cause should be used for each conclusion. The author refutes this absolutely, referring back to the earlier discussion where multiple causes (efficient, formal, etc.) can prove the same conclusion. The second: that the cause must be co-extensive (equivalent and convertible) with its effect. This is only necessary in a specific sense: when the middle term is a cause of the major term, but not in all demonstrations. The text breaks off after stating this last point, but the idea is clear: while equivalence is useful for two-way demonstration, it is not a universal requirement.

Key Terms
توسط
منعكس
الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In all demonstrations.
B) Only when the middle term is a cause of the major term.
C) Only in material causes.
D) Only in efficient causes.

Key Takeaway
A single conclusion can be proven by multiple causes, and cause-effect convertibility is required only when the middle term is a cause of the major term.

Cause-to-Effect and Effect-to-Cause: Burhan Limmi and Burhan Inni
Author explains eclipse example distinguishing limmi and inni.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between burhan limmi (cause-to-effect) and burhan inni (effect-to-cause) using the eclipse example.

Core Idea
A single phenomenon can be demonstrated both from cause to effect and from effect to cause without circularity.

The author uses the eclipse to illustrate how the interposition of the earth between the sun and the moon serves as a cause for the eclipse. He then shows that we can reason from the interposition to the eclipse (limmi) or from the eclipse to the interposition (inni), and this is not viciously circular because one direction was unknown while the other is known.

Guided Path
1
Identify the causal relationship
Find the cause (interposition) and the effect (eclipse) in the passage. Note that the cause is taken in the definition of the effect, but not vice versa.
2
Distinguish between limmi and inni
When the middle term is the cause (interposition) and the major is the effect (eclipse), we have burhan limmi. When the middle term is the effect and the major is the cause, we have burhan inni.
3
Address the charge of circularity
The passage explains that this is not a circular demonstration because in burhan limmi, the effect is unknown and becomes known through the cause, while in burhan inni, the cause is unknown and becomes known through the effect.
4
Apply to Islamic science
In kalam, one often uses burhan inni to prove the existence of a creator from the contingent world, and burhan limmi to deduce attributes from that existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم السبب قد يكون مساويًا للمسبب مثل توسط الأرض بين الشمس والقمر للخسوف فيتبين هذا بذلك وبالعكس؛ فيقال هذا دور لكنه بالحقيقة لا يكون دورًا لأن الخسوف الذي يتبين من التوسط كان مجهولا بخلاف ما يتبين التوسط منه . فالتوسط يعطي البرهان اللم للخسوف، والخسوف يعطي برهان الإنّ للتوسط. ألا ترى؟ أن التوسط علة للخسوف فيؤخذ الخسوف في حده؛ وليس الخسوف علة للتوسط فليس يؤخذ في حده فنقول إنا قد نبرهن على نتيجة واحدة بوسائط من أسباب مختلفة؛ فتارة من الفاعل، وتارة من الصورة، وتارة من الغاية، وتارة من العنصر؛ مثاله أنا نبرهن على أن الإنسان يجب أن يموت ببيان العلة الفاعلة للموت وهي الحرارة المفضية للرطوبة التي تتعلق بها الحياة، وتارة من جهة العلة المادية فإن كل موضوعة للكون فهي موضوعة للفساد. وكذلك سائر العلل؛ وذلك لأن الشيء إذا كان مادة يلزمها هيئة ما بالضرورة. وكان أيضًا هناك علة فاعلة تلزم عنها تلك الهيئة بالضرورة فيتبين أن يكون توسط المادة صالحًا لإنتاج وجود الهيئة. وكذلك توسط الفاعل. وكذلك توسطهما معًا لكن توسط أيهما كان وحده يتضمن في القوة توسط الآخر لأن المادة لا تخرج إلى الفعل إلا بفاعل؛ والفاعل في ذوات المادة لا يفعل إلا في مادة فيكون التوسط التام مجموعها جميعًا إما بالقوة وإما بالفعل وهذا من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه من قبل؛ ولهذا قد يظن أنه لا يجوز أن توسط في مطلوب واحد إلا سبب واحد؛ وليس كذلك على الإطلاق بل على النحو الذي بينا.
Then the cause may be equal to the effect, like the interposition of the earth between the sun and the moon for the eclipse; so this is known by that and vice versa; so it is said this is a circle, but in reality it is not a circle because the eclipse which is known from the interposition was unknown, contrary to what is known from the interposition. So the interposition gives the demonstration of the 'why' (burhan limmi) for the eclipse, and the eclipse gives the demonstration of the 'that' (burhan inni) for the interposition. Do you not see? That the interposition is a cause of the eclipse, so the eclipse is taken in its definition; and the eclipse is not a cause of the interposition, so it is not taken in its definition. So we say: Indeed, we may demonstrate one conclusion through intermediate terms from different causes: sometimes from the efficient, sometimes from the form, sometimes from the final, sometimes from the material. For example, we demonstrate that man must die by showing the efficient cause of death, which is the heat that eliminates the moisture upon which life depends, and sometimes from the material cause, for everything subject to generation is subject to corruption. And similarly for the rest of the causes. That is because a thing, if it is matter, necessarily entails a certain form. And there is also an efficient cause from which that form necessarily follows, so it becomes clear that the interposition of matter is suitable for producing the existence of the form. And likewise the interposition of the agent. And likewise the interposition of both together, but the interposition of either alone includes in potentiality the interposition of the other, because matter does not emerge into actuality except through an agent, and the agent in material things does not act except in matter. So the complete interposition is the combination of all of them, either potentially or actually. And this is among what has been discussed previously. For this reason it may be thought that only one cause can be intermediate for a single sought thing, but it is not so absolutely, but rather in the manner we have explained.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of how one conclusion can be demonstrated through multiple different causal routes, using the example of the eclipse to illustrate the distinction between demonstration of the 'why' (burhan limmi) and demonstration of the 'that' (burhan inni). The author then generalizes to the four causes: efficient, formal, final, and material. The example of man's death shows that the efficient cause (heat destroying moisture) and the material cause (the body being subject to corruption) both lead to the same conclusion. The author emphasizes that the middle term need not be unique; a single effect can have multiple causes. The passage also addresses a possible objection that only one cause can be used, but the author argues that this is not always the case, as explained.

Key Terms
برهان اللم
برهان الإنّ
التوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The eclipse
B) The interposition of the earth
C) The sun
D) The moon

Key Takeaway
Burhan limmi goes from cause to effect; burhan inni goes from effect to cause; they are not circular because one direction reveals the unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The Qur'an is miraculous (effect) because it is from Allah (cause).' Is this burhan limmi or burhan inni? Explain.
?
Identify whether the following argument uses limmi or inni: 'Because the universe is expanding, it must have a beginning.'

Multiple Demonstrations for a Single Conclusion
Author explains multiple causes for one conclusion with man's death example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a single conclusion (e.g., man's mortality) can be demonstrated through different causes (efficient, material, formal, final).

Core Idea
A single effect can be proven by multiple middle terms representing different types of causes.

The author generalizes from the eclipse example to show that one conclusion can be demonstrated through different intermediate terms from the four causes. He uses the example of man's death: it can be proven by the efficient cause (heat destroying moisture) or by the material cause (everything subject to generation is subject to corruption). This shows that the middle term need not be unique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that man must die. Note that this is the same conclusion for both demonstrations.
2
Extract the efficient cause demonstration
The efficient cause is heat that eliminates the moisture of life. This is a cause to effect argument (limmi).
3
Extract the material cause demonstration
The material cause is that everything composed of matter (subject to generation) is necessarily subject to corruption. This is also a limmi argument from the nature of matter.
4
Understand the relationship between causes
The author notes that matter and agent are complementary: matter needs an agent to actualize, and an agent needs matter to act upon. Thus, either cause alone contains the other in potentiality.
5
Apply to Islamic sciences
In fiqh, a ruling (e.g., prohibition of riba) can be derived from multiple 'illal (causes) such as increase or delay, leading to the same conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم السبب قد يكون مساويًا للمسبب مثل توسط الأرض بين الشمس والقمر للخسوف فيتبين هذا بذلك وبالعكس؛ فيقال هذا دور لكنه بالحقيقة لا يكون دورًا لأن الخسوف الذي يتبين من التوسط كان مجهولا بخلاف ما يتبين التوسط منه . فالتوسط يعطي البرهان اللم للخسوف، والخسوف يعطي برهان الإنّ للتوسط. ألا ترى؟ أن التوسط علة للخسوف فيؤخذ الخسوف في حده؛ وليس الخسوف علة للتوسط فليس يؤخذ في حده فنقول إنا قد نبرهن على نتيجة واحدة بوسائط من أسباب مختلفة؛ فتارة من الفاعل، وتارة من الصورة، وتارة من الغاية، وتارة من العنصر؛ مثاله أنا نبرهن على أن الإنسان يجب أن يموت ببيان العلة الفاعلة للموت وهي الحرارة المفضية للرطوبة التي تتعلق بها الحياة، وتارة من جهة العلة المادية فإن كل موضوعة للكون فهي موضوعة للفساد. وكذلك سائر العلل؛ وذلك لأن الشيء إذا كان مادة يلزمها هيئة ما بالضرورة. وكان أيضًا هناك علة فاعلة تلزم عنها تلك الهيئة بالضرورة فيتبين أن يكون توسط المادة صالحًا لإنتاج وجود الهيئة. وكذلك توسط الفاعل. وكذلك توسطهما معًا لكن توسط أيهما كان وحده يتضمن في القوة توسط الآخر لأن المادة لا تخرج إلى الفعل إلا بفاعل؛ والفاعل في ذوات المادة لا يفعل إلا في مادة فيكون التوسط التام مجموعها جميعًا إما بالقوة وإما بالفعل وهذا من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه من قبل؛ ولهذا قد يظن أنه لا يجوز أن توسط في مطلوب واحد إلا سبب واحد؛ وليس كذلك على الإطلاق بل على النحو الذي بينا.
Then the cause may be equal to the effect, like the interposition of the earth between the sun and the moon for the eclipse; so this is known by that and vice versa; so it is said this is a circle, but in reality it is not a circle because the eclipse which is known from the interposition was unknown, contrary to what is known from the interposition. So the interposition gives the demonstration of the 'why' (burhan limmi) for the eclipse, and the eclipse gives the demonstration of the 'that' (burhan inni) for the interposition. Do you not see? That the interposition is a cause of the eclipse, so the eclipse is taken in its definition; and the eclipse is not a cause of the interposition, so it is not taken in its definition. So we say: Indeed, we may demonstrate one conclusion through intermediate terms from different causes: sometimes from the efficient, sometimes from the form, sometimes from the final, sometimes from the material. For example, we demonstrate that man must die by showing the efficient cause of death, which is the heat that eliminates the moisture upon which life depends, and sometimes from the material cause, for everything subject to generation is subject to corruption. And similarly for the rest of the causes. That is because a thing, if it is matter, necessarily entails a certain form. And there is also an efficient cause from which that form necessarily follows, so it becomes clear that the interposition of matter is suitable for producing the existence of the form. And likewise the interposition of the agent. And likewise the interposition of both together, but the interposition of either alone includes in potentiality the interposition of the other, because matter does not emerge into actuality except through an agent, and the agent in material things does not act except in matter. So the complete interposition is the combination of all of them, either potentially or actually. And this is among what has been discussed previously. For this reason it may be thought that only one cause can be intermediate for a single sought thing, but it is not so absolutely, but rather in the manner we have explained.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of how one conclusion can be demonstrated through multiple different causal routes, using the example of the eclipse to illustrate the distinction between demonstration of the 'why' (burhan limmi) and demonstration of the 'that' (burhan inni). The author then generalizes to the four causes: efficient, formal, final, and material. The example of man's death shows that the efficient cause (heat destroying moisture) and the material cause (the body being subject to corruption) both lead to the same conclusion. The author emphasizes that the middle term need not be unique; a single effect can have multiple causes. The passage also addresses a possible objection that only one cause can be used, but the author argues that this is not always the case, as explained.

العلة المادية
العلة الصورية
العلة الغائية

A single conclusion can be demonstrated through multiple middle terms representing different types of causes, such as efficient, material, formal, and final.

When Must a Cause Be Reciprocal with Its Effect?
Author argues against necessary cause-effect reciprocity except in one syllogistic case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific condition under which a cause must be reciprocal with its effect in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Cause-effect reciprocity is only required when the cause is a universal middle term.

Many assume that a cause must be equivalent and reciprocal to its effect. However, the author clarifies that this is only necessary in one specific case: when the middle term of a syllogism is a universal cause of the major term. Otherwise, multiple specific causes can produce the same effect, as illustrated by thunder and fever.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the common misconception
The author begins by stating that some think the cause must be equal and reciprocal to the effect. This sets up the problem.
2
Identify the exceptional case
The exception is when the middle term is a universal cause of the major term. In that situation, wherever the effect exists, that specific cause must be present.
This corresponds to a first-figure categorical syllogism where the middle term is the cause of the major.
3
Examine the non-reciprocal examples
Thunder can be caused by wind in a cloud or by extinguishing fire. Fever can be caused by spirit, putrefaction, or organ use. These show that one effect can have multiple possible causes.
The existence of the effect does not entail any specific cause.
4
Connect to syllogistic structure
In a syllogism with multiple middle terms for the same major, the major's existence does not necessitate any particular middle. The author illustrates with abstract notation (j, h, b, d).
This emphasizes the logical point about causality and demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يظن أيضًا أن العلة يجب أن تكون مساوية للمعلول منعكسة عليه، وهذا أيضًا غير واجب إلا في وجه واحد؛ وذلك أن يكون الأوسط علة للأكبر مطلقا. وتكون طبيعة الأكبر في ماهيتها معلولة لطبيعة معينة فيكون حيث كانت تكون معلولة له. فأما الوجوه الأخرى فلا يجب فيها ذلك فإن الطبيعة الواحدة كالرعد مثلا تكون من أسباب كثيرة أخص وجودًا منها مثل ريح في سحاب وطفء نار فيه والسحاب نفسه طبيعة واحدة، ولها أسباب كثيرة مثل صعود البخار ، ومثل تبرد الهواء بنفسه. وكذلك الحرارة المنتشرة من القلب في الأعضاء التي هي الحمى قد تكون لها أسباب؛ إما استعمال روح أو عفونة خلط أو استعمال عضو. ولا يقال إن سخونة الروح سبب لحمى ما؛ وليس لذلك الحمى سبب آخر فإنها إذا كانت سببًا لحمى ما كانت لحمى مطلقا. وللمطلق أسباب على ما عرف في تحقيق ما أورده المعلم الأول في معنى توسط العلل ومحاذاة؛ فذهب كلامه فيه مع الإيضاح، فلنرجع الآن إلى الوجه الذي يجب أن يفهم عليه كلام المعلم الأول؛ لئلا يعرض من الشبه). فنقول: يجب أن يفهم كأنه يقول إنه وإن كان قد يمكن أن يحكم بالحد الأكبر الواحد لشيئين بتوسط شيئين مثل أن يحكم به على «ج» و «ه» بواسطتين؛ إحديهما ب والأخرى «د» ففي مثل ذلك لا يلزم إذا وضع المعلول الأعم موجودًا أن يوضع من علله التي هي أخص أي علة كانت واتفقت، ولا أن توضع واحدة بعينها وإن كان لا بد من أن يكون قد وجدت علة ما ولكن لا كل علة، وكيف اتفق بل إنما يتعين ما يتعين بسبب. وقد يمكن أن يوجد ما هو بخلاف هذا، وتكون العلة فيه لا توجد للأشياء الكثيرة إلا بتوسط معلول واحد مساو لها. وليكن المطلوب في المسألة كليا وعلته كلية، ويطلب لشيء كلي له العلة أولاً وإن كانت لما تحته ثانيًا مثل أن خمود الرطوبة يوجد لأشجار شتّى من التين والجزوع والكرم ولكن يوجد
It may also be thought that the cause must be equal to the effect and reciprocal with it, but this is not necessary except in one respect: that the middle term is a cause of the major absolutely, and the nature of the major is in its essence caused by a specific nature, so wherever it exists, it is caused by it. As for other respects, this is not necessary. For the same nature, like thunder, for example, may come from many causes more specific than it in existence, such as wind in a cloud and the extinguishing of fire in it, and the cloud itself is one nature, and it has many causes such as the rising of vapor and the cooling of air by itself. Similarly, the heat spreading from the heart to the limbs, which is fever, may have causes: either the use of spirit, or putrefaction of a humor, or the use of a limb. And it is not said that the heat of the spirit is a cause of a certain fever; that fever has no other cause, for if it were a cause of a certain fever, it would be a cause of fever absolutely. And for the absolute, there are causes as is known in the verification of what the First Teacher presented concerning the meaning of the interposition of causes and their alignment. His discussion of it has gone with clarification. So let us now return to the way in which the words of the First Teacher must be understood, lest confusion arise. We say: It must be understood as if he says that although it is possible to judge by one major term for two things by means of two middle terms, such as judging by it for 'j' and 'h' by two middle terms, one being 'b' and the other 'd', then in such a case, if the more general effect is posited as existing, it does not follow that any specific one of its causes (which are more specific) is posited, nor that one specific cause is posited, even though some cause must have existed, but not every cause, and howsoever it happens, but rather is determined by a cause. And it may happen that there is something contrary to this, where the cause does not exist for many things except through an intermediate of a single effect equal to it. Let the sought after in the problem be universal and its cause universal, and it is sought for a universal thing that primarily has a cause, even if secondarily for what is under it, such as the disappearance of moisture exists for various trees—fig, walnut, vine—but exists...
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage addresses the common belief that a cause must be equivalent and reciprocal with its effect. The author clarifies that this is only necessary in one specific case: when the middle term is a universal cause of the major term. Otherwise, multiple causes can produce the same effect, as illustrated by the examples of thunder and fever. Thunder can be caused by wind in a cloud or by extinguishing fire, and the cloud itself has multiple causes (vapor rise, air cooling). Fever can be caused by spirit, putrefaction, or limb use. The author then discusses how to understand Aristotle's (the First Teacher) statements about causality, emphasizing that when there are multiple middle terms for one major, the existence of the general effect does not determine which specific cause was effective. Finally, he mentions a case where the cause only exists through an intermediate equal effect.

A) Always, in all cases.

D) Never, because causes and effects are always independent.

Key Takeaway
Cause-effect reciprocity is not universally required; it holds only when the cause is a universal middle term in a categorical syllogism.

Abstract Notation for Multiple Causes in Syllogisms
Author uses abstract notation to illustrate multiple causes for one effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the abstract notation (j, h, b, d) used by the author to illustrate multiple middle terms for a single effect.

Core Idea
One effect can have multiple causes, represented by different middle terms in a syllogism.

The author uses abstract variables to explain how the same major term (effect) can be concluded through different middle terms. This shows that the effect does not necessitate a specific cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the variables in the passage
The author uses ج and ه as subjects, and ب and د as middle terms. The major term (the effect) is judged for these two subjects via the two middle terms.
This is a schematic representation of two separate syllogisms sharing a major term.
2
Understand the logical implication
If the effect exists, it does not follow that a specific middle term (cause) is present. For example, if the major is 'has fever', the cause could be 'spirit' or 'putrefaction'.
This refutes the idea that a single cause must be reciprocal.
3
Apply the notation to an Islamic example
Take the major term 'is haram'. Subject 1: wine (خمر), Subject 2: nabidh (نبيذ). Middle term 1: intoxication (إسكار), Middle term 2: being an intoxicant (مسكر). The existence of the ruling does not pick out a single cause; both are possible.
This mirrors the author's abstract example.
4
Generalize the principle
When a universal effect has multiple potential causes, the existence of the effect leaves the cause indeterminate. This is important in scientific and legal reasoning.
The author also mentions a contrary case where a cause only exists through an intermediate equal effect.

General Effects with Multiple Specific Causes
Author explains the first scenario where a general effect has multiple specific causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a general effect has multiple specific causes and explain why no specific cause can be inferred from the existence of the effect in a demonstrative syllogism.

Core Idea
A general effect can stem from many specific causes; existence of the effect does not imply any particular one.

In demonstration, the middle term must be coextensive with the major term. But when a single predicate (effect) can be caused by multiple different causes, we cannot infer a specific cause from the mere existence of the effect. This passage explores that scenario using examples from nature and logical notation, helping you understand a critical limit of demonstrative reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general effect and causes in the examples
Find the examples of thunder and fever in the Arabic. Thunder is the general effect; its causes include wind in a cloud or extinguishing a fire. Fever is the general effect; its causes include use of a spirit, putrefaction, or use of a limb.
The causes are more specific in existence than the effect.
2
Understand the logical notation introduced
The author uses letters: the major term (predicate) is predicated of two subjects 'C' and 'E' via two different middle terms 'B' and 'D'. This represents the case where the same general effect (major term) is caused by two different specific causes (middle terms).
The logical notation abstracts the concrete examples.
3
Analyze the inferential limitation
From the existence of the general effect (e.g., thunder), we cannot conclude which specific cause (wind or fire) occurred. The author states that no particular cause is necessitated, only that some cause must have existed.
This is a key point: the middle term in a valid demonstration must be convertible; here the causes are not convertible with the effect.
4
Apply this to Islamic sciences
Reflect on how this principle applies to theological and legal reasoning. For instance, the general ruling of prohibition (tahrim) of wine can have multiple specific causes (intoxication, grape origin, etc.). The existence of the prohibition does not specify which cause alone is operative without further analysis.
Demonstrative arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh must ensure that the middle term is uniquely and necessarily tied to the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلقا. وتكون طبيعة الأكبر في ماهيتها معلولة لطبيعة معينة فيكون حيث كانت تكون معلولة له. فأما الوجوه الأخرى فلا يجب فيها ذلك فإن الطبيعة الواحدة كالرعد مثلا تكون من أسباب كثيرة أخص وجودًا منها مثل ريح في سحاب وطفء نار فيه والسحاب نفسه طبيعة واحدة، ولها أسباب كثيرة مثل صعود البخار ، ومثل تبرد الهواء بنفسه. وكذلك الحرارة المنتشرة من القلب في الأعضاء التي هي الحمى قد تكون لها أسباب؛ إما استعمال روح أو عفونة خلط أو استعمال عضو. ولا يقال إن سخونة الروح سبب لحمى ما؛ وليس لذلك الحمى سبب آخر فإنها إذا كانت سببًا لحمى ما كانت لحمى مطلقا. وللمطلق أسباب على ما عرف في تحقيق ما أورده المعلم الأول في معنى توسط العلل ومحاذاة؛ فذهب كلامه فيه مع الإيضاح، فلنرجع الآن إلى الوجه الذي يجب أن يفهم عليه كلام المعلم الأول؛ لئلا يعرض من الشبه). فنقول: يجب أن يفهم كأنه يقول إنه وإن كان قد يمكن أن يحكم بالحد الأكبر الواحد لشيئين بتوسط شيئين مثل أن يحكم به على «ج» و «ه» بواسطتين؛ إحديهما ب والأخرى «د» ففي مثل ذلك لا يلزم إذا وضع المعلول الأعم موجودًا أن يوضع من علله التي هي أخص أي علة كانت واتفقت، ولا أن توضع واحدة بعينها وإن كان لا بد من أن يكون قد وجدت علة ما ولكن لا كل علة، وكيف اتفق بل إنما يتعين ما يتعين بسبب.
Absolutely. And the nature of the major term in its quiddity is caused by a determinate nature, so wherever it exists, it is caused by it. As for the other modes, that is not necessary. For a single nature, like thunder for example, comes from many causes more specific in existence than it, such as wind in a cloud, and the extinguishing of fire in it. And the cloud itself is a single nature, yet it has many causes such as the ascent of vapor and the cooling of the air itself. Similarly, the heat spreading from the heart to the limbs that is fever may have causes: either the use of a spirit, or putrefaction of a humor, or the use of a limb. And it is not said that the heat of the spirit is a cause of some fever; there is no other cause for that fever. For if it were a cause of some fever, it would be a cause of fever absolutely. And the absolute has causes, according to what is known in the investigation of what the First Teacher presented regarding the meaning of the mediation of causes and alignment. His speech there went with clarification. So let us now return to the mode in which the speech of the First Teacher must be understood, lest ambiguity arise. So we say: It must be understood as if he says that even though it may be possible to predicate a single major term of two things by means of two terms, such as predicating it of 'C' and 'E' by two intermediaries, one of them 'B' and the other 'D', then in such a case, if the more general effect is posited as existing, it does not necessitate that one of its causes which are more specific, whichever cause it may be, is also posited, nor that a particular one is posited, even if it is necessary that some cause existed, but not every cause, and not any cause whatsoever. Rather, only what is determined is determined by a cause.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage explains the first scenario in the relationship between a general effect and its specific causes, as interpreted from the First Teacher (Aristotle). The author begins by stating that the nature of a major term (the predicate in a demonstrative conclusion) is caused by a determinate nature, but this does not hold for all cases. He gives examples: thunder can arise from many specific causes—wind in a cloud, extinguishing a fire, etc. Similarly, fever can have multiple causes like use of a spirit, putrefaction, or use of a limb. The key point is that if a specific cause (e.g., heat of spirit) were a cause of some fever, it would be a cause of fever absolutely, which is not the case. Thus, when a general effect exists, we cannot infer a specific cause. The author then translates this into a logical notation: if the major term is predicated of two different subjects via two different middle terms (B and D), then from the existence of the general effect we cannot deduce that a particular cause (B or D) exists, only that some cause exists. This is essential for understanding demonstration: the middle term must be convertible and appropriate.

Second Scenario: Cause Included in Definition of Effect
Author discusses the case where a single cause produces multiple effects through a common intermediary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a universal cause must be included in the definition of its effect in a demonstrative syllogism and explain why the middle term becomes convertible.

Core Idea
A universal cause that acts through a common nature must be part of the definition of the effect.

This passage presents a key scenario in demonstrative science: a single cause producing multiple effects via a common intermediary. Understanding this relationship allows you to determine when a cause should be formally integrated into the definition of its effect – a critical insight for building sound demonstrations in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage describes a situation where a cause does not act directly on each individual subject but through a common property that collects them. This is opposite to the scenario where cause is specific to each effect.
The author calls this the 'second scenario' (al-wajh al-thani).
2
Extract the example
The example given: drying up of moisture exists for various trees (fig, vine, etc.) but exists primarily for a common property like broad-leavedness. So the cause (drying) applies first to the universal 'every broad-leaved tree', then to the individuals.
The common property serves as the middle term.
3
Determine cause-effect relationship
The cause (drying) is universal and convertible with the effect (scattering) through the intermediary (broad-leavedness). The effect is not just in individual subjects but in the universal nature.
Convertibility here means the cause applies to all instances of the effect and vice versa.
4
Apply the rule
Since the cause is not more specific than the effect (it is equal in scope), it must be included in the definition of the effect. This makes the cause the middle term of a demonstration that is convertible.
If the cause were more specific, it would only explain the effect in a particular subject.
5
Distinguish essential vs accidental cause
The author warns that the dissipation of moisture is not the essential cause of scattering—the essential cause is natural motion. The dissipation is only a remover of the obstacle (muzil al-'a'iq), an accidental cause. This shows why precise causal analysis is needed.
Essential cause (bi al-dhat) vs accidental cause (bi al-'arad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يمكن أن يوجد ما هو بخلاف هذا، وتكون العلة فيه لا توجد للأشياء الكثيرة إلا بتوسط معلول واحد مساو لها. وليكن المطلوب في المسألة كليا وعلته كلية، ويطلب لشيء كلي له العلة أولاً وإن كانت لما تحته ثانيًا مثل أن خمود الرطوبة يوجد لأشجار شتّى من التين والجزوع والكرم ولكن يوجد أولا لشيء عام؛ وهو عريض الورق مثلا؛ فيكون كل عريض الورق أو كل [٣١٨,] منتثر الورق فإن رطوبته تخمد. وإذا خمدت بطلت أروحتها / الماسكة فانتشرت فيكون الانتثار هو الأكبر المعلول، وخمود الرطوبة هو السبب والعلة، وعريض الورق وهو الذي له العلة أولا؛ وليس الانتثار معلول خمود الرطوبة لا في ذاته ولكن بحسب وجوده في موضوع قابل هو معلول له مطلقا. وما كان مثل هذا مما يكون يوجب حكمًا معلومًا في أشياء كثيرة ولكن ليس لها أولا بل بمعنى يجمعها كلها وهي علة له إلا في وجوده في موضوع فقط لوجوده مطلقًا. ففي مثل هذا ما يجب أن تكون العلة داخلة في حد الحكم المساوي لها ؛ وذلك لأن العلل هنا يجب أن لا تكون أخص من المعلول فإن الأخص من المعلول ليس علة لطبيعة الحد الأكبر المعلول على الإطلاق بل علة لوجوده في موضوع موضوع كما مرّ من قبل، وتلك الموضوعات لا محالة مختلفة الأنواع. وقد فرضنا هنا أن العلة ليست لموضوع موضوع بل الأمر جامع ؛ فإذن مثل هذه العلة داخلة في الحد فهي حد مبدأ برهان. والأوسط في مثل هذا الموضوع يكون منعكسا؛ فعلى [ ذلك ] يجب أن يفهم قول المعلم الأوّل؛ ولا يجب أن يصادق في مثل هذا المثال من جهة أن انفشاش الرطوبة ليست علة بالذات بل بالعرض (١). وإنما العلة للانتثار هو النقل الطبيعي، وإنما الانفشاش أو الخمود أيهما كان فهو علة لعدم العلة الواصلة فهو سبب الانفصال بالذات والانتثار بالعرض بمعنى مزيل العائق.
ثم قيل فليت شعري هل يمكن أن لا تكون لشيء واحد بعينه من العوارض المطلوبة بالبرهان في الكل علة واحدة أي في مثل المعنى الجامع مع الموضوعات المختلفة لا في موضوع موضوع. ثم قيل أما الحقيقة الذاتية
And it is possible that something exists opposite to this, where the cause does not exist for many things except through the mediation of a single effect equal to it. Let the demand in the problem be universal and its cause universal, and it is sought for a universal thing for which the cause exists first, even if it exists for what is under it secondarily. For example, the drying up of moisture exists for various trees like fig, Jaz', and vine, but exists first for a general thing—say, broad-leavedness. So every broad-leaved tree, or every [??] spreading-leaved tree, its moisture dries up. And when it dries up, its retaining spirits cease, so it scatters. Thus the scattering is the major effect, and the drying up of moisture is the cause, and broad-leavedness is that which has the cause first. And scattering is not an effect of drying up of moisture in itself, but according to its existence in a subject that is receptive to it absolutely. And what is like this, which necessitates a known judgment in many things but not primarily, but rather by a meaning that collects them all and is a cause for it except in its existence in a subject only due to its absolute existence. In such a case, the cause must be included in the definition of the judgment equal to it. This is because the causes here must not be more specific than the effect, for the more specific than the effect is not a cause for the nature of the major term (the effect) absolutely, but rather a cause for its existence in this or that subject, as mentioned before, and those subjects are necessarily of different types. And we have assumed here that the cause is not for a subject individually, but the matter is comprehensive. Therefore, such a cause is included in the definition; it is the defining principle of demonstration. And the middle term in such a subject is convertible. Thus, the statement of the First Teacher must be understood accordingly. And one must not assent in such an example from the perspective that the dissipation of moisture is not a cause per se but per accidens. Rather, the cause for scattering is natural motion; and the dissipation or drying up, whichever it is, is a cause for the absence of the connecting cause, so it is a cause per se for separation and per accidens for scattering, in the sense of removing the obstacle. Then it was said: Would that I knew whether it is possible that for one and the same accidental attribute sought by demonstration in the universal there is a single cause—i.e., in the sense of a comprehensive meaning across different subjects, not for each subject individually. Then it was said: As for the essential reality...
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents the second scenario, opposite to the first. Here, a single cause produces multiple effects via a common intermediary that is equal to it. The author uses the example of trees: the drying up of moisture (cause) leads to scattering of leaves (effect) in various trees like fig, vine, etc., but this cause applies primarily to a common property like broad-leavedness. Thus, the cause (drying up) is not for each tree individually but for the common nature. In such cases, the cause must be included in the definition of the effect. The author explains that if the cause were more specific than the effect, it would only explain the effect's existence in a particular subject, not its universal nature. Since here the cause is comprehensive, it enters the definition and becomes the middle term of a demonstration. Crucially, the middle term is convertible (i.e., it applies to all instances of the effect). The author then warns against a misinterpretation: the dissipation of moisture is not the essential cause of scattering but only the removal of an obstacle; the essential cause is natural motion. Finally, a question is raised: can a single accidental attribute have a single cause across different subjects? The passage ends abruptly with 'As for the essential reality...' likely continuing the discussion.

The Cause Must Equal the Effect in Demonstration
Author argues that the cause must equal the effect and the middle term must be convertible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that the cause must be coextensive with the effect in a demonstrative syllogism, and explain why the middle term must be convertible.

Core Idea
The cause in demonstration must equal the effect; a middle term can only be convertible if coextensive with the major term.

This passage addresses a crucial condition for valid demonstration: the cause (middle term) must be coextensive with the effect (major term). If the cause is more specific, it cannot serve as a universal principle of demonstration. Using the example of leaf scattering in plants, the author shows that the middle term must be convertible (منعكس) for a sound proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example
Find the example of broad-leaved plants: scattering is the effect (major term), suppression of moisture is the cause (middle term), and broad-leaved is the subject (minor term). Note the relationship.
2
Extract the condition
The author states that the cause must not be more specific than the effect; it must be equal (مساوٍ). A cause that is more specific only accounts for the effect in a particular subject, not universally.
3
Relate to demonstration
Understand that in a demonstrative syllogism, the middle term must be convertible (منعكس). Convertibility requires the middle term to be coextensive with the major term. If the cause is not equal, the middle term cannot be convertible.
4
Apply the principle to an Islamic example
In usul al-fiqh, the cause of the prohibition of wine is intoxication. For a sound qiyas, the middle term 'intoxicant' must be coextensive with the major term 'prohibited'. Check whether this condition holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولا لشيء عام؛ وهو عريض الورق مثلا؛ فيكون كل عريض الورق أو كل منتثر الورق فإن رطوبته تخمد. وإذا خمدت بطلت أروحتها الماسكة فانتشرت فيكون الانتثار هو الأكبر المعلول، وخمود الرطوبة هو السبب والعلة، وعريض الورق وهو الذي له العلة أولا؛ وليس الانتثار معلول خمود الرطوبة لا في ذاته ولكن بحسب وجوده في موضوع قابل هو معلول له مطلقا. وما كان مثل هذا مما يكون يوجب حكمًا معلومًا في أشياء كثيرة ولكن ليس لها أولا بل بمعنى يجمعها كلها وهي علة له إلا في وجوده في موضوع فقط لوجوده مطلقًا. ففي مثل هذا ما يجب أن تكون العلة داخلة في حد الحكم المساوي لها ؛ وذلك لأن العلل هنا يجب أن لا تكون أخص من المعلول فإن الأخص من المعلول ليس علة لطبيعة الحد الأكبر المعلول على الإطلاق بل علة لوجوده في موضوع موضوع كما مرّ من قبل، وتلك الموضوعات لا محالة مختلفة الأنواع. وقد فرضنا هنا أن العلة ليست لموضوع موضوع بل الأمر جامع ؛ فإذن مثل هذه العلة داخلة في الحد فهي حد مبدأ برهان. والأوسط في مثل هذا الموضوع يكون منعكسا؛ فعلى ذلك يجب أن يفهم قول المعلم الأوّل؛ ولا يجب أن يصادق في مثل هذا المثال من جهة أن انفشاش الرطوبة ليست علة بالذات بل بالعرض وإنما العلة للانتثار هو النقل الطبيعي، وإنما الانفشاش أو الخمود أيهما كان فهو علة لعدم العلة الواصلة فهو سبب الانفصال بالذات والانتثار بالعرض بمعنى مزيل العائق. ثم قيل فليت شعري هل يمكن أن لا تكون لشيء واحد بعينه من العوارض المطلوبة بالبرهان في الكل علة واحدة أي في مثل المعنى الجامع مع الموضوعات المختلفة لا في موضوع موضوع. ثم قيل أما الحقيقة الذاتية فلا يمكن لأنها تكون حدا مبدأ برهان كما مر . وأما علة القياس كالعلامة والأعراض القريبة فهو ممكن لا يمكن أن يفهم أنه تعني هذه التي هي علة في جميع الموضوعات ليست علة خاصة بموضوع موضوع بل هي مساوية للمعلول حتى إن المشترك بين العلل هو علة للمشترك بين المعلولات؛ فالعلة واحدة، والمعلول لا يمكن أن يوجد أحدهما بدون صاحبه. والأوسط على طبيعة الحد الأكبر؛ فإن كان الأكبر متواطناً كان ما يوجبه بالذات متواطئًا أيضًا. ولما ظهرت حال نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر فقد ظهرت حال نسبة الأوسط إلى الأصغر، ولنقتصر على هذا القدر فإن من المعلوم أن يكون الأوسط لا يكون منعكسًا إلا وأن يكون مساويًا، وإنه قد يكون مساويًا، وقد لا يكون كما مر من غير مرة.
First, for a universal thing; for example, broad-leaved plants; so every broad-leaved plant or every scattered-leaved plant, its moisture is suppressed. And when it is suppressed, its binding humors fail and it scatters. So scattering is the major effect, and suppression of moisture is the cause and reason, and broad-leaved is that which has the cause primarily. And scattering is not an effect of suppression of moisture in itself, but with respect to its existence in a receptive subject it is an effect of it absolutely. And what is like this, which necessitates a known judgment in many things but not primarily, but rather by a meaning that collects them all and is a cause for it except in its existence in a subject only because of its absolute existence. So in such a case, the cause must be included in the definition of the judgment equal to it. And that is because here the causes must not be more specific than the effect, for the more specific than the effect is not a cause for the nature of the major term effect absolutely but rather a cause for its existence in a particular subject as previously mentioned, and those subjects are inevitably of different kinds. And we have assumed here that the cause is not for a particular subject but the matter is comprehensive. So such a cause is included in the definition, so it is the definition of a principle of demonstration. And the middle term in such a subject is convertible. So on that basis the statement of the first teacher must be understood. And one must not affirm in such an example that the dissipation of moisture is a cause per se but rather per accidens. Rather, the cause of scattering is natural motion, and the dissipation or suppression, whichever it is, is a cause for the absence of the connecting cause, so it is a cause of separation per se and of scattering per accidens, in the sense of removing the obstacle. Then it was said: I wonder, can it be that for one and the same thing among the accidental attributes sought by demonstration in the universal there is a single cause, i.e., in the comprehensive meaning with different subjects, not for a particular subject? Then it was said: As for the essential reality, it is impossible because it would be a definition as a principle of demonstration, as previously. But as for the cause of the syllogism, like the sign and proximate accidents, it is possible. It cannot be understood that this which is a cause in all subjects is not a specific cause for a particular subject but is equal to the effect, such that the commonality between causes is a cause for the commonality between effects. So the cause is one, and the effect cannot exist one without the other. And the middle term is upon the nature of the major term; if the major term is univocal, then what it necessitates per se is also univocal. And when the state of the relation of the middle to the major has become manifest, then the state of the relation of the middle to the minor has become manifest. And we will confine ourselves to this extent, for it is known that the middle term is not convertible unless it is equal, and it may be equal and may not be, as has been mentioned many times.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a dense commentary on the conditions of demonstration (burhan), specifically the relationship between cause and effect and the role of the middle term. The author uses the example of broad-leaved plants where scattering of leaves is the effect and suppression of moisture is the cause. He argues that in a demonstrative syllogism, the cause must be coextensive (مساوي) with the effect; otherwise, the middle term cannot be convertible (منعكس). The cause cannot be more specific (أخص) than the effect, because that would make it a cause only for the existence of the effect in a particular subject, not for its absolute nature. Rather, the cause must be comprehensive (جامع) and included in the definition of the effect as a principle of demonstration. The author then addresses a possible objection: can one and the same accidental attribute have a single cause across different subjects? He distinguishes between the essential reality (الحقيقة الذاتية), which yields a definition, and the cause of the syllogism (علة القياس) like a sign or proximate accident, which can be equal to the effect. In the latter case, the commonality of causes is the cause of the commonality of effects. Finally, he reiterates that the middle term must be convertible only when it is equal to the major term. An Islamic example: In fiqh, the cause of the prohibition of wine is intoxication (سكر). If we say 'Every intoxicant is prohibited' and 'Wine is an intoxicant', the middle term 'intoxicant' must be coextensive with 'prohibited' for a valid demonstration. If intoxication is the cause, it cannot be more specific than the prohibition; rather, every prohibited item must share that cause.

Key Terms
العلة
المعلول
الأوسط
منعكس
الحد الأكبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the author's discussion, why must the cause be equal to the effect in a demonstrative syllogism?
A) Because otherwise the middle term would not be convertible
B) Because the cause must be specific to each subject
C) Because the effect cannot be known without the cause
D) Because the cause must be accidental rather than essential

Key Takeaway
In a valid demonstration, the cause (middle term) must be coextensive with the effect (major term); otherwise, the middle term cannot be convertible and the proof fails.

Check if the middle term 'suppression of moisture' is coextensive with the major term 'scattering'. Discuss whether this syllogism meets the author's condition.
?

The Epistemological Problem of First Principles
Author raises the epistemological problem of the origin of primary axioms used in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dilemma faced by the origin of self-evident axioms and explain why neither innateness nor demonstration provides a satisfactory answer.

Core Idea
Self-evident axioms are either innate (unconscious) or acquired by proof—both options are problematic.

The reliability of demonstration depends on its starting points. If those starting points are themselves uncertain, the whole edifice crumbles. In this passage, the author raises a crucial epistemological question: Are the self-evident principles that ground demonstration present in us innately, or are they learned? This question is not merely academic—it determines whether the knowledge produced by demonstration is truly certain or only probable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two alternatives
The author presents two possibilities for the origin of self-evident propositions (al-wāḍiḥāt / al-awwaliyyāt): either they are present in us from creation (innate), or they come into existence after not having been (acquired).
These correspond to the two major positions in the history of Islamic epistemology: the theory of innate knowledge (fiṭra) and the theory of acquired knowledge through sense or reason.
2
Examine the first alternative: innate
If the principles are innate from creation, we would expect to be conscious of them from the start. But the author notes that in childhood, we do not think about axioms like 'things equal to the same thing are equal' or 'a thing cannot be both A and not-A'. This lack of awareness makes innateness implausible.
The author calls this 'strange' (ʿajīb), implying that an unconscious innate knowledge is a problem.
3
Examine the second alternative: acquired by demonstration
If the principles are acquired after birth, they must be proven by demonstration. But demonstration itself depends on these very principles; proving them would require a prior demonstration, leading to either circularity or an infinite regress.
The passage cuts off before completing this argument, but the intended point is clear: demonstration cannot ground its own starting points.
4
Recognize the dilemma
Thus, the author presents a dilemma: innateness conflicts with our lack of awareness, and acquisition threatens the very possibility of demonstration. This forces us to seek another explanation—such as God-given necessary knowledge (ḍarūrī) or intuition—which the passage does not yet address.
This problem is central to Islamic kalam and falsafa. Al-Ghazālī, for example, appeals to God's creating knowledge directly in the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Section: In the conclusion of the discussion on demonstration. It has previously passed that knowledge of the principles of demonstration must be stronger and more powerful than knowledge of the conclusions of demonstration. And a speaker may say: It is not known that each of them is either knowledge of one power, or one is a cause and the other something else of another power. Nor is it known that the origin of clear things, which are primary axioms, is either that they are present in us as we were created, and we know them from that time, or that they are not present but rather come into existence after not having been. If they are present in us from the beginning of our creation, and we are not aware of them, then that is strange. How so? For it has never occurred to our minds at all in childhood that things equal to one thing are equal to each other, and that one thing cannot be devoid of both contradictories. And if they come into existence after not having been, then their coming into existence is either by way of demonstration; and then
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This section opens a new discussion on the epistemological foundations of demonstration. The author begins by recalling a prior point: knowledge of the principles of demonstration must be more certain than knowledge of the conclusions. He then raises an objection: it is not clear whether the knowledge of principles and conclusions are of the same type or different; likewise, it is unclear whether self-evident propositions (الواضحات), which are primary axioms (الأوليات), are innate in us from creation or acquired after birth. If they are innate, we would expect to be aware of them from the start, but we are not—for example, the axiom 'things equal to the same thing are equal to each other' does not occur to us in childhood. If they are acquired, they would need to be proven by demonstration, which would lead to circularity or infinite regress. The passage ends mid-sentence at the page break, likely continuing to argue that such axioms are known intuitively or by some other means. This problem is central to Islamic epistemology: how do we ground certainty in demonstration without relying on unproven first principles? In kalam, theologians often appeal to God-given innate knowledge (fitra) or necessary knowledge (ẓarūrī).

The origin of self-evident axioms is a foundational epistemological problem: they cannot be proven without circularity, nor can they be innate without awareness.
