The Dilemma of Primary Premises: Innate or Acquired?
Author defends that primary premises are acquired through sensory experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that primary premises are neither innate nor derived from demonstration, and explain his alternative account.

Core Idea
Primary premises are potential in the soul, actualized through the senses without demonstration.

This passage confronts a fundamental epistemological question: How do we obtain the first principles of knowledge? An objector presents a dilemma: either they are present from birth or they are acquired. If acquired, they must come from demonstration, but then we face circularity. The author cuts the knot by proposing that these premises are not actual at birth but potential, and become actual through sensory experience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's dilemma
The objector asks whether primary premises are innate (present from creation) or occur after non-existence. Note the two horns: if innate, we should be aware of them from the start; if acquired, they must be via demonstration or something else.
This mirrors classic epistemological debates.
2
Critique the innatist option
The author rejects innate actuality: we are not aware of primary premises in childhood (e.g., the principle of equality). If they were innate in actuality, we would know them from the beginning.
The passage uses the example that children do not know that things equal to one thing are equal.
3
Critique the demonstration option
If primary premises were acquired via demonstration, that demonstration would itself require premises, leading to either circularity or infinite regress. This is impossible because demonstration must start from something known.
This argument assumes the foundations of demonstration cannot themselves be demonstrated.
4
The author's resolution: potentiality
The author states that primary premises exist in potentiality at creation. They become actual through the help of the senses, which provide the necessary conceptualizations (tasawwurat) without requiring demonstration.
This avoids both innatism and regress.
5
Role of the senses in actualization
The senses (external and internal) supply the images from which the intellect abstracts concepts. Once the terms of a primary premise are conceptualized, the mind immediately assents to it.
For example, once you understand 'whole' and 'part', you see that the whole is greater than the part.

المعقولات بلا تعلم واكتساب، ولبعضها تعلم واكتساب ؛ وقد عرفت طريق التعلم وما يدرك منها بلا تعلم فذلك بمعونة الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة. فإن الحس وليكن حس البصر إذا أدرك إنسانًا أو فرسا تأدَّت تلك الصورة وهي الجسمية عن المختلف فيه وهي الحيوانية والشيئية، وميزت الحيوانية المتفق فيها بين الإنسان والفرس عما اختلفا فيه من الإنسانية والفرسية فيكون هذا اقتناء المعاني الكلية.
Chapter: On the conclusion of the discourse on demonstration. It has passed before that knowledge of the principles of demonstration must be stronger and more powerful than knowledge of the results of demonstration. And an objector may say: It is not known that each one of them either is knowledge with a single strength, or that one is the cause and the other is something else with another strength. Nor is it known that the return of the self-evident premises, which are the primaries, either are present in us as we were created, and we know them from that time, or they are not present but occur after non-existence. If they are present in us from the beginning of our creation, and we are not aware of them, that is strange. And how, when it never occurred to our minds in childhood that things equal to one thing are equal to each other, and that one thing cannot be free from either of two contradictories. And if they occur after non-existence, then their occurrence is either by way of demonstration, in which case either circularity or infinite regress follows, or they are not by way of demonstration but by something else; and that suggests an impossibility according to your statement that there is no way to capture unknown assents without demonstration. As for the way to resolve this difficulty, it is that they are not present in us in the beginning of creation in actuality, but in potentiality, whose nature is to be known from things without learning and with the help of assistants whose assistance is in the manner of assistance in primary instruction. And those assistants are the external and internal senses. And the assents that occur after non-existence: it is not the case that each of them occurs by demonstration, but rather among them are those that, when their simple parts are conceptualized and the relation between them with affirmation or negation is considered, the mind stops from decisive judgment in them. The primaries are not of this kind; rather, when the mind conceptualizes their simple parts, it does not stop in judging the necessary relation between them for anything else. They are not present in actuality only because they lack what must be presented before them of conceptualization. For there is no assent unless it is preceded by conceptualizations, as you know. And the net for capturing these conceptualizations is the external senses in that if the sensed objects are not impressed in them and do not lead from them to the imagination, the mind does not begin to act upon them. The explanation of this is that we have a power that apprehends some intelligibles without learning and acquisition, and some with learning and acquisition. You have already known the method of learning, and that which is apprehended without learning is with the help of the external and internal senses. For the sense, let it be the sense of sight, when it perceives a human or a horse, that image applied from the sense is conveyed to the imagination via the common sense – and they are from the internal senses – then the power that apprehends intelligibles turns to these images stored in the imagination, composes them as agreeing in things that differ in parts, then distinguishes what is agreed upon, which is corporeality, from what is disagreed upon, which is animality and thingness, and distinguishes the animality agreed upon between human and horse from what they differ in, which is humanity and horseness. This results in the acquisition of universal meanings.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a chapter (fasl) concluding the discussion on demonstration (burhan). It addresses a critical objection: how do we acquire the primary premises (al-awwaliyat) that are the foundations of demonstration? The author argues that these premises are not innate (present at birth in actuality) nor are they acquired through demonstration (which would lead to circularity or infinite regress). Instead, they are present in potentiality (bi al-quwwa) and become actual through sensory experience. The external senses (sight, etc.) provide images that are stored in the imagination (khayal). Then the intellect abstracts universal concepts by comparing and distinguishing common and differing attributes. For example, from seeing a human and a horse, the intellect derives the universal 'animal' and 'body'. Thus, primary knowledge is built from sensory data without requiring demonstration, resolving the dilemma. The author also notes that primary premises are such that once their concepts are understood, the mind immediately assents to them (e.g., 'things equal to the same thing are equal to each other') without needing any further proof.

الأوليات
القوة الدراكة للمعقولات

The Sensory Pathway to Universal Concepts
Author explains how the intellect abstracts universals from sensory images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the process by which external sensory data leads to the formation of universal concepts in the intellect.

Core Idea
Universals are abstracted from sensory images by the intellect, progressing from particular to general.

How do we go from seeing a particular horse to having the universal concept 'horse'? The author outlines a multi-stage process: the external senses capture images, which are transferred to the common sense and stored in the imagination. Then the intellect compares these images, identifying commonalities and differences, and thereby abstracts universal meanings.

Guided Path
1
Starting point: external senses
The process begins with the external senses (e.g., sight) perceiving individual objects such as a human or a horse. These senses receive impressions of particular forms.
Only external senses capture raw data from the world.
2
Transmission via common sense
The sensed image passes from the external sense to the internal sense called 'common sense' (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), which integrates data from different senses and then passes it to the imagination (khayal).
Common sense is an internal sense that acts as a bridge.
3
Storage in imagination
The impression is stored in the imagination (al-khayal), which holds sensory images even when the object is no longer present. This allows the intellect to later access these images.
Imagination is a repository of particular forms.
4
Intellectual abstraction
The rational faculty (al-quwwa al-darrāka li-l-ma'qūlāt) turns to the stored images. It compares them, notices common attributes (e.g., corporeality, animality) and distinguishes them from specific differences (e.g., humanity, horseness).
This is the key step of abstraction.
5
Acquisition of universal meanings
Through this process, the intellect acquires universal concepts (ma'ānī kulliyya) such as 'body', 'animal', and 'thing'. These are the building blocks of primary premises.
Universals are not directly sensed but abstracted.

Chapter: On the conclusion of the discourse on demonstration. It has passed before that knowledge of the principles of demonstration must be stronger and more powerful than knowledge of the results of demonstration. And an objector may say: It is not known that each one of them either is knowledge with a single strength, or that one is the cause and the other is something else with another strength. Nor is it known that the return of the self-evident premises, which are the primaries, either are present in us as we were created, and we know them from that time, or they are not present but occur after non-existence. If they are present in us from the beginning of our creation, and we are not aware of them, that is strange. And how, when it never occurred to our minds in childhood that things equal to one thing are equal to each other, and that one thing cannot be free from either of two contradictories. And if they occur after non-existence, then their occurrence is either by way of demonstration, in which case either circularity or infinite regress follows, or they are not by way of demonstration but by something else; and that suggests an impossibility according to your statement that there is no way to capture unknown assents without demonstration. As for the way to resolve this difficulty, it is that they are not present in us in the beginning of creation in actuality, but in potentiality, whose nature is to be known from things without learning and with the help of assistants whose assistance is in the manner of assistance in primary instruction. And those assistants are the external and internal senses. And the assents that occur after non-existence: it is not the case that each of them occurs by demonstration, but rather among them are those that, when their simple parts are conceptualized and the relation between them with affirmation or negation is considered, the mind stops from decisive judgment in them. The primaries are not of this kind; rather, when the mind conceptualizes their simple parts, it does not stop in judging the necessary relation between them for anything else. They are not present in actuality only because they lack what must be presented before them of conceptualization. For there is no assent unless it is preceded by conceptualizations, as you know. And the net for capturing these conceptualizations is the external senses in that if the sensed objects are not impressed in them and do not lead from them to the imagination, the mind does not begin to act upon them. The explanation of this is that we have a power that apprehends some intelligibles without learning and acquisition, and some with learning and acquisition. You have already known the method of learning, and that which is apprehended without learning is with the help of the external and internal senses. For the sense, let it be the sense of sight, when it perceives a human or a horse, that image applied from the sense is conveyed to the imagination via the common sense – and they are from the internal senses – then the power that apprehends intelligibles turns to these images stored in the imagination, composes them as agreeing in things that differ in parts, then distinguishes what is agreed upon, which is corporeality, from what is disagreed upon, which is animality and thingness, and distinguishes the animality agreed upon between human and horse from what they differ in, which is humanity and horseness. This results in the acquisition of universal meanings.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a chapter (fasl) concluding the discussion on demonstration (burhan). It addresses a critical objection: how do we acquire the primary premises (al-awwaliyat) that are the foundations of demonstration? The author argues that these premises are not innate (present at birth in actuality) nor are they acquired through demonstration (which would lead to circularity or infinite regress). Instead, they are present in potentiality (bi al-quwwa) and become actual through sensory experience. The external senses (sight, etc.) provide images that are stored in the imagination (khayal). Then the intellect abstracts universal concepts by comparing and distinguishing common and differing attributes. For example, from seeing a human and a horse, the intellect derives the universal 'animal' and 'body'. Thus, primary knowledge is built from sensory data without requiring demonstration, resolving the dilemma. The author also notes that primary premises are such that once their concepts are understood, the mind immediately assents to them (e.g., 'things equal to the same thing are equal to each other') without needing any further proof.

Key Terms
القوة الدراكة للمعقولات
الحس المشترك
الخيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Memory (al-ḥāfiẓa)
C) Estimative faculty (al-wahm)

Key Takeaway
Universal concepts are abstracted by the intellect from sensory images stored in the imagination.

Direct vs Indirect Benefit of Dialectical Syllogisms
Author explains the indirect benefits of dialectical syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct (naf‘ bi al-dhāt) and indirect (naf‘ bi al-‘araḍ) benefits of dialectical syllogisms and provide examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Dialectical syllogisms benefit indirectly: they don't produce certainty directly but can lead to it.

The author explains that dialectical syllogisms have two types of benefit: direct and indirect. The direct benefit is producing a conclusion (even if probable). The indirect benefit includes helping one reach certainty by weighing common opinions, and also preparing the ground for acquiring demonstrative premises. Understanding this distinction is crucial for appreciating the role of dialectic in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of benefit
In the passage, find where the author discusses 'benefit as a syllogism' vs 'benefit because it produces a predominant conclusion' and later 'benefit in demonstration'.
2
Distinguish direct vs indirect
The direct benefit is intrinsic to the syllogism's form (producing a conclusion). The indirect benefits are accidental, like leading to certainty or helping acquire demonstrative premises.
3
Apply to a scenario
Consider a dialectical syllogism using common opinions: does it directly give certainty? No, but it might indirectly lead to it through repeated weighing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ينتفع إذا لم يجد من اليقينيات فيأخذ من المشهورات ما ينتج أحد طرفي اليقين. وكذلك يأخذ منها ما ينتج الطرف الآخر فلا يزال يترجح بينهما ترجيحا بعد ترجيح حتى ربما يلوج له الحق. ويخرج به إلى اليقين لكن هذا النفع لا يكون بما هو قياس؛ فإن نفع القياس بما هو قياس أنه ينتج. وكذلك النفع الأول فإن نفعه لا يكون لأنه ينتج بل لأنه ينتج نتيجة هو الغلبة. وقد ينتفع به في البرهان أيضًا لما أن اكتساب المقدّمات المشهورة وإعدادها أعم من اكتساب المقدمات البرهانية وإعدادها؛ والمشهور أعم من البرهاني فيتّفق له في اكتساب المشهورات وإعدادها أن ستكتسب البرهانية. فيتبين من جملة هذا أن النمط من القياسات لا ينفع الإنسان استعماله فيما بينه وبين نفسه بالذات بل إنما تكون منفعته على سبيل المخاطبة، ولا أن ينفع المخاطب في أن يكمل ذاته بل فيما يؤدي إلى تكميل ذاته بالقصد الثاني. وبالجملة فالقياس الجدلي نافع في سائر العلوم إما بالذات وإما بالعرض. ومن المعلوم أن استعمال القياس لا يكون نافعا إلا وأن يحصل غرض المستعمل من استعماله؛ وذلك بوجوه في المباحث العلمية. وقد علمت بأن المبحث قبل منع السائل مقدمة من مقدمات القياس غير المبحث بعد المنع لما أن البحث في المقدمة غير البحث في النتيجة؛ وهذا ظاهر.
And it may benefit when one does not find certainties: he takes from common opinions what produces one side of certainty. Likewise he takes from them what produces the other side, and he continues to weigh them, one weighing after another, until perhaps the truth becomes clear to him, and he thereby reaches certainty. But this benefit is not as a syllogism per se; for the benefit of a syllogism as a syllogism is that it produces a conclusion. Likewise the first benefit: its benefit is not because it produces a conclusion, but because it produces a conclusion that is predominant. And it may also benefit in demonstration, because acquiring and preparing common premises is more general than acquiring and preparing demonstrative premises; and the common is more general than the demonstrative. So it may happen, in acquiring and preparing common premises, that one acquires demonstrative premises. From all this it becomes clear that this type of syllogism does not benefit a person in his own solitary use, but rather its benefit is in discourse. Nor does it benefit the addressee in completing his own essence, but in leading to the completion of his essence in a secondary intention. In summary, the dialectical syllogism benefits all sciences, either directly or accidentally. And it is known that using a syllogism is only beneficial if the user's purpose in using it is achieved; and that occurs in various ways in scientific investigations. And you have known that the investigation before the questioner's rejection of a premise of the syllogism is different from the investigation after the rejection, because the investigation of the premise is different from the investigation of the conclusion; and this is clear.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage explains the epistemic utility of dialectical syllogisms (قياس جدلي). The author begins by describing a scenario where one lacks certain premises (اليقينيات) and instead uses common opinions (المشهورات) to approximate certainty by weighing both sides of an issue until truth becomes apparent. He emphasizes that this benefit is not intrinsic to the syllogism as a logical form (since its essence is to produce a conclusion), but rather arises from the fact that the conclusion reached is probable or predominant. Next, the author notes a further benefit: acquiring common premises can indirectly help in gathering demonstrative premises, because the set of common premises is broader and more accessible. Thus, dialectic aids demonstration indirectly. The author then clarifies that dialectical syllogisms are only useful in discourse (مخاطبة) with others, not in solitary reasoning. They help the addressee in a secondary sense, by leading him toward completing his own knowledge. In summary, dialectical syllogisms are beneficial across all sciences, either directly (بالذات) or indirectly (بالعرض). The passage concludes by reiterating that the benefit of a syllogism depends on the user's purpose, and that investigating a premise differs from investigating a conclusion.

Key Terms
المشهورات
اليقينيات
النفع بالذات
النفع بالعرض
القياس الجدلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the 'first benefit' of a dialectical syllogism differ from the benefit of a syllogism as a syllogism?
A) The first benefit is direct, the other is indirect.
B) The first benefit is that it produces a conclusion that is predominant, not just any conclusion.
C) The first benefit is that it leads to certainty.
D) The first benefit is that it helps in demonstration.

Key Takeaway
Dialectical syllogisms have indirect benefits: they can lead to certainty and prepare the ground for demonstration, even though their direct benefit is merely producing probable conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a fiqh example: a jurist uses the common opinion 'every intoxicant is prohibited' as a premise in a dialectical syllogism to argue that a new intoxicating substance is prohibited. Is the benefit of this syllogism direct or indirect? Explain your reasoning.
?
In the passage, the author says the first benefit (producing a predominant conclusion) is not as a syllogism per se. What does he mean? Provide a concise answer.

Using Dialectical Syllogisms to Reach Certainty
Author explains the indirect benefits of dialectical syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the process by which dialectical syllogisms, using common opinions, can lead to certainty according to the author.

Core Idea
Weighing common opinions step by step can eventually make truth apparent.

The author presents a method: when certain premises are lacking, one can use common opinions to produce each side of an issue, then weigh them repeatedly until truth becomes clear. This shows that dialectical syllogisms are not just for debate; they can be a tool for discovery. In this lesson, you will analyze this process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting point
The author says: when one does not find certainties, he takes from common opinions what produces one side of certainty.
2
Understand the weighing process
Then he takes from them what produces the other side, and continues weighing until truth 'becomes clear'.
3
Note the epistemic status
This process does not guarantee certainty like demonstration, but it can lead to it through repeated weighing.
4
Relate to Islamic sciences
Think of a kalam debate where a mutakallim uses common premises to gradually build a case for a theological doctrine.

The Three Arts of Persuasion in Logic
Definition of rhetorical syllogisms with contrast to demonstration and dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between demonstration, dialectic, and rhetoric based on their purpose and audience.

Core Idea
Demonstration certifies specialists; rhetoric persuades commoners; dialectic aims at victory.

Islamic logic classifies syllogisms into three arts: demonstration, dialectic, and rhetoric. Each serves a distinct audience and purpose. In this lesson, you will learn how the author defines rhetorical syllogisms and contrasts them with the other two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of syllogisms
Read the opening lines and note the three categories mentioned: rhetorical (خطابية), demonstrative (برهان), and dialectical (جدل).
2
Extract the purpose of each type
Rhetoric aims at convincing the public (إقناع الجمهور). Demonstration produces certain knowledge for specialists. Dialectic seeks victory and obligating the opponent (الغلبة والإلزام).
3
Determine the intended audience for each
Demonstration is for the specialist (خاصي), rhetoric for the commoner (عامي), dialectic is for disputation where truth is not the primary goal.
4
Analyze why dialectic is not for producing belief
The author states dialectic aims at victory, not belief; otherwise it would not be based on accepted premises (مسلمات) or address opposites.
This distinguishes dialectic from the sophist who also seeks victory but without any pretense of benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : في القياسات الخطابية
أما الخطابية فهي التي تعد نحو إقناع الجمهور فيما يحق عليهم أن يصدقوا به، ولينتفع عن نفع يعود منها على الحكمة أو على الجدل. ولما كان المخاطب إنسانًا، والإنسان إما خاصي وإما عامي. والخاصي لا ينتفع من حيث يحتاج أن يصدق تصديق الخواص إلا بالبرهان، والعامي لا ينتفع من حيث يحتاج أن يصدق تصديق العوام إلا بالخطابية؛ فالصناعة النافعة في أن يكتسب الإنسان تصديقا نافعا إما البرهان وإما الخطابة. وأما الجدل بالانتفاع بحصول الغرض وهو الغلبة والإلزام كما مر ; وليس قصد الغلبة هو بعينه قصد إفادة التصديق فإن السوفسطائي يقصد الغلبة ولا يقصد الإفادة أصلا. وكذلك المفاوضة الامتحانية والمحاورة العنادية. ولو أريد من الجدل الدلالة على الصدق لما كانت الصناعة متجهة إلى المتقابلات ومبنية على المسلمات. وحيث يراد به إقناع التعلم في المبادئ فليس يراد أن يفاد تصديقا جزئيا بل الغرض فيه أن يزيل عن نفس المتعلّم الاستذكار، ويشعره قرب الوضع من الإمكان، وتميل فطنته إلى طرف واحد من طرفي النقيض. وإن كان من العامي تصديقا فليس يكون من الخاصي تصديقا فإن تصديق الخاصي إنما هو بالحق ولا تصديق له بما فيه إمكان عناد. وأما تصديق العامي فليس من شرط أن يمحق الريب معه، ولذلك من بيانه أن يقول لمخاطبه صدقت.

وليس لقائل أن يقول إن التصديق أعم من التصديق الخاصي فيكون المتعلم إذا أقنع في المبادئ كيف كان فقد أفاد التصديق المطلق وإن لم يفد التصديق الخاصي. فإنَّا نقول إن الخاصي لو رفع له بمثل هذه الصفة تصديق من جنس التصديق العامي لكان يحق علينا أن نقول: إن هذه المخاطبة تفيده تصديقا وإن لم يكن تصديقًا خاصيًّا لكن الشاعر بالتصديق الخاصي ليس من شأنه أن ينفع له التصديق إلا على النحو الخاصي أو الشبيه به الذي لا يخطر ما يقابله بالبال . وأما العامي فلا يشعر بذلك بل يأخذ الأمر مصدقا به إذا مالت إليه نفسه، ويجري أن يمنط المقابل عن ذهنه وإن لاح له حواره فيكون ميل نفسه إليه مقارنا للتصديق، وعلة له وإن لم يكن نفس التصديق وإلا لا يكون هناك تصديق، ولا ظن مؤكد بل مثل ظن وميل النفس يوقع التصديق عند العامي؛ إذ نظره في ظاهر الشيء أي شيء كان. وبالجملة فكما أن المخاطبة البرهانية لا يبعد أن يراد بها العلية. وكذلك المخاطبة الخطابية فكذلك المخاطبة الجدلية لا يبعد أن يعدل باستعمالها عن جهتها إلى جهة التصديق. وقد نطق الكتاب الذي لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه فقال: ﴿ ادْعُ إِلَى سَبِيلِ رَبِّكَ بِالْحِكْمَةِ وَالْمَوْعِظَةِ الْحَسَنَةِ ﴾ [النحل : ١٢٥] أي بالبرهان؛ وذلك ممن يحتمله وَالْمَوْعِظَةِ الْحَسَنَةِ ﴾ [النحل: ١٢٥] أي الخطابة؛ وذلك ممن يقتصر عنه، وَحَدِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ﴾ [النحل : ١٢٥) أي بالمشهورات المحمودة، فقدم البرهان والخطابة لما أنهما مصروفتان إلى الفائدة، والمجادلة مصروفة إلى المفاوضة ، والخطابة ملكة وافرة النفع في مصالح المدن وبها تدين العامة. ثم الخطابة قد تشارك الجدل باعتبار وتشاكله / باعتبار. [١] أما المشاركة فمن جهتين: إحديهما في القصد والثانية في الموضوع؛ أما في القصد فلأن
The third type: On rhetorical syllogisms. As for rhetorical syllogisms, they are those prepared for the persuasion of the public regarding what it is right for them to believe, and so that benefit may be obtained from the benefit that accrues from them to wisdom or to dialectic. Since the addressee is a human being, and the human being is either a specialist or a commoner. The specialist does not benefit, insofar as he needs to believe the belief of specialists, except by demonstration, and the commoner does not benefit, insofar as he needs to believe the belief of commoners, except by rhetoric. So the beneficial art by which a person acquires a beneficial belief is either demonstration or rhetoric. As for dialectic, benefit is obtained by achieving the goal, which is victory and obligating (the opponent), as has passed. And the intention of victory is not identical to the intention of producing belief; for the sophist intends victory and does not intend to produce anything at all. Likewise, the examinatory discussion and the contentious debate. And if dialectic were intended for indication of truth, the art would not be directed at opposites and based on accepted premises. And when it is intended for persuasion of the learner in first principles, it is not intended to produce a particular belief; rather, the purpose in it is to remove from the learner's soul doubt, and to make him feel the closeness of the position to possibility, and his sagacity inclines to one side of the two sides of the contradiction. And if the commoner has a belief, the specialist does not have that belief; for the specialist's belief is only by truth, and he has no belief in what contains the possibility of opposition. As for the commoner's belief, it is not a condition that doubt be eliminated with it; therefore it is part of his explanation that he says to his addressee: 'You have spoken truthfully.' And it is not for anyone to say that belief is more general than specialist belief, so the learner, if he is persuaded in first principles in any manner, has indeed produced absolute belief even if he did not produce specialist belief. For we say: If a specialist were presented with such a description of belief of the kind of commoner belief, it would be right for us to say that this address benefits him with belief even if it is not a specialist belief; but the one who perceives specialist belief is not such that belief benefits him except in the specialist manner or what resembles it that does not bring its opposite to mind. As for the commoner, he does not perceive that; rather, he takes the matter as believed when his soul inclines to it, and he proceeds to expel the opposite from his mind even if its argument occurs to him. So the inclination of his soul to it is concomitant with belief and a cause for it, even if it is not belief itself; otherwise, there would be no belief, nor firm opinion, but rather like opinion and inclination of the soul produces belief for the commoner, since his consideration is of the apparent aspect of the thing, whatever it is. In summary, just as the demonstrative address is not far from being intended for causality, and likewise the rhetorical address, so too the dialectical address is not far from being diverted from its direction to the direction of producing belief. And the Book, to which falsehood cannot come from before it or behind it, has spoken, saying: 'Invite to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good admonition' [an-Nahl:125]; that is, with demonstration, for those who can bear it; 'and good admonition' [an-Nahl:125]; that is, rhetoric, for those who fall short of that; 'and argue with them in the way that is best' [an-Nahl:125]; that is, with praiseworthy accepted premises. He gave priority to demonstration and rhetoric because they are directed to benefit, while disputation is directed to discussion. Rhetoric is a faculty abundant in benefit for the interests of cities, and by it the common people become religious. Then, rhetoric may share with dialectic in one respect and resemble it in another respect. As for sharing, it is from two aspects: one of them in intention and the second in subject matter; as for in intention, because
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the third type of syllogism: rhetorical syllogisms (القياسات الخطابية). The author begins by stating the purpose of rhetorical syllogisms: they are designed for persuading the public (الجمهور) in matters they ought to believe, and they derive benefit either from wisdom (الحكمة) i.e., demonstration, or from dialectic (الجدل). The audience is divided into two categories: the specialist (خاصي) and the commoner (عامي). The specialist can only be convinced by demonstration (برهان), which yields certain truth, while the commoner can only be convinced by rhetoric (خطابة). Thus, the beneficial arts for acquiring beneficial belief are demonstration and rhetoric. Dialectic, however, aims not at producing belief but at victory and obligating the opponent, as with the sophist. The author then clarifies that even when dialectic is used for persuasion in first principles (المبادئ), it does not produce a particular belief; rather, it removes doubt and inclines the learner's mind toward one side of a contradiction. For the specialist, belief must be based on truth with no possibility of opposition, whereas for the commoner, belief can occur without eliminating doubt—his mind accepts what his soul inclines to. An objection is raised: if belief is more general than specialist belief, then any persuasive address produces absolute belief. The author responds that for a specialist, only specialist belief (or something resembling it) is beneficial; a commoner's belief is insufficient. Then the author cites the Quranic verse (an-Nahl:125) to support the tripartite division: 'wisdom' (الحكمة) is demonstration for those who can bear it, 'good admonition' (الموعظة الحسنة) is rhetoric for those who fall short, and 'arguing in the best way' (الجدال بالتي هي أحسن) is dialectic with praiseworthy accepted premises (المشهورات المحمودة). Demonstration and rhetoric are given priority as they directly benefit the individual and society. Finally, the author begins a comparison between rhetoric and dialectic, stating they share in two aspects: intention and subject matter. The passage ends mid-sentence with the first aspect, but the point is clear: both arts aim to persuade, but their methods and audiences differ.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
6 min
Specialist vs. Commoner Belief
Refutation of the objection that all persuasion yields the same kind of belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish specialist belief from commoner belief based on the role of certainty and inclination.

Core Idea
Specialist belief demands certainty; commoner belief rests on soul's inclination.

Is belief (تصديق) a single category? The author addresses an objection that all persuasion produces belief. He explains that specialist belief requires truth and excludes doubt, while commoner belief can arise from inclination without full certainty. This distinction is crucial for understanding the proper use of rhetorical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being raised
The objector claims that if belief (تصديق) is a general category, then any persuasive address (even to a learner) produces absolute belief, making all persuasion equally valuable.
2
Find the author's response to the objection
The author responds by differentiating two types of belief: specialist belief (تصديق خاصي) which requires truth and excludes any possibility of opposition, and commoner belief (تصديق عامي) which is based on the inclination of the soul and does not require elimination of doubt.
3
Analyze how each type of belief is produced
Specialist belief is produced only by demonstration. Commoner belief is produced when the soul inclines toward a proposition, expelling the opposite from mind even if contrary arguments occur.
4
Understand why a specialist does not benefit from commoner belief
For a specialist, only belief that is certain and leaves no room for opposition is beneficial; commoner belief, which coexists with doubt, is insufficient.

النوع الثالث : في القياسات الخطابية
أما الخطابية فهي التي تعد نحو إقناع الجمهور فيما يحق عليهم أن يصدقوا به، ولينتفع عن نفع يعود منها على الحكمة أو على الجدل. ولما كان المخاطب إنسانًا، والإنسان إما خاصي وإما عامي. والخاصي لا ينتفع من حيث يحتاج أن يصدق تصديق الخواص إلا بالبرهان، والعامي لا ينتفع من حيث يحتاج أن يصدق تصديق العوام إلا بالخطابية؛ فالصناعة النافعة في أن يكتسب الإنسان تصديقا نافعا إما البرهان وإما الخطابة. وأما الجدل بالانتفاع بحصول الغرض وهو الغلبة والإلزام كما مر ; وليس قصد الغلبة هو بعينه قصد إفادة التصديق فإن السوفسطائي يقصد الغلبة ولا يقصد الإفادة أصلا. وكذلك المفاوضة الامتحانية والمحاورة العنادية. ولو أريد من الجدل الدلالة على الصدق لما كانت الصناعة متجهة إلى المتقابلات ومبنية على المسلمات. وحيث يراد به إقناع التعلم في المبادئ فليس يراد أن يفاد تصديقا جزئيا بل الغرض فيه أن يزيل عن نفس المتعلّم الاستذكار، ويشعره قرب الوضع من الإمكان، وتميل فطنته إلى طرف واحد من طرفي النقيض. وإن كان من العامي تصديقا فليس يكون من الخاصي تصديقا فإن تصديق الخاصي إنما هو بالحق ولا تصديق له بما فيه إمكان عناد. وأما تصديق العامي فليس من شرط أن يمحق الريب معه، ولذلك من بيانه أن يقول لمخاطبه صدقت.

وليس لقائل أن يقول إن التصديق أعم من التصديق الخاصي فيكون المتعلم إذا أقنع في المبادئ كيف كان فقد أفاد التصديق المطلق وإن لم يفد التصديق الخاصي. فإنَّا نقول إن الخاصي لو رفع له بمثل هذه الصفة تصديق من جنس التصديق العامي لكان يحق علينا أن نقول: إن هذه المخاطبة تفيده تصديقا وإن لم يكن تصديقًا خاصيًّا لكن الشاعر بالتصديق الخاصي ليس من شأنه أن ينفع له التصديق إلا على النحو الخاصي أو الشبيه به الذي لا يخطر ما يقابله بالبال . وأما العامي فلا يشعر بذلك بل يأخذ الأمر مصدقا به إذا مالت إليه نفسه، ويجري أن يمنط المقابل عن ذهنه وإن لاح له حواره فيكون ميل نفسه إليه مقارنا للتصديق، وعلة له وإن لم يكن نفس التصديق وإلا لا يكون هناك تصديق، ولا ظن مؤكد بل مثل ظن وميل النفس يوقع التصديق عند العامي؛ إذ نظره في ظاهر الشيء أي شيء كان. وبالجملة فكما أن المخاطبة البرهانية لا يبعد أن يراد بها العلية. وكذلك المخاطبة الخطابية فكذلك المخاطبة الجدلية لا يبعد أن يعدل باستعمالها عن جهتها إلى جهة التصديق. وقد نطق الكتاب الذي لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه فقال: ﴿ ادْعُ إِلَى سَبِيلِ رَبِّكَ بِالْحِكْمَةِ وَالْمَوْعِظَةِ الْحَسَنَةِ ﴾ [النحل : ١٢٥] أي بالبرهان؛ وذلك ممن يحتمله وَالْمَوْعِظَةِ الْحَسَنَةِ ﴾ [النحل: ١٢٥] أي الخطابة؛ وذلك ممن يقتصر عنه، وَحَدِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ﴾ [النحل : ١٢٥) أي بالمشهورات المحمودة، فقدم البرهان والخطابة لما أنهما مصروفتان إلى الفائدة، والمجادلة مصروفة إلى المفاوضة ، والخطابة ملكة وافرة النفع في مصالح المدن وبها تدين العامة. ثم الخطابة قد تشارك الجدل باعتبار وتشاكله / باعتبار. [١] أما المشاركة فمن جهتين: إحديهما في القصد والثانية في الموضوع؛ أما في القصد فلأن
The third type: On rhetorical syllogisms. As for rhetorical syllogisms, they are those prepared for the persuasion of the public regarding what it is right for them to believe, and so that benefit may be obtained from the benefit that accrues from them to wisdom or to dialectic. Since the addressee is a human being, and the human being is either a specialist or a commoner. The specialist does not benefit, insofar as he needs to believe the belief of specialists, except by demonstration, and the commoner does not benefit, insofar as he needs to believe the belief of commoners, except by rhetoric. So the beneficial art by which a person acquires a beneficial belief is either demonstration or rhetoric. As for dialectic, benefit is obtained by achieving the goal, which is victory and obligating (the opponent), as has passed. And the intention of victory is not identical to the intention of producing belief; for the sophist intends victory and does not intend to produce anything at all. Likewise, the examinatory discussion and the contentious debate. And if dialectic were intended for indication of truth, the art would not be directed at opposites and based on accepted premises. And when it is intended for persuasion of the learner in first principles, it is not intended to produce a particular belief; rather, the purpose in it is to remove from the learner's soul doubt, and to make him feel the closeness of the position to possibility, and his sagacity inclines to one side of the two sides of the contradiction. And if the commoner has a belief, the specialist does not have that belief; for the specialist's belief is only by truth, and he has no belief in what contains the possibility of opposition. As for the commoner's belief, it is not a condition that doubt be eliminated with it; therefore it is part of his explanation that he says to his addressee: 'You have spoken truthfully.' And it is not for anyone to say that belief is more general than specialist belief, so the learner, if he is persuaded in first principles in any manner, has indeed produced absolute belief even if he did not produce specialist belief. For we say: If a specialist were presented with such a description of belief of the kind of commoner belief, it would be right for us to say that this address benefits him with belief even if it is not a specialist belief; but the one who perceives specialist belief is not such that belief benefits him except in the specialist manner or what resembles it that does not bring its opposite to mind. As for the commoner, he does not perceive that; rather, he takes the matter as believed when his soul inclines to it, and he proceeds to expel the opposite from his mind even if its argument occurs to him. So the inclination of his soul to it is concomitant with belief and a cause for it, even if it is not belief itself; otherwise, there would be no belief, nor firm opinion, but rather like opinion and inclination of the soul produces belief for the commoner, since his consideration is of the apparent aspect of the thing, whatever it is. In summary, just as the demonstrative address is not far from being intended for causality, and likewise the rhetorical address, so too the dialectical address is not far from being diverted from its direction to the direction of producing belief. And the Book, to which falsehood cannot come from before it or behind it, has spoken, saying: 'Invite to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good admonition' [an-Nahl:125]; that is, with demonstration, for those who can bear it; 'and good admonition' [an-Nahl:125]; that is, rhetoric, for those who fall short of that; 'and argue with them in the way that is best' [an-Nahl:125]; that is, with praiseworthy accepted premises. He gave priority to demonstration and rhetoric because they are directed to benefit, while disputation is directed to discussion. Rhetoric is a faculty abundant in benefit for the interests of cities, and by it the common people become religious. Then, rhetoric may share with dialectic in one respect and resemble it in another respect. As for sharing, it is from two aspects: one of them in intention and the second in subject matter; as for in intention, because

Demonstration -- Burhan

The Quranic Model for Da'wa: Wisdom, Admonition, Argument
Exegesis of Quran 16:125 to support the tripartite logical division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to map the three Quranic modes of da'wa (wisdom, good admonition, best argument) to the three logical arts: demonstration, rhetoric, dialectic.

Core Idea
The Quran prescribes demonstration for the elite, rhetoric for the masses, dialectic for opponents.

The author grounds his logical classification in the Quran itself. Verse 16:125 commands inviting to Allah with wisdom, good admonition, and arguing in the best way. The author interprets these as demonstration, rhetoric, and dialectic respectively, and explains why priority is given to the first two.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Quranic verse in the passage
The author quotes Surah an-Nahl (16:125): 'Invite to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good admonition, and argue with them in the way that is best.'
2
Identify the author's interpretation of each part
The author says: 'Wisdom' (الحكمة) means demonstration (برهان) for those who can bear it; 'good admonition' (الموعظة الحسنة) means rhetoric (خطابة) for those who fall short; 'argue in the best way' means dialectic (جدل) using praiseworthy accepted premises (المشهورات المحمودة).
3
Understand why demonstration and rhetoric are given priority
The author notes that demonstration and rhetoric are directed to benefit (فائدة), while disputation (مجادلة) is for discussion (مفاوضة). Hence the verse prioritizes the first two.
4
Connect this to the three audiences
Demonstration is for the specialist, rhetoric for the commoner, and dialectic for the disputant. This completes the tripartite model.

The third type: On rhetorical syllogisms. As for rhetorical syllogisms, they are those prepared for the persuasion of the public regarding what it is right for them to believe, and so that benefit may be obtained from the benefit that accrues from them to wisdom or to dialectic. Since the addressee is a human being, and the human being is either a specialist or a commoner. The specialist does not benefit, insofar as he needs to believe the belief of specialists, except by demonstration, and the commoner does not benefit, insofar as he needs to believe the belief of commoners, except by rhetoric. So the beneficial art by which a person acquires a beneficial belief is either demonstration or rhetoric. As for dialectic, benefit is obtained by achieving the goal, which is victory and obligating (the opponent), as has passed. And the intention of victory is not identical to the intention of producing belief; for the sophist intends victory and does not intend to produce anything at all. Likewise, the examinatory discussion and the contentious debate. And if dialectic were intended for indication of truth, the art would not be directed at opposites and based on accepted premises. And when it is intended for persuasion of the learner in first principles, it is not intended to produce a particular belief; rather, the purpose in it is to remove from the learner's soul doubt, and to make him feel the closeness of the position to possibility, and his sagacity inclines to one side of the two sides of the contradiction. And if the commoner has a belief, the specialist does not have that belief; for the specialist's belief is only by truth, and he has no belief in what contains the possibility of opposition. As for the commoner's belief, it is not a condition that doubt be eliminated with it; therefore it is part of his explanation that he says to his addressee: 'You have spoken truthfully.' And it is not for anyone to say that belief is more general than specialist belief, so the learner, if he is persuaded in first principles in any manner, has indeed produced absolute belief even if he did not produce specialist belief. For we say: If a specialist were presented with such a description of belief of the kind of commoner belief, it would be right for us to say that this address benefits him with belief even if it is not a specialist belief; but the one who perceives specialist belief is not such that belief benefits him except in the specialist manner or what resembles it that does not bring its opposite to mind. As for the commoner, he does not perceive that; rather, he takes the matter as believed when his soul inclines to it, and he proceeds to expel the opposite from his mind even if its argument occurs to him. So the inclination of his soul to it is concomitant with belief and a cause for it, even if it is not belief itself; otherwise, there would be no belief, nor firm opinion, but rather like opinion and inclination of the soul produces belief for the commoner, since his consideration is of the apparent aspect of the thing, whatever it is. In summary, just as the demonstrative address is not far from being intended for causality, and likewise the rhetorical address, so too the dialectical address is not far from being diverted from its direction to the direction of producing belief. And the Book, to which falsehood cannot come from before it or behind it, has spoken, saying: 'Invite to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good admonition' [an-Nahl:125]; that is, with demonstration, for those who can bear it; 'and good admonition' [an-Nahl:125]; that is, rhetoric, for those who fall short of that; 'and argue with them in the way that is best' [an-Nahl:125]; that is, with praiseworthy accepted premises. He gave priority to demonstration and rhetoric because they are directed to benefit, while disputation is directed to discussion. Rhetoric is a faculty abundant in benefit for the interests of cities, and by it the common people become religious. Then, rhetoric may share with dialectic in one respect and resemble it in another respect. As for sharing, it is from two aspects: one of them in intention and the second in subject matter; as for in intention, because
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the third type of syllogism: rhetorical syllogisms (القياسات الخطابية). The author begins by stating the purpose of rhetorical syllogisms: they are designed for persuading the public (الجمهور) in matters they ought to believe, and they derive benefit either from wisdom (الحكمة) i.e., demonstration, or from dialectic (الجدل). The audience is divided into two categories: the specialist (خاصي) and the commoner (عامي). The specialist can only be convinced by demonstration (برهان), which yields certain truth, while the commoner can only be convinced by rhetoric (خطابة). Thus, the beneficial arts for acquiring beneficial belief are demonstration and rhetoric. Dialectic, however, aims not at producing belief but at victory and obligating the opponent, as with the sophist. The author then clarifies that even when dialectic is used for persuasion in first principles (المبادئ), it does not produce a particular belief; rather, it removes doubt and inclines the learner's mind toward one side of a contradiction. For the specialist, belief must be based on truth with no possibility of opposition, whereas for the commoner, belief can occur without eliminating doubt—his mind accepts what his soul inclines to. An objection is raised: if belief is more general than specialist belief, then any persuasive address produces absolute belief. The author responds that for a specialist, only specialist belief (or something resembling it) is beneficial; a commoner's belief is insufficient. Then the author cites the Quranic verse (an-Nahl:125) to support the tripartite division: 'wisdom' (الحكمة) is demonstration for those who can bear it, 'good admonition' (الموعظة الحسنة) is rhetoric for those who fall short, and 'arguing in the best way' (الجدال بالتي هي أحسن) is dialectic with praiseworthy accepted premises (المشهورات المحمودة). Demonstration and rhetoric are given priority as they directly benefit the individual and society. Finally, the author begins a comparison between rhetoric and dialectic, stating they share in two aspects: intention and subject matter. The passage ends mid-sentence with the first aspect, but the point is clear: both arts aim to persuade, but their methods and audiences differ.

الحكمة
الموعظة الحسنة
الجدال بالتي هي أحسن
المشهورات المحمودة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'good admonition' (الموعظة الحسنة) in the verse correspond to?
A) Demonstration (برهان)
B) Rhetoric (خطابة)
C) Dialectic (جدل)
D) Sophistry (سفسطة)

Key Takeaway
The three Quranic modes of da'wa map exactly to the three logical arts: demonstration, rhetoric, and dialectic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Match each term from the verse (الحكمة, الموعظة الحسنة, الجدال بالتي هي أحسن) to the corresponding logical art (برهان, خطابة, جدل) and explain the connection as per the author.
?
In a modern da'wa context, a university student requires rational proofs, while an uneducated elder needs gentle admonition. Which two of the three methods would you use for each? Justify using the verse and the logical principles.
